1 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Strand


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


464 Pa. 544; 347 A.2d 675; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 1096


October 20, 1975, Argued  
November 26, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the trial court (Pennsylvania) convicting her of murder in the second degree, acquitting her of violations of the Uniform Firearms Act, and sentencing her to two years' imprisonment, claiming that the evidence did not support the verdict, the verdict was inconsistent, and that the trial court erred by failing to set a minimum sentence.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was found guilty of murder in the second degree and not guilty of violations of the Uniform Firearms Act. She was sentenced to two years' imprisonment. On appeal, she contended that the evidence was insufficient to prove her guilt beyond a reasonable doubt. The court affirmed, finding that the evidence was sufficient to sustain the verdict where a witness observed appellant with a gun and saw victim staggering after it was fired. Appellant next argued that the not-guilty verdict on the Uniform Firearms indictment was inconsistent with the guilty verdict on the murder indictment and that such inconsistency was reversible error. The court noted that even if the two verdicts were logically inconsistent, such inconsistency alone could not be grounds for a new trial or for reversal. Finally, appellant asserted that the trial court erred when it sentenced her to two years' incarceration without setting a minimum sentence. While the court agreed, it found that the issue had not been properly preserved for appellate review because she did not raise it at the time of her sentencing.


OUTCOME: The judgment convicting appellant of murder in the second degree, acquitting her of violations of the Uniform Firearms Act, and sentencing her to two years' imprisonment was affirmed, because the verdict was supported by sufficient evidence, the inconsistency of the verdict was not grounds for reversal, and she had not preserved the issue of the failure of the trial court to set a minimum sentence for appellate review.

 

2 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth ex rel. Davis v. Van Emberg


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


464 Pa. 618; 347 A.2d 712; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 1103


September 26, 1975, Argued  
November 26, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, adult bookstore and proprietor, sought review of a decree in equity by the Court of Common Pleas of Blair County (Pennsylvania) that granted the Commonwealth an ex parte injunction against the operation of the adult bookstore and prohibited any business activity at the premises.


OVERVIEW: While criminal proceedings were pending against appellants, adult bookstore and proprietor, the Commonwealth obtained an ex parte injunction against appellants that prohibited the operation of the bookstore and prohibited any business activity on the premises. Appellants received no notice or rule to show cause prior to the grant of the injunction and the Commonwealth provided no affidavits showing the threat of irreparable harm or justification for the failure to notify appellants. The court found the injunction improperly issued and vacated the decree, finding the issuance unjustified under Pa. R. Civ. P. 1531 and overbroad in its scope.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the decree granting an ex parte injunction against appellants, adult bookstore and proprietor. The Commonwealth had obtained an ex parte injunction without notice to appellants and without affidavits showing immediate and irreparable harm or the impossibility of service on appellants. The court held that issuance of the injunction was unjustified under Pa. R. Civ. P. 1531 and overbroad in its scope.

 

3 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Martin


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


465 Pa. 134; 348 A.2d 391; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 1116


October 8, 1974, Argued  
November 26, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction on three counts of first degree murder and death sentence, entered in the trial court (Pennsylvania).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was hired to assassinate a former candidate for president of the United Mine Workers. One morning he shot and killed the man, his wife, and daughter as they slept in their homes in Pennsylvania. Defendant was arrested in Ohio, extradited and tried by a jury. He was convicted of three counts of first degree murder and sentenced to death. Defendant appealed and argued that the trial court erred in rulings it made regarding venue and the jury venire, and in its charge to the jury. The court affirmed the convictions for first degree murder, vacated the imposition of the death sentence, and remanded for imposition of legal sentences. The court held that the trial court did not err in its denial of a change of venue motion because enough time had passed between the murders and the trial for the publicity to ebb and there was no prejudice to defendant. The court also held that the keyman jury selection process was complied with, and was not prejudicial to defendant. Finally, the court held that under Furman v. Georgia, the death penalty could not be imposed on defendant under the Pennsylvania death penalty statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for first degree murder, vacated the sentence of death and remanded for resentencing, holding that the death penalty was improperly imposed.

 

4 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Horton


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


465 Pa. 213; 348 A.2d 728; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 1122


October 21, 1975, Argued  
November 26, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant was convicted of passing a worthless check under 18 P.S. § 4854 (1972-73). Appellant's conviction was affirmed by the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), and appellant sought the instant review. Appellant challenged the sufficiency of the evidence to sustain the verdict of guilt.


OVERVIEW: Appellant went to a check cashing agency and presented for payment a check of the Farragut Hall Corporation, drawn on the First National Bank of Philadelphia, which was made payable to appellant. The check was dishonored and it was determined that the bank had been defunct for 15 years, and that the corporation was non-existent. Appellant was charged with passing a worthless check under 18 P.S. § 4854 (1972-73). After a demurrer to the commonwealth's evidence was denied, the defense rested and the verdict of guilt entered. Appellant was convicted and sought review, challenging the sufficiency of the evidence. The court reversed and discharged appellant, finding that it was the intention of the legislature to restrict the statutory scheme of proof to prosecutions of drawers or makers where there was an obvious reason to conclude that they possessed the requisite knowledge and intent. The court determined that the direct evidence was insufficient to prove two of the three elements of the crime and further, that the prosecution could not properly rely upon the statutory inference to withstand the demurrer.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court affirming appellant's conviction of passing a worthless check, and discharged appellant. The court held that the state had failed to prove appellant acted with the intent to defraud and that she knew at the time that the maker or drawer had insufficient funds in, or credit with the depository for the payment of such a check.

 

5 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Estate of Rose


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


465 Pa. 53; 348 A.2d 113; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 1110


March 10, 1975, Argued  
November 26, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commonwealth of Pennsylvania appealed the decision of the Orphans' Court Division (Pennsylvania) sustaining appellee estate's position that items representing "income in respect of a decedent" be devalued to reflect state and federal income taxes paid by the estate and that certain additional insurance benefits were exempt from inheritance tax.


OVERVIEW: After the executors of appellee estate filed the estate's Pennsylvania inheritance tax return, the appraiser for appellant Commonwealth of Pennsylvania increased the valuation of two items on the return and included a third item previously omitted. The difference in valuation of the two re-appraised items represented federal and state income tax properly paid on the items by the estate since they were "income in respect of a decedent," while the third item represented additional insurance benefits. Appellee's executors thereafter petitioned the court to reduce appellant's appraisement, arguing that appellant would receive a windfall if the two items were not devalued to reflect income taxes paid by the estate. After the court sustained appellee's position, appellant contested the ruling. The court affirmed in part, holding that since it was unclear whether the legislature intended to impose double taxation in this fashion, the court must presume that no double taxation was intended.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the determination in favor of appellee since there was no affirmative indication of legislative intent to impose double taxation, but reversed and remanded the determination regarding the additional insurance proceeds for further analysis.

 

6 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. MacDonald


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


464 Pa. 435; 347 A.2d 290; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 1081


May 5, 1975, Argued  
October 30, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commonwealth of Pennsylvania challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania), which found that the obscenity statute, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5903 (1974), which prevented the exhibition of pornographic motion pictures was unconstitutional under U.S. Const. amend. I, and therefore it had no legal basis to grant appellant's injunction to prevent further showings.


OVERVIEW: Appellees exhibited two pornographic motions pictures to the public and were arrested for obscenity and nuisance under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 5903 (1974), and 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6504 (1974), respectively. The trial court found that § 5903 was unconstitutional under U.S. Const. amend. I, and therefore it had no legal basis to grant appellant Commonwealth of Pennsylvania an injunction against future showing. Appellant challenged the ruling, and the court held that under the U.S. Supreme Court's five part Miller test for obscenity, the statute was unconstitutional because it did not define the conduct that was to be forbidden specifically by state law. The court also determined that because § 5903 was unconstitutional, the trial court did not have any authority to grant appellant an injunction to prevent appellees from further showings of the motion pictures. The court affirmed the lower court, and dismissed appellant's challenge.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, and held that state statute against obscenity was unconstitutional under the U.S. Supreme Court's five-part Miller test, and therefore appellees could not be convicted of obscenity, and appellant could not be granted an injunction to prevent appellees from further showings of pornographic motion pictures.

 

7 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. McQuaid


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


464 Pa. 499; 347 A.2d 465; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 1085


June 27, 1975, Argued  
October 30, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, who was charged with murder, sought review of the judgment of (Pennsylvania) trial court, which ordered appellant committed under 50 P.S. § 4408 (1969) of the Mental Health and Mental Retardation Act until he either became competent to stand trial or ceased to be dangerous to himself or others, even though medical experts had testified that appellant would probably never become competent to stand trial.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was charged with murder after a 1960 stabbing incident. Appellant, who was found incompetent to stand trial, was committed to a state hospital pursuant to 50 P.S. § 4408 (1969) of the Mental Health and Mental Retardation Act. In 1974, three medical experts testified that appellant, because of his paranoid schizophrenia, was potentially dangerous to himself and others and would probably never become competent to stand trial. However, the court below ordered appellant committed until he either became competent to stand trial or ceased to be dangerous to himself or others. On appeal, the court vacated and remanded appellant's order of commitment under § 4408. The court held that a defendant could only be committed under § 4408 for a reasonable period of time necessary to determine whether there is a substantial probability that he would attain that capacity in the foreseeable future. Since appellant's incompetency caused what amounted to an indefinite commitment, the court below, on remand, was directed to either civilly commit appellant under 50 P.S. § 4406 or release him.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court below, which ordered appellant, who was charged with murder, committed in a state hospital until he either became competent to stand trial or ceased to be dangerous to himself or others, even though medical experts had testified that appellant would probably never become competent to stand trial, was vacated and remanded because the commitment amounted to an impermissible indefinite commitment.

 

8 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Office of Disciplinary Counsel v. Campbell


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


463 Pa. 472; 345 A.2d 616; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 1012


January 13, 1975, Argued  
October 3, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Office of Disciplinary Counsel sought to disbar respondent attorney from the practice of law upon the recommendation of the Disciplinary Board based on charges against him stemming from his representation of client.


OVERVIEW: Respondent attorney represented client under federal indictment for possessing a quantity of marijuana. During the course of his representation, respondent attempted to defraud the client out of her money. He also attempted to form a criminal conspiracy to transport and sell drugs. Respondent was charged with mail fraud, but was later acquitted. Following his acquittal, petitioner Office of the Disciplinary Counsel commenced proceedings against him. The disciplinary board, after reviewing the report of the hearing committee, agreed with its decision and petitioned the court to disbar respondent from the practice of law. On review of the disciplinary board's decision, the court held that there was sufficient credible evidence to support the findings of the hearing committee. In addition, the court ruled that the mere acquittal of a crime would not be a bar to a suspension of the attorney's right to practice or his disbarment for unprofessional conduct based upon the same acts or conduct involved. Therefore, petitioner's recommendation that respondent be disbarred was accepted by the court.


OUTCOME: The court adopted petitioner Office of Disciplinary Counsel's recommendation that respondent attorney be disbarred. The mere acquittal of a crime was not a bar to a suspension of the attorney's right to practice or his disbarment for unprofessional conduct based upon the same acts or conduct involved.

 

9 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Cropper


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


463 Pa. 529; 345 A.2d 645; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 1017


April 9, 1975, Argued  
October 3, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a direct appeal of a judgment of sentence entered in a trial court (Pennsylvania) upon his non-jury trial conviction of voluntary manslaughter for the death by stabbing of another.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the testimony established that the stabbing of the victim was in self-defense and that the judgment of sentence should be arrested and should be discharged. The court held that under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 505, the state had to prove beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant was not acting in self-defense. The court held that the evidence showed that the state had met its burden in proving that defendant had not acted in self-defense. The trial testimony showed that defendant and the victim were old friends, who were accustomed to engaging in a rough kind of horseplay that involved verbal and physical abuse, and that the victim was unarmed at the time of the scuffle. There was the victim's conditional threat that if you even speak to me tomorrow I am going to kill you, but that was insufficient to justify defendant's belief of the necessity to kill in order to protect himself. Even if defendant's testimony as to his fear were believed, it was a fear not reasonably justified by the immediate circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment of sentence of defendant for voluntary manslaughter because the evidence was sufficient to show that defendant did not act in self-defense when he killed the victim.

 

10 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Gonzales


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


463 Pa. 597; 345 A.2d 691; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 1023


November 19, 1974, Argued  
October 3, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a direct appeal from his judgment of sentence in the trial court (Pennsylvania) for voluntary manslaughter under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2503. Defendant asserted that the evidence was insufficient to support his claim; that contradictory expert testimony, a medical doctor's testimony regarding ballistics, and inflammatory X-rays were improperly admitted into evidence; and that § 2503 violated due process.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for involuntary manslaughter under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2503 based on sufficient evidence. The court held that no knife was located at the scene, thus precluding defendant's self-defense argument. The court also held that the state's expert witnesses' testimony regarding the distance of the fatal shot was not contradictory and improperly admitted into evidence because after reviewing the experts' testimony no conflict existed. The court determined that an examining doctor, while only qualified as to medical matters, was qualified to testify regarding the ballistics of the distance of the fatal shot, because precedent permitted examining medical doctors to testify regarding the distance, and the jury was to determine its weight. The court further ruled that X-ray photographs depicting the victim's wounds were properly admitted into evidence without cautionary instructions because no timely objection was made at trial and black and white X-rays were not very inflammatory. Finally, the court held that defendant's claim that § 2503 was unconstitutional lacked merit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment of sentence of defendant for voluntary manslaughter because the evidence was sufficient to support the conviction; contradictory expert testimony, a medical doctor's testimony regarding ballistics, and X-rays were properly admitted into evidence; and the involuntary manslaughter statute did not violate due process.

 

11 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth ex rel. Toole v. Yanoshak


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


464 Pa. 239; 346 A.2d 304; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 1055


January 21, 1975, Argued  
October 3, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county controller-elect sought review of an order from a lower court (Pennsylvania), that held appellee county controller was lawfully occupying the office of controller and dismissed appellant's complaint in an action of quo warranto against appellee.


OVERVIEW: Appellant county controller-elect challenged the validity of 16 P.S. § 602(b), which made named officials and their deputies ineligible for the office of controller during the continuance in such office and for a period of two years thereafter, on the basis that it was an unconstitutional infringement of his U.S. Const. amend. I right to hold public office. Appellant had served as chief deputy recorder of deeds for the county, but resigned the post when elected controller. The court affirmed the dismissal of appellant's complaint in his action of quo warranto against appellee county controller. Though holding a political office was a form of protected political expression, the court found that the purpose of § 602(b) was to prevent any of the named officers from being in the position of auditing their own book and that the two year period corresponded with the two year statute of limitations for prosecuting public officials after they left office, under 19 P.S. § 211. As chief deputy, appellant was at times required to act in the name of the recorder of deeds, and the possible abuse of the position was the compelling state interest at which § 602(b) was directed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that dismissed appellant county controller-elect's complaint in his action of quo warranto against appellee county controller because the statutory provision that made appellant ineligible to hold the office of controller for two years after resigning from his position as chief deputy recorder of deeds was based on a compelling state interest to protect itself from official misconduct.

 

12 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Dussia v. Barger


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


466 Pa. 152; 351 A.2d 667; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 1130



October 3, 1975, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied February 26, 1976.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant police officer challenged the order of the commonwealth court (Pennsylvania) which denied appellant's request for permanent injunctive relief from his pending court martial, which was ordered by appellee commissioner pursuant to the Administrative Code, 1929, Pa. Laws 177, art. VII, § 711, as amended 1967, Pa. Laws 671, § 1, 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 251 (1974-75), as a result of allegations of impropriety against appellant.


OVERVIEW: After appellee commissioner initiated court-martial proceedings pursuant to the Administrative Code (Code), 1929 Pa. Laws 177, art. VII, § 711, as amended 1967 Pa. Laws 671, § 1, 71 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 251 (1974-75), against appellant police officer, appellant petitioned the lower court for permanent injunctive relief from the pending proceedings. The lower court denied appellant's request for injunctive relief, and appellant challenged the lower court's order. The court held that while the Code had not violated appellant's constitutional rights to a fair hearing and was not an unconstitutional delegation of power by the legislature, appellant was entitled to permanent injunctive relief because when the Code was implemented by the Pennsylvania State Police Field Regulation 3.03-E, the functions of appellee commissioner were unconstitutionally commingled. The court stated that it was unconstitutional to give appellee commissioner the right to prosecute a court-martial hearing and also allow him to have final judicial authority in determining appellant's guilt or innocence. Accordingly, the court granted appellant a permanent injunction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the lower court and granted appellant's request for a permanent injunction against court-martial proceedings, initiated by appellee commissioner. The court stated that the dual role performed by appellee commissioner, whereby he was authorized to initiate the prosecution against appellant as well as be the final arbiter of such prosecution, was unlawful and violated appellant's rights to due process.

 

13 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


School Dist. v. Kane


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


463 Pa. 554; 345 A.2d 658; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 1020


October 11, 1974, Argued  
October 3, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, taxing authorities, sought review of an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) that sustained the demurrer of appellee revenue officials and dismissed their action in equity to distribute the proceeds of public utility realty tax. Appellants claimed that the forfeiture provision of the Public Utility Realty Tax Act, 72 P.S. § 3271 et seq. (1975) was unconstitutional under Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 4 (1968).


OVERVIEW: Appellants, taxing authorities, challenged the order of the trial court granting appellees revenue officials' demurrer to their action in equity seeking distribution of the proceeds of realty tax. They claimed that the forfeiture clause of the Public Utility Realty Tax Act (PURTA), 72 P.S. § 3271 et seq. (1975) violated Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 4 (1968). The court affirmed. It ruled that because the constitution expressly authorized the enactment of the special PURTA tax, but did not specify the nature of the tax, or the details of its imposition, collection, and distribution, it could be assumed that the legislature had the freedom to make such reasonable provisions. Thus, it was not necessary that each local taxing authority receive a share of the tax proceeds in all circumstances. It was sufficient to provide for the apportionment of shares among local taxing authorities, which had fulfilled all reasonable requirements. The timely filing of the annual report required by PURTA imposed a reasonable condition to the receipt of distributive shares and thus, did not violate the Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 4 mandate that such tax "shall be distributed" annually.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court dismissing appellants taxing authorities' action in equity seeking the distribution of proceeds of a public utility realty tax. The statutory requirement for appellants to file annual reports imposed a reasonable condition to appellants' receipt of a share of the proceeds and was constitutional.

 

14 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Wm. Penn Parking Garage, Inc. v. Pittsburgh


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


464 Pa. 168; 346 A.2d 269; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 1047


March 10, 1975, Argued  
October 3, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city of Pittsburgh appealed an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) that vacated and remanded an order of the trial court dismissing, without leave to amend, a challenge by plaintiffs, parking operators and individuals, to a tax on patrons of non-residential parking places.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, parking operators and individuals, challenged a tax on patrons of non-residential parking places. The trial court sustained the objections asserted by defendant city of Pittsburgh and dismissed the petition without leave to amend. Plaintiffs appealed. The appellate court reversed and remanded for amendment of the pleadings and further proceedings. Defendant appealed, and the court allowed the appeal because of the importance of the issues involved to the administration of the Local Tax Enabling Act § 6; Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, § 6906 (1972) (the Act). The court affirmed the order of the appellate court because the Act did not preclude amendment of a petition after expiration of the period allowed for commencing an appeal due to the practice of allowing free amendment in order to promote resolution of cases on the merits and to promote prompt determination of the validity of a taxing ordinance. The court found plaintiffs to have standing to challenge the tax.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court order that vacated a trial court order for defendant city dismissing a challenge by plaintiffs, parking operators and individuals, to a tax on patrons of non-residential parking places because the trial court erred in refusing to allow amendment of the pleadings.

 

15 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Moyer v. Phillips


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


462 Pa. 395; 341 A.2d 441; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 895; 77 A.L.R.3d 1339


November 21, 1974, Submitted  
July 7, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted plaintiff tractor-trailer driver allocatur to consider an order of a trial court (Pennsylvania) that dismissed plaintiff's cause of action in libel against defendant doctor pursuant to the survival statute, 20 P.S. § 3371. Plaintiff asserted that the survival statute arbitrarily denied plaintiff equal protection and denied him access to the courts for an injury suffered by defendant's libelous actions.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff tractor-trailer driver was physically examined by defendant doctor, and based on that examination the doctor wrote to plaintiff's employer and told them that plaintiff was a chronic alcoholic and that plaintiff ought not to be allowed to drive a truck. Plaintiff filed a suit against defendant for libel and the trial court dismissed the cause of action under the survival statute, 20 P.S. § 3371, because defendant had died. Section 3371 allowed all causes of actions to survive after the death of a defendant except actions for slander or libel. Plaintiff argued to the court that this exception for libel was arbitrary and a denial of equal protection under both the state and federal constitutions. The court held that the distinction set up by § 3371 was arbitrary and that no policy or legislative purpose was furthered by the exception for defamation. The distinction did not promote the purpose of a libel action that was to restore plaintiff's good name by providing him with money damages. Nor did it serve any function of the survival statute whose antiquated purpose was to prevent vengeance on a dead person.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the trial court's order that dismissed plaintiff tractor-trailer driver's cause of action in libel against defendant doctor pursuant to the survival statute and remanded the cause for further proceedings consistent with its opinion because the survival statute was unconstitutional.

 

16 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Tower


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


463 Pa. 93; 343 A.2d 671; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 926


November 29, 1973, Argued  
July 7, 1975, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied September 16, 1975.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant adopted children of beneficiary challenged a decision of the Orphan's Court (Pennsylvania), which denied their request to participate in income from a trust established in testator's will.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were adopted children of a beneficiary of a testamentary trust. The will established the trust to be paid to beneficiaries that were the linear descendants of testator. After the beneficiary died, an action was brought to determine whether appellants would share in the income, and a final judgment was entered that limited beneficiaries to linear descendants. After Pennsylvania adopted a new presumption that unless otherwise specifically mentioned adopted children would be treated the same as natural children for purposes of benefiting from wills, appellants brought another action seeking to participate. The trial court denied their request to participate. The court affirmed the decision and held that res judicata applied to the later suit where four identities were established. The four identities were (1) identity of the thing or subject matter sued for; (2) identity of the cause of action; (3) identity of parties to the action: and (4) identity of the quality or capacity of the parties suing or being sued. The court held that the doctrine of "separate funds" did not apply to property rights such as the rights of the beneficiaries to income.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision that denied participation by appellant adopted children of beneficiary in income from a trust established in the will of testator because the issue had been resolved in a prior action that involved the same parties in the same capacity and was res judicata to the action.

 

17 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Gwinn v. Kane


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


465 Pa. 269; 348 A.2d 900; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 1128



July 7, 1975, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Decision July 7, 1975. Opinion November 26, 1975.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) that granted appellee Pennsylvania Attorney General and a deputy attorney general's cross-motions for summary judgment on the basis that there was no disputed issue of material fact and that the appointment of a special attorney superseding the District Attorney of Philadelphia in certain matters was valid.


OVERVIEW: Appellee Pennsylvania Attorney General appointed a deputy attorney general and directed him to organize the "office of the Special Prosecutor" to continue an investigation of official corruption in Philadelphia begun by the Pennsylvania Crime Commission after the district attorney repeatedly and publicly refused to furnish the necessary staff to assist with the investigation. Appellant, who was prosecuted under the activities of the special attorney, challenged summary judgment in favor of the validity of the appointment, which superseded the District Attorney of Philadelphia. The court affirmed the order on the basis that special attorney's powers were derived from appellee's action pursuant to the Administrative Code of 1929 § 907, 71 P.S. § 297 (1962). Pursuant to § 907, the president judge of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia informed appellee that he believed the investigation that had been undertaken by a grand jury was a proper case "for the Commonwealth's intervention," and requested that he appoint a special attorney. The court found ample basis for the appointment as the investigation had revealed persuasive and systematic corruption among public officials.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment in favor of the validity of the appointment of a special attorney because, upon the request of the president judge, appellee Pennsylvania Attorney General was statutorily authorized to appoint a special attorney as he deemed necessary, to investigate the charges of official corruption in Philadelphia, and there was ample basis to support the appointment.

 

18 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Singer v. Sheppard


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


464 Pa. 387; 346 A.2d 897; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 1078



June 26, 1975, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied August 1, 1975.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, individually and on behalf of all residents, taxpayers, pedestrians, motor vehicle owners and occupants of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, sought a declaratory judgment from the Supreme Court of Pennsylvania, requesting a declaration of unconstitutionality of the Pennsylvania No-fault Motor Vehicle Insurance Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 40, §1009.101 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, as representative of all others similarly situated, sought a declaratory judgment requesting a declaration of unconstitutionality of the Pennsylvania No-fault Motor Vehicle Insurance Act (No-fault Act), Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 40, §1009.101 et seq. The court limited plaintiff's amended petition to the issue of constitutionality of the No-fault Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 40, §1009.301(a). The constitutionality of the No-fault Act was sustained because plaintiff failed to meet his burden of proving that statute was clearly, palpably, and plainly a violation of the Constitution. Whether a litigant retained his common-law remedy under the statute, rested on whether he was included in the list of exceptions in §301(a). A limitation of recovery to a sum less than actual damage would be in conflict with the right to remedy by due course of law, but if the right to the remedy was eliminated by the legislature, there would be no violation. Nothing in Pa. Const. art. I, §11 prevented the extinguishing of a cause of action. The legislative classification was sustained because it bore a rational relationship to a legitimate governmental interest.


OUTCOME: In limiting plaintiff's petition to the statute, the court rejected plaintiff's positions and concluded that the statute was compatible with both the Pennsylvania and United States Constitutions.

 

19 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Wiegand v. Wiegand


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


461 Pa. 482; 337 A.2d 256; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 800


May 23, 1974, Argued  
May 13, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former wife sought review of an order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which declared two sections of the Divorce Law, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 23, § 11 and Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 23, § 46, unconstitutional as contrary to the Equal Rights Amendment, Pa. Const. art. I, § 28. The wife had filed an action for divorce against appellee former husband.


OVERVIEW: The wife initiated a cause of action for divorce from bed and board against her husband. Pursuant to Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 23, § 46, the husband was ordered to pay alimony and preliminary counsel fees. He was subsequently ordered to pay additional preliminary counsel fees and expenses. The husband asserted that the trial court had erred by refusing to permit him to cross-examine his wife during the hearings about the disposition of her own estate and contended that the award of $ 5,000 attorney's fees was an abuse of the trial court's discretion. The superior court concluded that the statutory provisions under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 23, §§ 11, 46 impermissibly discriminated on the basis of sex and were void as repugnant to the Pennsylvania Constitution. The court reversed and remanded the cause to the superior court because sua sponte consideration of issues exceeded its proper appellate function of deciding controversies presented to it. The court held that sua sponte consideration of the issues deprived counsel of the opportunity to brief and argue the issues, and the attorney general was denied the opportunity of appearing and responding to the constitutional challenge.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the superior court because the constitutionality of sections of the divorce law was never question by the parties. The case was remanded to the superior court for consideration of the issues properly presented by the parties for review.

 

20 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Triplett


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


462 Pa. 244; 341 A.2d 62; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 876


January 13, 1975, Argued  
May 13, 1975, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied July 7, 1975.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of sentence entered by a trial court (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of burglary, larceny, and receiving stolen goods.


OVERVIEW: After responding to a report of a warehouse burglary and finding no suspects, defendant, who was a police officer, was observed assisting another officer in stealing televisions from the warehouse by later arriving police officers. Defendant was taken to the police station and questioned, where he was obligated to respond or risk losing his job pursuant to the city home rule charter. After being charged with burglary, larceny, and receiving stolen goods, defendant moved to suppress his pre-trial admissions. The trial court granted the motion but subsequently allowed the prosecution to use those same statements to impeach defendant during trial. Defendant was convicted. On appeal, the court held, in reversing defendant's conviction, that once the statements had been declared inadmissible, it was error to allow them to be later admitted for the purposes of impeachment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment of sentence and remanded the case for proceedings consistent with the court's opinion.

 

21 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


American Tel. & Tel. Co. v. Board of Property Assessment, Appeals & Review


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


461 Pa. 716; 337 A.2d 844; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 827


October 9, 1974, Argued  
May 13, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant public utility challenged a judgment of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), holding that appellant's payment of the tax imposed by the Public Utility Realty Tax Act, 72 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3271 et seq., was not in lieu of local real estate taxes pursuant to Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 4.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to the Public Utility Realty Tax Act (PURTA), 72 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3271 et seq., appellant public utility paid a state-wide property tax upon the depreciated value of its realty. The lower court held that because the PURTA tax was supposedly in lieu of local property taxes, it was unconstitutional as inconsistent with Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 4, which provided that special taxes replaced gross receipt taxes. On appeal, the court held that the legislature's expression in 72 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3278 that the tax not be interpreted to "replace" the gross receipts tax and its subsequent reenactment of the gross receipts tax, 72 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 8101, did not render PURTA in any sense irreconcilable with Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 4 . Section 3278 was included in PURTA to assure that it would not be interpreted as impliedly repealing the gross receipts tax and thereby adversely affecting this source of general commonwealth revenues. Thus, the PURTA tax was a special tax imposed specially for purposes of art. VIII, § 4. Accordingly, payment of that tax by a public utility and distribution by the commonwealth to local taxing authorities was in lieu of local real estate taxation.


OUTCOME: The judgment holding appellant public utility subject to local taxation of its operating realty in addition to the tax imposed by the Public Utility Realty Tax Act (PURTA) was reversed because the PURTA tax was imposed in lieu of local real estate taxes.

 

22 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Staub


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


461 Pa. 486; 337 A.2d 258; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 801


May 23, 1974, Argued  
April 2, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant father sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania), which penalized defendant under the fornication and bastardy statute, 18 P.S. § 4506.


OVERVIEW: Defendant father was charged with a violation of the fornication and bastardy statute, 18 P.S. § 4506, that punished illicit sexual connection that resulted in a child's birth. Defendant pleaded guilty, was sentenced, and sought review, contending that the statute's more severe sanction permitted for a male offender than for the female offender for the same conduct violated the Equal Protection Clause of the U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court found that the sanction imposed against the female was limited to the payment of a $ 100 fine, but the male offender was liable for the fine, lying-in expenses, child's maintenance during its minority, and funeral expenses if the child dies at birth or during the continuance of an order. The court vacated and remanded for resentencing, as no rational basis existed to justify the distinction in treatment because both parties were equally culpable and equally responsible for the child's care and maintenance. The court concluded that the remaining sentencing section providing for a $ 100 fine was severable and that defendant should be resentenced under that provision.


OUTCOME: The judgment of lower court that penalized defendant father under the fornication and bastardy statute was vacated, as the statute unconstitutionally permitted more severe sanctions on male offenders that females, and remanded for resentencing limited to $ 100 fine equally imposed under a severable provision of the statute.

Baltimore & O. R. Co. v. Commonwealth, Dep't of Labor & Industry


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


461 Pa. 68; 334 A.2d 636; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 726


October 11, 1974, Argued  
March 18, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant transportation union sought review of a judgment from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which reversed an order of the Secretary of Labor and Industry, which required that appellee railroads adopt a weekly pay period for certain of their employees under 43 P.S. § 255.1and held that state statute to be unconstitutional because it conflicted with the Railway Labor Act, 45 U.S.C.S. § 151 et seq. (1970).


OVERVIEW: Appellee railroads brought an action after being ordered to adopt weekly pay periods for certain of their employees as required by 43 P.S. § 255.1 as a result of an administrative proceeding commenced by appellant transportation union. The lower court reversed the order and held that 43 P.S. § 255.1 was unconstitutional because it conflicted with the Railway Labor Act, 45 U.S.C.S. § 151 et seq. (1970). On appeal, the court determined that the state statute would be invalid if it stood as an obstacle to the accomplishment and execution of the full purposes and objectives of congress. The court found no conflict between the purposes of the statutes. The court concluded that the state statute expressly deferred to an applicable labor agreement so it could not be applied to prevent the contracting parties from carrying out their agreement upon a subject matter, which directed them to bargain. The court determined that appellees would not violate the Railway Labor Act by complying with the state statute because compliance was precisely what appellant sought. The court reversed the order of the lower court, which set aside the order directing appellees to adopt weekly pay periods.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the lower court setting aside the order, which required appellee railroads to adopt a weekly pay period for certain of their employees. The court held that the state statute did was constitutional because it did not stand as an obstacle to the accomplishment and execution of the full purposes and objectives of the Railway Labor Act.

 

25 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Whitson


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


461 Pa. 101; 334 A.2d 653; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 728


October 8, 1974, Argued  
March 18, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision from a trial court (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of first-degree murder. Appellant contended that his confession was the product of an illegal arrest and an unnecessary delay, that the warrant issued was defective, and that the trial judge committed prejudicial error both in his jury charge and in stating that appellant's motion for a mistrial was frivolous.


OVERVIEW: The Commonwealth prosecuted appellant for first-degree murder. Appellant asserted that his confession should have been excluded because it was the product of an illegal arrest. The court determined that there was probable cause for a warrantless arrest because the investigating officer knew the supporting facts and they were sufficient to find probable cause. The court held that appellant's assertion that his confession was a result of the delay between his arrest and arraignment was without merit. The court stated that delay in arraignment caused by the necessity to investigate appellant's story was permissible. The court found that the search warrant for appellant's car was not defective. The court held that the warrant was based on the officer's testimony gained from speaking with an eyewitness who had given a description of the car. The court further found that the trial judge did not commit prejudicial error in his charge because he recited facts brought out on cross-examination and instructed the jury to use their recollection and he called appellant's motion for mistrial frivolous at a sidebar. Thus, the court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, which convicted appellant of first-degree murder. The court held that appellant's confession was admissible because the investigating officer had probable cause to arrest and that the delay prior to the arraignment was permissible to investigate appellant's story, that the warrant was proper, and that no prejudicial error was committed.

 

26 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Graver


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


461 Pa. 131; 334 A.2d 667; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 730


September 25, 1973, Argued  
March 18, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bar owner challenged an order from the Commonwealth court (Pennsylvania), which issued a preliminary injunction against appellant in appellee Commonwealth of Pennsylvania's action to enjoin appellant from operating his bar as a nuisance under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 47, § 6-611 (1969).


OVERVIEW: Appellee Commonwealth of Pennsylvania filed an action against appellant bar owner to obtain an injunction preventing appellant from operating his bar as a nuisance under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 47, § 6-611 (1969) of the Liquor Code. The trial court granted appellee's request for a preliminary injunction, and appellant challenged the order, arguing that the evidence presented was insufficient to support the decree issuing the preliminary injunction. The court affirmed, finding that "voluminous testimony" established increasingly abhorrent conduct by patrons that mortified and disgusted neighbors and threatened to destroy the neighborhood. Also, the evidence established that appellant's bar had violated numerous Liquor Code violations, and that, as such, the evidence supported issuance of the injunction. The court found that the fact that the bar had been in existence for eleven years did not negate the existence of "immediate harm." The court also found that the trial court properly allowed the custodian of police records to testify as to the records, and that such testimony did not violate the Uniform Business Records as Evidence Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 28, § 91b (1958).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court that granted appellee Commonwealth of Pennsylvania's request for a preliminary injunction against appellant bar owner. The court found that the injunction was proper because the evidence established that neighbors had been subject to increasing abhorrent conduct by patrons of appellant's bar and that the bar had violated multiple Liquor Code provisions, thus making it a nuisance.

 

27 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Redevelopment Authority of Philadelphia v. Lieberman


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


461 Pa. 208; 336 A.2d 249; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 750


November 26, 1973, Argued  
March 18, 1975, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied May 2, 1975.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant condemnee sought review of a decision of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which held that appellant was not entitled to receive compensation for the loss of his liquor license as a result of the condemnation of the premises for which the license was issued. Appellee condemnor argued that appellant was not entitled to have such loss considered in the award of just compensation.


OVERVIEW: The trial court in an eminent domain proceeding held that appellant condemnee, whose retail liquor license lost value as a result of the condemnation of the premises for which the license was issued, was entitled to have such loss considered in the award of just compensation to be paid by appellee condemnor. The appellate court reversed and remanded for a new trial, holding that appellant's loss of the liquor license was not compensable. On appeal to the supreme court, the court vacated the appellate court's decision, and affirmed the trial court. The supreme court examined the definition of "property," as that term was used in the constitutional provisions mandating that property shall not be taken for public use without just compensation. U.S. Const. amend. V, and Pa. Const. art. I, § 10 and art. X, § 4. The court concluded that the trial court properly considered the loss of value in appellant's liquor license resulting from the condemnation. That loss resulted from the destruction of a property interest within the meaning of the United States Constitution and Pennsylvania Constitution, and the Eminent Domain Code, 26 P.S. § 1-101 et seq., (1964).


OUTCOME: The order of the lower court, which held that appellant condemnee was not entitled to receive compensation for the loss of his liquor license as a result of the condemnation of the premises for which the license was issued, was vacated. Appellant was entitled to receive compensation for the loss of his liquor license.

 

28 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Schubach v. Silver


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


461 Pa. 366; 336 A.2d 328; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 778


January 21, 1974, Argued  
March 18, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Proponents of a nursing facility for multiple handicapped children, the city, and the financing bank, appealed from a judgment of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which reversed the trial court, and held that the zoning ordinance at issue was invalid. The Commonwealth Court concluded that the zoning ordinance constituted "spot zoning," and was therefore unconstitutional. Appellees were individuals opposed to the building of the facility.


OVERVIEW: A nursing facility for multiple handicapped children was being constructed on certain land. After prior litigation concerning the zoning of the land, appellees, opponents of the nursing facility, filed an action in equity seeking to enforce the court's decision in earlier litigation and enjoin further construction of the facility. Appellees sought to have the building permits revoked, the structure as it then existed demolished, and the award of money damages to the individual appellees. Appellants, individuals, the city, and the financing bank, argued that the zoning ordinance was valid. The trial court agreed with appellants and held that the zoning ordinance was valid. The lower court reversed the trial court and held that the ordinance was unconstitutional as constituting "spot zoning." The court granted allocatur, and reversed the lower court. The court determined that the ordinance was not "spot zoning," and was therefore valid. The ordinance did not create an "island" of substantially different use without proper justification. Moreover, the court's earlier decisions in the prior litigation were not res judicata or collateral estoppel on any of the issues.


OUTCOME: Order of the lower court, which reversed the trial court, and determined that the ordinance at issue was unconstitutional, was reversed. The ordinance did not create an "island" of substantially different use without proper justification. Therefore, the ordinance was constitutional and did not constitute "spot zoning." The court further ruled that the doctrines of res judicata and collateral estoppel were not applicable.

Altemose Constr. Co. v. Building & Constr. Trades Council


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


461 Pa. 524; 337 A.2d 277; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 804; 88 L.R.R.M. 3448


January 15, 1974, Argued  
March 18, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant trade council sought review of a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which denied dissolution of a preliminary injunction enjoining appellant from certain activities after the occurrence of acts of violence during picketing at various building sites.


OVERVIEW: A trial court issued a preliminary injunction enjoining appellant trade council from certain activities after the occurrence of acts of violence during picketing at various building sites. Appellant's motion to modify or dissolve the preliminary injunction was denied. Appellant sought review. The court concluded that appellant's attempt to obtain review of the denial to dissolve the preliminary injunction was required to be quashed. Decisional law provided that a denial of dissolution of a preliminary injunction was interlocutory and, therefore, not appealable. Moreover, if appellant's petition was premised on 17 P.S. § 211.502(c), that statute provided that petition for allowance of appeal from an interlocutory order was required to be filed within 20 days of its entry. Appellant's petition was filed well past the 20-day period. Accordingly, the appeal was quashed.


OUTCOME: The court quashed the appeal of an order denying dissolution of a preliminary injunction enjoining appellant trade council from certain activities after the occurrence of acts of violence during picketing at various building sites. The court concluded that the order was nonappealable or that the appeal was untimely.

Glass v. Commonwealth, Dep't of Transp., Bureau of Traffic Safety


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


460 Pa. 362; 333 A.2d 768; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 653


October 11, 1974, Argued  
February 20, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant driver challenged an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a decision by appellee Pennsylvania Department of Transportation, Bureau of Traffic Safety, to suspend appellant's license under 75 P.S. § 624.1(a) after he was arrested for the offense of driving under the influence of alcohol and refused to submit to a breathalyzer test.


OVERVIEW: Appellant driver was arrested for driving under the influence of alcohol after being involved in a motor vehicle accident. The arresting police officer had not seen appellant operating the vehicle, nor had he obtained a warrant for appellant's arrest. Appellant refused to submit to a breathalyzer test, and, under 75 P.S. § 624.1(a), appellee Pennsylvania Department of Transportation, Bureau of Traffic Safety, suspended his license. Appellant challenged the suspension, and the lower court upheld the decision. On appeal, the court affirmed, finding that reference to the word "arrest" in § 624.1(a) was not a reference to a lawful arrest and that appellee had the authority to suspend appellant's license even in the absence of a lawful arrest. The court found that had the legislature intended otherwise, it would have stated "lawful arrest" as it had done in other statutes. The court found that the danger present to the public by intoxicated drivers and the interest in removing them from the roads outweighed the public interest in deterring illegal arrests (particularly where the arrest was not unconstitutional).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court that upheld the decision of appellee Pennsylvania Department of Transportation, Bureau of Traffic Safety, to suspend appellant driver's license. The court found that the legislature did not intend that an arrest be legal in order for appellee to have the authority to suspend a license after a person suspected of driving under the influence of alcohol refused to take a breathalyzer test.

Commonwealth v. Saunders


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


459 Pa. 677; 331 A.2d 193; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 601


April 24, 1974, Argued  
January 27, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the trial court (Pennsylvania), which convicted her of second-degree murder and sentenced her to incarceration.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was present at the apartment of a drug dealer when he refused to sell to someone who did not have enough money. The prospective purchaser left the premises and met a friend downstairs who began yelling at the occupants of the dealer's apartment. When the friend walked to his car, a shotgun blast coming from the apartment killed him. Defendant was arrested and later confessed to the crime after failing a polygraph test. At trial, defendant took the stand and corroborated the confession. Defendant was convicted and sentenced, and appeal went directly to the court. Additionally, the imposition of the minimum sentence was proper because the statute proscribing the imposition of minimum sentences for women was held unconstitutional subsequent to defendant's sentencing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence of the trial court entered on defendant's conviction for second-degree murder because defendant's testimony at trial that corroborated her confession disentitled her from claiming that an error in admitting the statement into evidence constituted grounds for reversal.

Tosto v. Pennsylvania Nursing Home Loan Agency


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


460 Pa. 1; 331 A.2d 198; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 602


November 27, 1974, Argued  
January 27, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer sought to enjoin the operation of the Nursing Home Loan Agency Law, 62 P.S. § 1521.01 et seq., as constitutionally defective.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayer brought an action to enjoin the operation of the Nursing Home Loan Agency Law, 62 P.S. § 1521.101 et seq., claiming it contained constitutional defects. The court concluded that plaintiff failed to demonstrate that a program of making loans to nursing homes for repair, reconstruction, and rehabilitation was not a rational approach to assisting the homes to comply with state and federal safety standards and thus enhance the safety of the residents. The court also concluded that the legislative program was reasonably designed to effectuate the law's public purpose. The court found that the argument that private gain to nursing homes somehow outweighed the public purpose of the law was meritless because it was reasonably designed to yield benefits to the public. The court held that the pervasive general policy was clearly sufficient to satisfy the constitutional requirement that the basic policy choices be made by the legislature. The court also held that the legislature had provided adequate standards and guidelines for the guidance and restraint of administrative discretion The court entered a judgment on the pleadings in favor of defendants agency and treasurer.


OUTCOME: The court entered a judgment on the pleadings upholding the constitutionality of the Nursing Home Loan Agency Law. The court held that a program of making loans to nursing homes for repair, reconstruction, and rehabilitation was a rational approach to assisting the homes comply with safety standards and enhance the safety of the residents and that it was reasonably designed to effectuate the its public purpose.

 

33 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Morgan


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


460 Pa. 112; 331 A.2d 444; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 614


October 7, 1974, Argued  
January 27, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed his conviction for violation of 18 P.S. § 4211 (1973), a statute punishing one who publicly or privately mutilates, defaces, defiles or tramples upon, or casts contempt either by words or act upon the American flag. Defendant sewed a flag on the seat of his pants.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sewed a flag to the seat of his pants and was prosecuted under of 18 P.S. § 4211 (1973), a statute punishing one who publicly or privately mutilates, defaces, defiles or tramples upon, or casts contempt either by words or act upon the American flag. He was convicted and his sentence upheld by the superior court. The court reversed the conviction. The court held that the statute was impermissibly vague. The supreme court had held that a statute prohibiting treating the flag contemptuously was unconstitutionally void for vagueness. The difference between "treats contemptuously" and "casts contempt upon" was constitutionally insignificant. The court also held that defendant's conviction for "defiling" the flag was also unconstitutional for vagueness. Had the term been defined more narrowly, as in physically destroying or desecrating a flag so as to affect its physical integrity, the statute would not have been void for vagueness.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction because the language of the statute that criminalizes behavior that "casts contempt upon" or "defiles" the American flag was impermissibly vague and violated defendant's right to due process.

 

34 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Segers


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


460 Pa. 149; 331 A.2d 462; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 618


January 21, 1974, Argued  
January 27, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the trial court (Pennsylvania) convicting him of murder in the first degree. Defendant argued that bail was fixed at an unreasonably high figure causing him to remain incarcerated and severely hampering him in the preparation of his defense and that the denial of his petition for a change of court-appointed counsel was error.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted by a jury of murder in the first degree, in connection with the shooting death of a man he believed was having an affair with his common-law wife. On review, defendant argued that bail was fixed at an unreasonably high figure, causing him to remain incarcerated and severely hampering him in the preparation of his defense, and that the denial of his petition for a change of court-appointed counsel was error. The appeals court affirmed. The court ruled that although all offenses are bailable, the right to release before trial is conditioned upon the accused giving adequate assurance he will appear for trial. In light of defendant's flight from the Commonwealth following the slaying, bail could have been properly denied him. Thus, he could not complain when bail was actually set, albeit at a comparatively high figure. Similarly, the court ruled that defendant's petition for a change of court-appointed counsel was properly denied since an indigent is not entitled to free counsel of his choice. The court ruled that defendant's general dissatisfaction with court-appointed attorneys did not constitute good cause for the granting of the petition.


OUTCOME: The appeals court affirmed defendant's conviction for murder in the first degree. The court ruled that because the right to release before trial is conditioned upon the accused giving adequate assurance he will appear for trial, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in setting a high bail in light of defendant's flight from the Commonwealth following the slaying.

 

35 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Frantz v. Baldwin-Whitehall School Dist.


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


460 Pa. 192; 331 A.2d 484; 1975 Pa. LEXIS 622


October 1, 1973, Argued  
January 27, 1975, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant teacher challenged an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) affirming the action of appellee school district in terminating his employment as a teacher on his 62nd birthday, pursuant to 24 P.S. § 11-1122. Appellant argued that he was denied his constitutional right of equal protection by being forced into retirement as a member of the class covered by Social Security, but not under the Act of 1956, 65 P.S. § 201 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellee school district terminated appellant teacher's employment as a school teacher on his 62nd birthday, pursuant to 24 P.S. § 11-1122. Appellant sought a writ of mandamus in the trial court, which rendered judgment for appellee. The lower court affirmed the action, and appellant sought further review. Appellant contended that he was denied his constitutional right of equal protection by being forced into retirement as a member of the class covered by Social Security but not under the Act of 1956, 65 P.S. § 201 et seq. (Act of 1956). The reviewing court affirmed. The state supreme court ruled that the classification was valid because it related to a valid state interest in protecting the availability of benefits to those teachers who joined the system pursuant to the Act of 1956, which was reasonably insured by the exemption for those teachers. In contrast, the supreme court noted that appellant's access to benefits, by his election not to become a member of such system, had no relationship to his employment as a teacher.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court affirmed the appellate court order that affirmed the action of appellee school district in terminating appellant's employment as a school teacher on his 62nd birthday. The supreme court ruled that the exemption policy did not violate the constitution because it was based on substantial distinctions between different classes of teachers and was pertinent to the purpose of the law.

Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board v. Zelem


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


459 Pa. 399; 329 A.2d 477; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 479; 88 L.R.R.M. 2524


November 16, 1973, Argued  
December 5, 1974, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board and a union challenged the decision of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a judgment finding that appellants engaged in an unfair labor practice against appellee employee under the Public Employee Relations Act (PERA), 43 P. S. § 1201.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board and union, challenged an appellate court decision which affirmed a judgment finding that appellants engaged in an unfair labor practice against appellee employee under the Public Employee Relations Act (PERA), 43 P. S. § 1201. Appellants claimed that a renewal contract which contained an agency-shop provision requiring all employees, even non-members of the union, to pay dues was permitted under the grandfather clause of § 904. Section 904 banned agency-shop provisions in collective bargaining agreements effective after January, 1970. The renewal contract was effective as of July, 1970, so appellants argued that it should be considered as an extension of the original contract, which was effective in October 1967. The problem, as asserted by appellee, was that the original agreement had exempted appellee, as an existing employee, from the agency-shop requirement. Thus, appellee asserted that the renewal contract was not an extension of the first contract. The court agreed, finding that the second contract was not an example of continued bargaining because only appellee was affected. The court affirmed the appellate court decision.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed an appellate court decision affirming an unfair labor practice judgment against appellants, Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board and union. The renewal contract mandating an agency-shop provision was not an extension of the first contract, which did not require compliance with the provision. Thus, the agency-shop provision was not within the grandfather clause of the statute banning such provisions.

 

37 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Riley


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


459 Pa. 428; 329 A.2d 511; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 481


November 13, 1973, Argued  
December 5, 1974, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, intestate heirs of the decedent, challenged an order of a trial court (Pennsylvania), which granted appellee church's request for reargument and held that the Pennsylvania Mortmain Statute, 1947 Pa. Laws 89, violated the First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I, and the Due Process Clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: The heirs argued that the trial court erred in granted the request for reargument where the trial court had already granted the relief sought by the church. The court disagreed. The request for a reargument was made within a reasonable period of time and before the time for filing an appeal had expired. Where the trial court attempted to bring all the facts and relevant law before it upon petition for rehearing filed within the appropriate period of time, it was to have been commended for its attempt to insure the just and comprehensive resolution of the case. As to the merits of the ruling, the court affirmed the trial court on the basis of an opinion in a case filed that same day -- Estate of Cavill, 329 A.2d 503 (1974). Cavill held that the Pennsylvania Mortmain Statute, 1947 Pa. Laws 89, was violative of equal protection. Affirmation was required even where the trial court came to the correct result for the wrong reason.


OUTCOME: The order was affirmed.

Stottlemyer v. Stottlemyer


No. 27, May T., 1974


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


458 Pa. 503; 329 A.2d 892; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 749


January 21, 1974, Argued  
December 5, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, March T., 1973, No. 2, affirming order of Court of Common Pleas of York County, Jan. T., 1972, No. 231, in case of Marian Stottlemyer, a minor by her mother and next friend, Daisy Whipperman v. Eugene F. Stottlemyer.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife sought review of an order from the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), affirming a trial court's order dismissing appellant's divorce petition for a lack of jurisdiction. Appellant claimed that a residency requirement under the Act of 1929, 23 P.S. § 16 (1974), penalized the exercise of her constitutional right of interstate travel and was subject to strict scrutiny under the compelling state interest test.


OVERVIEW: Appellant wife and appellee husband moved from their home state. They later moved back, and appellant filed for divorce after being in the state only four months. Appellee asserted that appellant failed to meet the residency requirement of the Act of 1929, 23 P.S. § 16 (1974), and that the court lacked jurisdiction. The trial court sustained appellee's objections. The appellate court affirmed. The reviewing court granted allocutor. The court affirmed, finding the imposition of a period of residence as a prerequisite to the exercise of the right to seek a dissolution of the marital relationship is not such a penalty upon interstate travel as to call for application of the "compelling state interest" test. Thus, in applying a rational basis test, the court found the state had an interest in the marriage relationship and in avoiding serious problems inherent in a divorce decree issued without a jurisdictional basis. Because the state's residency requirement was rationally related to the state's interest, the parties were required to meet the one-year residency requirement before petitioning for divorce.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's finding that because appellant wife failed to meet a state residency requirement, the state was without jurisdiction to grant her divorce petition. Because the state had a legitimate interest in the marriage relationship and in preventing problems arising from decrees granted without a jurisdictional basis, and the residency requirement was rationally related to this interest, the requirement was upheld.

 

39 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth by Creamer v. Monumental Properties, Inc.


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


459 Pa. 450; 329 A.2d 812; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 483


May 22, 1974, Argued  
December 5, 1974, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed a Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) judgment, which sustained appellees', landlords and printing companies, preliminary objections and dismissed the State's complaint. The lower court held that the Unfair Trade Practices and Consumer Protection Law, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 73, §§ 201- 1 to 201- 9 (1971), was not applicable to the State's claims that the use of appellees' leases constituted an unfair or deceptive act or practice.


OVERVIEW: The State had filed the action against appellees, landlords and printing companies, under the Unfair Trade Practices and Consumer Protection Law (Law), Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 73, §§ 201- 1 to 201- 9 (1971). The State claimed that appellees used printed form leases allegedly in violation of the Law, and that the printing companies illegally printed and sold the form leases. The reviewing court held that the Law covered allegedly unfair or deceptive practices in connection with the leasing of housing. However, that portion of the order dismissing the state's complaint for failure to state a cause of action with respect to the use of "unenforceable" lease provisions was affirmed. For reasons different than those advanced by the lower court, that portion of its order dismissing the complaint with respect to the printing companies was also affirmed. In all other respects the order was vacated and the case was remanded. No support in the federal precursors of the Consumer Protection Law could be found for an exclusion for leasing from the Law's broad prohibition of unfair and deceptive practices in market transactions.


OUTCOME: That portion of the order that dismissed the State's complaint for failure to state a cause of action with respect to the use of "unenforceable" lease provisions was affirmed. For reasons different than those advanced by the lower court, that portion of its order dismissing the complaint with respect to appellee printing companies was also affirmed. In all other respects the order was vacated and the case was remanded.

 

40 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Cavill


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


459 Pa. 411; 329 A.2d 503; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 480


May 20, 1974, Argued  
December 5, 1974, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant heirs challenged the decision of the trial court (Pennsylvania), which held that decedent's residuary estate should be distributed to the five charitable institutions named in decedent's will.


OVERVIEW: Appellant heirs challenged the trial court decision which held that decedent's residuary estate should be distributed to the five charitable institutions named in decedent's will. Appellants argued that the gifts, which were made 24 days prior to decedent's death should be invalidated pursuant to 20 Pa. Const. Stat. § 2507 (1). The court held that the statute violated the Equal Protection of U.S. Const. amend. XIV because it impermissibly divided testators into two classes, one whose wills provided for charitable gifts and who died within 30 days of executing their wills. The other was composed of testators who either made no charitable gifts or survived the execution of their wills by at least 30 days. The statute rendered invalid any charitable gifts made by a testator in the first class if any person "who would benefit by its invalidity" objected. Clearly, the statutory classification bore only the most tenuous relation to the legislative purpose of protecting the decedent's family, and also sought to nullify bequests to charity even where the testator left no immediate family. The court affirmed the trial court's decree.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decree which distributed a decedent's residual estate to the five charities named in her will. The court held that the statutory provision invalidating such gifts made less than 30 days before a decedent's death lacked a fair and substantial relation to the legislative intent of protecting an elderly person from influence prior to death and thus violated the Equal Protection Clause.
