Hetherington v. McHale


Nos. 40, 41 and 42, May T., 1974


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


458 Pa. 479; 329 A.2d 250; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 747


January 17, 1974, Argued  
November 22, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from order of Commonwealth Court, Nos. 555, 559 and 560 C.D. 1973, in case of Burton Hetherington et al. v. James A. McHale et al.



DISPOSITION: Order of Commonwealth Court reversed and preliminary objections sustained.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Three actions were filed by appellees, designees to a committee to allocate tax revenue, to enjoin a committee from meeting or acting without appellees, and separate quo warranto and mandamus actions to enforce their appointments. Appellants, represented by the Attorney General, challenged a decision by the Pennsylvania Commonwealth Court that held the Pennsylvania Harness Racing Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 15, § 2616(e) (Supp. 1974), was valid.


OVERVIEW: After the Pennsylvania Attorney General ordered the Secretary of Agriculture to refuse state agricultural committee membership to eight people appointed by private groups, appellees, the people who were refused membership, sued to obtain judicial enforcement of their appointments. Appellants challenged the ruling of the commonwealth court, which held constitutional the Pennsylvania Harness Racing Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 15, § 2616(e) (Supp. 1974), which granted to private organizations authority to appoint members of a state committee responsible for disbursing public funds. Appellees claimed the statute was constitutional because it imposed standards on the appointment process, and because appellees had special expertise in agriculture. The state supreme court reversed the commonwealth court's decision and found the statute unconstitutional. The court held that appellees' expertise and the statute's appointment guidelines could not be substituted for the constitutional requirement that people who made government decisions be elected by the people.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the ruling that held constitutional the Pennsylvania Harness Racing Act, for the statute impermissibly granted government power to private groups.

 

42 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Casey v. Zoning Hearing Bd.


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


459 Pa. 219; 328 A.2d 464; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 465


January 15, 1974, Argued  
November 20, 1974, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant zoning board sought review of a judgment from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which directed the township's building inspector to issue a building permit to appellee land developer.


OVERVIEW: Appellant zoning board challenged the appellate court's ruling which held that the township's amendment that added a multi-family housing provision to the zoning ordinance did not cure the constitutional defect in the original ordinance and ordered that the township issue a permit to appellee land developer. Appellant sought review of the appellate court's ruling and argued that there was sufficient public declaration of its intent to provide for multi-family housing in the township zoning ordinance. The court vacated the appellate court's order and remanded to appellant with instructions to issue a permit once appellee had complied with the administrative requirements of the zoning ordinance. The court held that under the pending ordinance doctrine, the amendment involved was not pending when this challenge was commenced. Further, the court held that because the zoning ordinance was already amended, the stay provided for in 53 P.S. § 11009(2)(ii) was not necessary. Finally, the court held that it was not justified in ordering the immediate issuance of the building permit because the right to the permit was premised on other prior approvals, which had not been given.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the appellate court's order that the township to grant a building permit to appellee land developer. The court remanded and ordered appellant zoning board to issue a permit once appellee had complied with the administrative requirements of the zoning ordinance. The court held that pursuant to the pending ordinance doctrine, the amendment was not pending and that the stay provided for in the statute was not needed.

 

43 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Borough of Greentree v. Bd. of Prop. Assessments, Appeals & Review


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


459 Pa. 268; 328 A.2d 819; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 471


September 28, 1973, Argued  
November 20, 1974, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, borough and residents, sought review of a judgment from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which dismissed their action against appellees, county property assessment board and county commissioners, challenging the constitutionality of certain sections of the Second Class County Assessment Law, 72 P.S. § 5452.1 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, borough and residents, brought an action challenging the constitutionality of the Second Class County Assessment Law, 72 P.S. § 5452.1 et seq. The lower court dismissed the action. On appeal, the court held that a court of equity was not always required to entertain a constitutional attack on a taxing statute without regard for the adequacy of the legislatively prescribed remedy. The court determined that the more direct the attack on a statute, the more likely it was that the exercise of equitable jurisdiction would not damage the role of the administrative agency, nor require the factual fabric which might develop at the agency level. The court concluded that the specialized proceedings would be of little use in determining the constitutionality of the statute and that whatever benefit could be derived from the filing of individual appeals would be far outweighed by the inconvenience, delay, and expense involved. The court held that the statutory remedy was not adequate and that there was a substantial question of constitutionality going directly to the validity of the assessment scheme. The court reversed the dismissal and remanded for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of the action challenging the constitutionality of the Second Class County Assessment Law and remanded for further proceedings because the statutory remedy was not adequate, and there was a substantial question of constitutionality going directly to the validity of the assessment scheme.

Goodman v. Kennedy


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


459 Pa. 313; 329 A.2d 224; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 475


April 30, 1973, Argued  
October 30, 1974, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied December 27, 1974.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant merchant challenged the decision of the trial court (Pennsylvania) that denied his complaint in equity that sought to enjoin enforcement of the criminal statute that prevented the Sunday sales of fresh meats, produce, and groceries under 18 Pa. Stat. § 7364, except for three enumerated exceptions.


OVERVIEW: Appellant merchant operated a number of grocery stores and had employees arrested for operating on Sunday. Appellant filed a complaint in equity that sought to enjoin enforcement of the criminal statute that proscribed the Sunday sales of fresh meats, produce, and groceries under 18 Pa. Stat. § 7364. The trial court denied relief. The court affirmed its earlier decision in another case that the legislature's choice of less than 10 employees as the dividing line between the classifications for the purpose of prohibiting Sunday sales did not violate Pa. Const. art. 3, or U.S. Const. amend XIV equal protection clauses. The court found that the second exception allowing sales on Sunday if the groceries were sold by the proprietor or members of his immediate family was unconstitutional because the family status classification violated the equal protection clause. The court held that this second exception was severable from the law under 1 Pa. Stat. § 1925.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision in upholding the Sunday sales of fresh meats, produce, and groceries, but severed the second exception allowing sales on Sundays, holding that the classification based on family status violated the United States and Pennsylvania's equal protection clause.

Commonwealth v. Morewood Realty Corp.


No. 20, May T., 1973


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


458 Pa. 204; 327 A.2d 328; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 710


May 22, 1973, Argued  
October 29, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order and judgment of Commonwealth Court, No. 698 Transfer Docket 1970, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Morewood Realty Corp.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Commonwealth sought review of a decision of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which held that out-of-state property owned by appellee realty corporation should be excluded from the valuation of appellee's capital stock for purposes of the Pennsylvania franchise tax, 72 P. S. § 1871(b).


OVERVIEW: The Commonwealth challenged a decision of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which held that out-of-state property owned by appellee realty corporation, should be excluded from the valuation of appellee's capital stock for purposes of the Pennsylvania franchise tax, 72 P. S. § 1871(b). Appellant alleged that the trial court erred in excluding the New York assets and receipts because appellee had not met its burden of proof that inclusion would create an unconstitutional application of the tax; and that the valuation based on the book value of the properties was incorrect. The facts showed that the only connection between the Pennsylvania properties and the out-of-state enterprises was common ownership. Thus, appellee amply met its burden in establishing that it was a multiform business whose out-of state assets were unrelated to and completely independent of its Pennsylvania franchise. The Commonwealth made no attempt to offer specific evidence of the "other values" such as value for tax purposes and appraisal value, so the book value absent such other evidence was an appropriate measure of the franchise's value. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment excluding appellee realty corporation's out-of-state properties from valuation of appellee's capital stock for purposes of the Pennsylvania franchise tax. Appellee sustained its burden in showing that its inclusion would create an unconstitutional application of the tax because the only connection between the Pennsylvania properties and the out-of-state enterprises was common ownership.

Commonwealth ex rel. Carcaci v. Brandamore


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


459 Pa. 48; 327 A.2d 1; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 449


April 25, 1974, Argued  
October 28, 1974, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner challenged a ruling of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed petitioner's request for a writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: The Pennsylvania House of Representatives adopted a House Resolution which established a special committee to investigate matters pertaining to law enforcement. Petitioner, a lieutenant of the state police, was summoned to testify before the committee concerning alleged wiretapping activities by the state police. On the advice of counsel, petitioner refused to answer certain questions despite being requested several times on separate occasions to do so. The House of Representatives then adopted a resolution which found petitioner in contempt of the House and authorized the issuance of a warrant for his commitment. Petitioner filed his application for writ of habeas corpus in the trial court and the application was dismissed. Petitioner challenged that ruling. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the power of all Houses of the General Assembly to vindicate their authority and processes by punishing acts of contempt committed in their presence was inherent in the legislative function. The House had the power under 46 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 61 to commit to prison any person who refused to answer legal questions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court ruling, which dismissed petitioner's request for a writ of habeas corpus. The court held that the House of Representatives properly enforced their power to commit petitioner after petitioner refused to answer questions when called before the House for questioning on an issue presented during legislative investigation.

In re Alignment of Election Dists.


Nos. 416 to 419, inclusive, Jan. T., 1974


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


458 Pa. 607; 327 A.2d 56; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 757


Argued April 30, 1974.  
October 16, 1974, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reported at 458 Pa. 607 at 608.


PRIOR HISTORY: Original Opinion of May 9, 1974, Reported at 458 Pa. 607.



DISPOSITION: Accordingly we reiterate our order of May 9, 1974, that the orders below are reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), that granted petitions of appellee county board of elections for the consolidation and realignment of various election districts, on the ground that the notice provisions of Pa. Code § 504, 25 Pa. Stat. § 2704, were not complied with.


OVERVIEW: The trial court granted the petitions of appellee county board of elections for the consolidation and realignment of various election districts. Appeals were taken to the Commonwealth Court, but were then transferred to the appellate court pursuant to 17 Pa. Stat. § 211.503(b), since they involved the right to public office. Appellant argued for reversal of the orders of the court on the ground that the notice provisions of the Election Code, Pa. Code § 504, 25 P.S. § 2704, were not complied with. Notices of the proposed consolidations were posted on February 27, 1964, the petitions were filed on March 4, a hearing was begun on March 8, and the final orders consolidating the election districts were entered on March 11, 1974. The appellate court reversed the order. The court determined that the court was without power to set a hearing date or to entertain objections to the consolidation prior to filing a petition. The court ruled that the actions of the trial court were not in compliance with § 504 because the court considered the petitions only four days after filing and the final orders were entered only seven days after filing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order and held that the actions of the trial court in setting a hearing to entertain objections to the consolidation proposal only four days after filing was in contravention of the Election Code. The court ruled that the limitations on the court's power created by the code were jurisdictional requirements that could not be waived.

 

48 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


In re Silverberg


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


459 Pa. 107; 327 A.2d 106; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 455


January 22, 1974, Argued  
October 16, 1974, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of the decision of a three-judge special disciplinary court (Pennsylvania), that suspended them from the practice of law after finding that they had engaged in unethical conduct on the ground that their constitutional rights guaranteed by U. S. Const. amend. V and Pa. Const. art. I, § 9 were violated.


OVERVIEW: Appellants sought review after they were suspended from the practice of law arguing that their constitutional rights were violated when they were required during cross examination to admit that they had claimed the privilege against self-incrimination in the preliminary proceedings. The court reversed, concluding that it was a violation of appellants' rights pursuant to U. S. Const. amend. V and Pa. Const. art. I, § 9 to admit the evidence. The court found that appellants' credibility was impeached and possibly severely impaired by the unconstitutional references to their prior claims of the privilege, and that this was an improper penalty imposed for exercising a constitutional privilege. The court stated that a prior statement could have been used to impeach the credibility of a witness, but only if the prior statement was inconsistent with the testimony of the witness at trial. In this instance, the court reasoned that asserting the privilege against self-incrimination was not inconsistent with testimony of innocence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision that suspended appellants from the practice of law for unethical conduct and remanded the case for further proceedings. The court found that it was a violation of appellants' constitutional rights to elicit during cross examination that they had claimed the privilege against self-incrimination in the preliminary proceeding.

October 16, 1974, Decided

Commonwealth v. Di Francesco


Nos. 205 to 208, inclusive, Jan. T., 1973


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


458 Pa. 188; 329 A.2d 204; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 706


January 8, 1974, Argued  
October 16, 1974, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued April 22, 1974. Reargument Refused December 17, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from orders of Superior Court, Oct. T., 1971, Nos. 824, 825, 847, and 848, affirming judgments of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County, March T., 1969, No. 367, Sept. T., 1969, No. 321, and Nos. 514 and 876 of 1970, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Samuel R. DiFrancesco, Jr., John H. Buckwalter, Anthony L. Steckel, Jr., and Fred A. Douts.



DISPOSITION: Judgments of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of the decisions of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), that affirmed their convictions of operating a vehicle while under the influence of intoxicating liquors on the ground that 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 624.1(c) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellants challenged their convictions for operating a vehicle while under the influence of intoxicating liquors arguing that 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 624.1(c), which established a presumption of intoxication, was unconstitutional. The court affirmed finding that § 624.1(c) created a presumption, but that it could be rebutted by a defendant. Further, the court found that the trier of fact could find that the presumption was not applicable in a particular case, as well. Given that a blood alcohol level as defined in the statute did not prove beyond a reasonable doubt that a defendant was guilty, the court found that the statute was not unconstitutional. Appellants argued that they were also denied their right to cross examine the experts that determined the blood alcohol level at which intoxication normally occurred. The court found that the legislature could incorporate findings of fact into statutory inferences without infringing on a defendant's right of confrontation because a defendant was entitled to call his own experts at trial to rebut the presumption.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders finding that the applicable statute was not unconstitutional because it merely created a presumption that an individual with a certain blood alcohol level was under the influence of intoxicating liquors because a defendant had the opportunity to rebut that presumption.

Commonwealth v. Butler


No. 410, Jan. T., 1971


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


458 Pa. 289; 328 A.2d 851; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 721


April 25, 1974, Argued  
October 16, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County, Jan. T., 1971, No. 103, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Ronald Allen Butler.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his sentence of punishment of 10 to 20 years imprisonment for his conviction of murder in the second degree entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County (Pennsylvania). Appellant's sole challenge was with regard to the constitutionality of the Muncy Act, 61 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 566 (1974), which required men to receive minimum sentences with no such requirement for women.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, a male offender, was convicted of second-degree murder and sentenced to 10 to 20 years of imprisonment. Appellant challenged his sentence, claiming that the Act of 1911, 19 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 1051-1057 (1964) and the new Muncy Act, 61 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 566 (1974) by their joint operation were constitutionally impermissible. The court affirmed appellant's sentence, but held that the new Muncy Act, 61 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 566 was unconstitutional because it required trial courts not to fix a minimum sentence when imposing sentence on a woman. The court held that the idea that one person was eligible for parole at a different time than another person solely because of his or her sex was discrimination of the most obvious sort under Pa. Cons. art. I, § 28 and U.S. Const. amend. XIV. As such, the court struck the last sentence of § 566, which prohibited minimum sentences for female offenders. However, the court found that the Act of 1911, under which appellant was sentenced, was completely neutral and expressed a legislative judgment concerning sentencing alternatives that applied to any person regardless of sex.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's sentence of 10 to 20 years imprisonment for his conviction of second degree murder because the general sentencing statute under which appellant was sentenced was completely neutral on its face. However, the court held unconstitutional that portion of the new Muncy Act that directed that no minimum sentence was imposed on women who were convicted of crime.

Commonwealth v. Piper


No. 27, Jan. T., 1973

458 Pa. 307; 328 A.2d 845; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 722


November 8, 1972, Submitted

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review from an order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas for Berks County (Pennsylvania) for prostitution and solicitation to commit sodomy. Appellant claimed error in the trial court's refusal to charge on identification, entrapment, and argued that the facts did not support a verdict for both convictions. She also alleged error in the sentence imposed.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was tried by a jury and convicted of prostitution and solicitation to commit sodomy. A sentence of fifteen months imprisonment and a $ 500 fine was imposed by the trial court. The lower court affirmed without an opinion in a per curiam order. Appellant alleged that the trial court erroneously refused to charge the jury on both identification and entrapment. Finally, the court found that the trial court's sentence was not so excessive as to be constitutionally impermissible.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence because appellant had failed to preserve any of her issues for review by the court. Additionally, the court determined that the sentence imposed by the trial court was not so excessive as to be constitutionally impermissible.

Commonwealth v. Duncan


No. 224, Jan. T., 1973


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


456 Pa. 495; 321 A.2d 917; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 555


April 30, 1973, Argued  
July 1, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court affirming judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, May T., 1971, No. 1876, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Leon Duncan.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), that convicted appellant of loitering and prowling under 18 Pa. Stat. 4418, on the ground that appellant's activity did not come within the coverage of the statute and the statute was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Appellant entered the rear fire tower of a hospital after the end of visiting hours. In response to a call by a nurse who heard noise in the tower, the police proceeded to the area and arrested appellant. Appellant was convicted of loitering and prowling under 18 Pa. Stat. 4418. Appellant contended that his activity did not come within the coverage of the statute and the statute was unconstitutionally vague. Appellant argued that the statute did not cover indoor prowling and loitering and relied on the principle of strict construction of penal statutes. The appellate court affirmed and held that the statute was designed to alleviate the danger to human life and quiet enjoyment of one's dwelling, which was presented by the nighttime loitering or prowling of malicious-minded individuals and the danger was intensified when the proscribed activity occurred inside, rather than outside, the dwelling. The court found that the statute gave adequate warning to potential offenders and was not unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence and held that the construction urged by appellant that his activity was not within the coverage of the statute thwarted the legislative intent behind the statute, which was designed to alleviate the danger to human life and quiet enjoyment of one's dwelling. The court ruled that the statute gave adequate warning to potential offenders.

 

55 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Mason


SUPREME COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


456 Pa. 602; 322 A.2d 357; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 574


May 22, 1974, Argued.  
July 1, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals, Nos. 6 and 7, May T., 1974, from orders of Superior Court, March T., 1972, Nos. 76 and 77, affirming judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Mifflin County, No. 21 of 1970, reversing judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Mifflin County, No. 22 of 1970, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Benjamin J. Mason. Order of Superior Court and judgment of sentence in No. 6 reversed; order of Superior Court reversing judgment of sentence in No. 7 affirmed. 603

Same case in Superior Court: 222 Pa. Superior Ct. 453.

Indictments charging defendant with criminal libel and anonymous communication by publication. Before LANSBERRY, J., specially presiding.

Verdict of guilty on both indictments and judgment of sentence entered thereon. Defendant appealed to Superior Court, which affirmed the conviction for criminal libel and reversed the conviction for anonymous communication, opinion by PACKEL, J., concurring and dissenting opinion by SPAULDING, J., in which HOFFMAN, J., joined; WATKINS, J., would certify the appeal to the Supreme Court. Appeals by defendant and Commonwealth to Supreme Court allowed.



DISPOSITION: 1. Order of the Superior Court at No. 76 March [***2] Term 1972 reversed; Judgment of Sentence at No. 21 of 1970 Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Trial Division, Mifflin County, reversed.

2. Order of the Superior Court at No. 77 March Term 1972 affirmed; Judgment of Sentence at No. 22 of 1970 Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Trial Division, Mifflin County, reversed.

Mr. Justice ROBERTS took no part in the consideration or decision of these cases.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant publisher sought review in one case from an order of the lower court affirming a judgment of sentence for criminal libel, and prosecution sought review of a lower court order reversing a conviction for anonymous publication.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted by a judge and jury of thirteen counts of criminal libel and twenty-one counts of anonymous publication. The convictions all arose out of his attacks against four county officials in his irregularly published, mimeographed newspaper. The lower court affirmed the criminal libel convictions, but reversed the convictions for anonymous publications. Cross-petitions for allocatur were filed, prosecution requesting to have the conviction for anonymous publication reinstated, and appellant seeking reversal of his convictions for criminal libel. The court reversed the lower court's affirmation of the criminal libel convictions because the lower court erroneously concluded that a previous decision by only five members of the court was not binding. The court noted that when a majority of the justices participating join in an opinion, the opinion became binding precedent on the courts of the state. The court reversed the lower court's affirmation of the anonymous publication convictions because the publication was not anonymous.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the affirmation of the criminal libel convictions because the lower court erroneously concluded that a previous decision of the court was not binding. The court reversed the affimation of the anonymous publication convictions because the publication was not anonymous.

 

56 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Jackson v. Hendrick


No. 298, Jan. T., 1974


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


457 Pa. 405; 321 A.2d 603; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 849


April 26, 1974, Argued  
July 1, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Commonwealth Court, No. 576 C.D. 1972, reversing in part decree of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, Feb. T., 1971, No. 2437, in case of Gerald Jackson et al. v. Edward J. Hendrick et al.



DISPOSITION: Order of Commonwealth Court modified and decree of Court of Common Pleas reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants inmates sought review of an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) reversing in part a lower court's order providing for the appointment of a master as an administrative aid in implementing a plan to eliminate unconstitutional conditions of confinement. Although the court agreed that appellee prison's conditions were unconstitutional, it found that the appointment of a master violated Pa. R. Civ. P. 1514, 1515.


OVERVIEW: Appellants inmates prevailed on an action asserting that the conditions of their confinement in appellee prison were unconstitutional. The lower court reversed an order of the trial court providing for the appointment of a master as an administrative aid in implementing the plan to eliminate the proscribed conditions, finding that the appointment of a master violated Pa. R. Civ. P. 1514, 1515. The court modified the lower court's decision, reinstating the order calling for appointment of a master. The court noted that the test of whether or not the appointment of a master or similar functionary was permitted was whether or not the functions assigned to the master were excessively judicial. The court held that the functions assigned to the master were not problematic, noting that the master was not asked to act in an administrative fashion, take testimony, or make findings of fact, but was merely required to compile data and make recommendations.


OUTCOME: The court modified the lower court's decision and reinstated the order calling for appointment of a master. The court noted that the test was whether or not the functions assigned to the master were excessively judicial and held that this was not the case. The court noted that the master was not acting in an administrative fashion, taking testimony, or making findings of fact but merely compiling data and making recommendations.

 

57 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Sexton v. Stine


No. 17, May T., 1974


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


456 Pa. 301; 319 A.2d 666; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 526


January 21, 1974, Argued  
May 22, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of York County, Oct. T., 1972, No. 8, in case of Rita Sexton and Willie Mae Mosley, on behalf of themselves and all others similarly situated v. Clair R. Stine, Clerk of Courts of York County, and the County of York.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant complaining witnesses challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of York County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed their exceptions to a dismissal of their complaint as to imposing the costs of prosecuting criminal assault cases that appellants instigated but were dismissed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court dismissed appellant complaining witnesses' complaint as to the imposition of the costs of prosecution on them under 19 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1222 after a grand jury dismissed the criminal assault cases that appellants instigated. The trial court also dismissed the exceptions that appellants filed as to that judgment. The court affirmed that decree on appeal. The court found that appellants' case did not lie in equity because adequate legal remedies in the form of petitioning the trial court for remission of costs and challenging the validity of the statute when the county authorities sought to collect the costs imposed were available. The court further held that the determination of the propriety of equity jurisdiction was distinct and independent from a determination of the propriety of a declaratory judgment. The presence of appellants' declaratory relief claim had no bearing on the decision that equity did not lie in the case. The court also found that appellants could have raised their constitutional arguments in the legal proceedings that were available to them.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of appellant complaining witnesses' exceptions to a dismissal of their complaint as to imposing the costs of prosecuting the criminal assault cases that appellants instigated, but were dismissed. The court found that appellants' case did not lie in equity because adequate legal remedies were available for all of their claims, and a declaratory relief claim had no bearing on the decision as to equity.

 

58 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


State Dental Council & Examining Bd. v. Pollock


No. 2, May T., 1974


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


457 Pa. 264; 318 A.2d 910; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 839


November 14, 1973, Argued  
May 3, 1974, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused November 7, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of Commonwealth Court, No. 1151 C.D. 1971, in case of State Dental Council and Examining Board v. Stanley L. Pollock, D.M.D.



DISPOSITION: Order of Commonwealth Court affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant dentist sought review of a judgment from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed an order from appellee State Dental Council and Examining Board, which suspended appellant's license to practice dentistry for 30 days.


OVERVIEW: Appellee State Dental Council and Examining Board held a hearing and determined that appellant dentist had hired someone who was not a licensed dentist to perform procedures that a dentist was required to perform and therefore appellant had breached his professional conduct. Appellee entered an order suspending appellant's license to practice for 30 days and the commonwealth court affirmed the decision. On appeal the court affirmed the orders of the lower court and appellee. The court held that members of the board were de facto officials whose actions were legally binding. The court found that there was no due process violation in the administrative structure where various functions were handled by distinct entities with no direct affiliation and there was no prejudice shown. The court held that the citation had precisely informed appellant of the charges against him and any delay in procedure was not violative of appellant's due process rights because he had not alleged any harm. The court held also that there could have been no doubt that the tasks done by of the unlicensed staff member were non-delegable based on the language of the regulations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders suspending appellant dentist's license because the procedures employed by appellee State Dental Council and Examining Board had not denied appellant of his rights to due process and where he had not shown any harm, the order was deemed valid.

 

59 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Jackson


No. 9, March T., 1974


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


457 Pa. 79; 319 A.2d 161; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 819


October 1, 1973, Argued  
May 2, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, April T., 1970, No. 125, affirming judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Mercer County, Sept. T., 1967, No. 31, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Floyd Jackson.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence reversed and new trial granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the trial court judgment convicting him of armed robbery and sentencing him to one to four years' imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of armed robbery and sentenced to one to four years' imprisonment. Pursuant to Pa. R. Crim. P. 312, defendant notified the commonwealth pretrial of the names and addresses his alibi witnesses. Defendant's request for the names and addresses of those witnesses the commonwealth planned to produce to place him at the scene of the crime was refused. The appellate court affirmed his conviction. Defendant appealed, and the court reversed the judgment of sentence and granted a new trial. Noting that the Due Process Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV forbids enforcement of alibi rules unless reciprocal discovery rights are given to criminal defendants, the court held that, once defendant, under compulsion of rule 312, disclosed his alibi witnesses to the commonwealth, the commonwealth was constitutionally bound to afford appellant reciprocal discovery by furnishing him the names and addresses of any witnesses whose testimony would refute his alibi. The court found that the trial court committed constitutional error in requiring defendant to supply the names of his alibi witnesses, without directing the commonwealth to afford defendant reciprocal discovery rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of sentence convicting defendant of armed robbery and granted a new trial. The court held that the trial court committed constitutional error in requiring defendant to divulge the details of his case without directing the commonwealth to afford defendant reciprocal discovery rights.

Montgomery County Bar Asso. v. Hecht


No. 143, Jan. T., 1973


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


456 Pa. 13; 317 A.2d 597; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 497


May 4, 1973, Argued  
March 25, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, No. 72-11633, in case of Montgomery County Bar Association v. Samuel J. Hecht.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney sought review of an order prohibiting appellant from practicing law for one year entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), and claimed that there was insufficient evidence to support the determination.


OVERVIEW: Appellant attorney was suspended from law practice for a period of one year by the trial court based upon a finding by the trial court that appellant had committed perjury while testifying under oath in a deposition in aid of judgment regarding appellant's knowledge of the ownership of certain real estate. Appellant sought reversal of the order and claimed that there was insufficient evidence to support the determination and the sanction of the trial court. The court affirmed the trial court's order and held that even though the conduct and proceedings were prior to appellant's conviction for perjury, the trial court had authority to enter the order based upon the petition of appellee bar association. The court further held that there was sufficient evidence to support the determination that appellant had not been truthful during the deposition and that it was not necessary for appellant to be convicted of perjury prior to any disciplinary action being brought against appellant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order suspending appellant, because the trial court had authority to enter the order and there was sufficient evidence to support the trial court's findings.

 

62 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Turner


No. 75, Jan. T., 1973


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


456 Pa. 116; 317 A.2d 298; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 507


November 27, 1972, Argued  
March 25, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, Oct. T., 1971, No. 1250, affirming judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Sept. T., 1968, No. 232, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Frank Turner.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence reversed and new trial granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed his conviction of larceny.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of larceny based on the theft of blank checks from his former employer. Appellant was apprehended after one of the missing checks had been cashed. The court of appeals affirmed the conviction, but on appeal, the court reversed. The court held that the trial court erroneously instructed the jury that they could infer appellant's guilt from his recent possession of the stolen goods by itself. The court noted that the term "recent possession" was undergoing a redefinition in light of present-day experience, and found that it had become a term of art dependent upon the peculiar circumstances of each case. Therefore, the court found that the utility of "recent possession" as a standardized inference was seriously in doubt, although the court did not specifically rule that it no longer existed. The court held that in this case use of the inference segregated appellant's possession of the stolen checks from the other evidence of his guilt but it was not sufficient on its own to establish guilt beyond a reasonable doubt, as there was no proof of the time of the theft. The court remanded for a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals' judgment, which affirmed appellant's conviction of larceny because the trial court erroneously instructed the jury that they could infer appellant's guilt from his recent possession of the stolen goods. His recent possession was insufficient to establish guilt beyond a reasonable doubt as there was no proof of the time of the theft.

 

63 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Stewart


No. 15, May T., 1974


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


456 Pa. 447; 317 A.2d 616; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 552


September 28, 1973, Argued  
March 25, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, Sept. T., 1960, No. 19, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Frederick Charles Stewart.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of murder in the first degree. He contended that Pa. R. Crim. P. 1118(b) precluded a sua sponte declaration of mistrial by the trial judge.


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged his conviction of murder in the first degree on the ground that Pa. R. Crim. 1118(b) precluded a sua sponte declaration of mistrial by the trial judge. Following appellant's first trial, a new trial was ordered, because the trial court had not granted his motion for withdrawal of a juror when the trial court became aware that the father of the victim was a juror. At the second trial, the trial judge learned that the victim's father was employed as a tipstaff and was attending the jurors. Appellant's counsel, when told, declined to file a motion, but the trial court declared a mistrial. Appellant filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus alleging that a retrial would constitute double jeopardy. The trial court denied the petition, and appellant sought review. The court determined that Rule 1118 abolished a motion to withdraw a juror, but did not change the sua sponte declaration of mistrial by the trial court. In affirming the conviction, the court held that the trial court had the authority to abort a trial when in its sound discretion it found that either manifest necessity or the ends of public justice required the declaration of a mistrial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for murder in the first degree and held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in declaring a mistrial without a motion by appellant's counsel.

 

64 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Pope


No. 392, Jan. T., 1973


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


455 Pa. 384; 317 A.2d 887; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 642


January 11, 1974, Argued  
March 25, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, affirming order of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, Jan. T., 1970, No. 786, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Phillip Pope.



DISPOSITION: Orders of Superior Court and Court of Common Pleas reversed and record remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted the state's petition for allowance to appeal from the order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the trial court's order that sustained defendant's demurrer based on its determination that 18 P.S. § 6108 (1973) proscribed only the carrying of rifles with barrels measuring less than 15 inches.


OVERVIEW: The state sought review of the lower court's judgment affirming the trial court's determination that 18 P.S. § 6108 (1973) proscribed only the carrying of rifles with barrels measuring less than 15 inches. The court reversed the judgment of the lower court and held that as evidenced by its explicit language, the clear intent of § 6108, which was the 1968 amendment to 18 P.S. § 6102 (1973), was that in described circumstances the carrying of any firearm, shotgun, or rifle, constituted a criminal act. The court stated that the trial court's reading of the statute was too narrow and impermissibly rendered the words "rifle" and "shotgun" in the 1968 amendment superfluous, and that its holding that the unlicensed carrying of a rifle with a 22-inch barrel was not a crime was error. The court held that the indictment notified defendant of the charge against him, and that the common-sense meaning of those words was that he was accused of illegally carrying a weapon, a rifle. The court further held that the state's proof was consistent with the common sense reading of the language of the indictment and that the defense was clearly directed to the alleged conduct.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders of the lower court. The court held that defendant was adequately notified that he was indicted for the criminal act of carrying a weapon, a rifle, and that his defense was clearly directed to such conduct, not to some different offense at a different time or place. The court also concluded that defendant was not misled by the indictment, and that the record did not substantiate a claim of prejudice.

Simco Stores v. Redevelopment Authority of Philadelphia


No. 424, Jan. T., 1973


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


455 Pa. 438; 317 A.2d 610; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 648


September 25, 1973, Argued  
March 25, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of Commonwealth Court, No. 553 C.D. 1972, affirming judgment of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, May T., 1970, No. 3965, in case of Simco Stores, 901 Market Street Co., Inc. d/b/a Dial Shoe Co., Arthur S. Kaufman, Herbert Somekh, Charles Blum, Merchants National Properties, Inc., Tappin's Inc., and Hollander & Fleischman, Inc. v. The Redevelopment Authority of the City of Philadelphia.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners challenged the decision of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) affirming the judgment of the lower court, which dismissed appellants' preliminary objections to the declaration of taking of appellee, Redevelopment Authority of the City of Philadelphia.


OVERVIEW: Appellee, Redevelopment Authority of the City of Philadelphia (authority), condemned the property of appellant landowners which had been certified as blighted by the City Planning Commission (commission). The court affirmed the decisions of the lower courts. It found that the trial court was not required to make a finding on whether the area was in fact blighted; that it was only required to allow appellant to present testimony to prove the certification of blight was arbitrary and capricious, but not to substitute its discretion for the discretion of the commission. The court found appellants failed to prove the arbitrariness of the certification because they provided only the unsupported testimony of one witness who was not familiar with all the conditions which are statutorily required to be used to determine the existence of blight. It also held the balance of appellants' contentions could not be raised by preliminary injunction because the scope of preliminary injunctions in this circumstance was limited.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the lower courts' judgments which denied appellant landowners' objections to the taking of their property because the reviewing court was not required to determine area was blighted, only whether certification was arbitrary and capricious, and appellants failed to prove the certification was arbitrary.

 

66 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Olan Mills, Inc.


No. 8, May T., 1973


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


456 Pa. 78; 317 A.2d 592; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 502


May 22, 1973, Argued  
March 25, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order and judgment of Commonwealth Court, No. 349T.D. 1970, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Olan Mills Inc. of Ohio.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer challenged the order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which refused to strike a use tax imposed on appellant by appellee commonwealth under 72 P.S. § 3403-201 for camera equipment and film purchased in the commonwealth and used to manufacture personal property.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayer produced custom-made portraits. Appellant took photos in appellee commonwealth, but all processing of the photos took place in another state. Under 72 P.S. § 3403-201, appellee assessed a use tax against appellant on camera equipment and film purchased and used in the commonwealth. The lower court dismissed the appeal brought by appellant regarding the use tax assessed. On appeal, the court agreed that the making of a custom-made portrait constituted manufacturing within 72 P.S. § 3403-2(c) and that the cameras and film were used in one of the operations of the manufacture of personal property which was excluded from the use tax by 72 P.S. § 3403-2(n)(4)(c). The court held that the statute was clear that the cameras and film were excluded from use tax, and there was no basis for the lower court's finding that appellant could still be taxed because the other steps in the manufacturing process took place outside of the commonwealth. The judgment of the trial court was reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and prohibited appellee commonwealth from imposing a use tax on the camera equipment and film of appellant taxpayer because the items were excluded from the use tax as they were used in the manufacturing of personal property. The fact that most of the manufacturing took place outside of the commonwealth did not cause the excluded items to become taxable.

Kasula Estate


No. 79, March T., 1973


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


456 Pa. 62; 318 A.2d 338; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 501


September 24, 1973, Argued  
March 25, 1974, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused April 29, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas, Orphans' Court Division, of Allegheny County, No. 4752 of 1971, in re estate of Mihaly Kasula, deceased.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant purported heirs sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed their petition to set aside a decree of distribution entered six years after the original partial distribution of decedent's estate because no evidence of entitlement had been produced. The partial distribution had paid part of the estate to the state treasury without escheat under 1953 Pa. Laws 674 (repealed).


OVERVIEW: Decedent died intestate in the Soviet Union, and his estate's only asset was a pension fund in Pennsylvania. Letters of administration were granted to decedent's grandson, and a partial distribution of the estate was made under 1953 Pa. Laws 674 (repealed), with a portion of the estate to be held by the state treasury without escheat until the identity of other heirs could be determined. Six years passed without evidence of entitlement, and the final distribution was made. Appellant purported heirs then filed a petition to set aside the final distribution, which the trial court dismissed. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that foreign heirs were subject to the same burden of proof of kinship as American claimants, which was proof both that heirs at law existed and that claimants were those heirs at law. The court held that the power of attorney signed by appellants, which was the only evidence offered, was a mere assertion of kinship, not proof. The court held that appellants failed to establish by clear, precise, and definite proof that decedent had children in the Soviet Union and that appellants were decedent's heirs.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the petition filed by appellant purported heirs to set aside the final distribution of decedent's estate because appellants failed to prove that heirs at law existed and that they were those qualified statutory heirs.

 

68 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Contakos


No. 2, March T., 1974


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


455 Pa. 136; 314 A.2d 259; 1974 Pa. LEXIS 612


October 1, 1973, Argued  
January 24, 1974, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, April T., 1972, No. 211, affirming judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County, April T., 1970, No. 60, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Samuel C. Contakos.



DISPOSITION: Order of Superior Court and judgment reversed, and a new trial ordered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a judgment of the trial court excluding the testimony of an alibi witness and convicting appellant of sodomy.


OVERVIEW: After the trial court excluded the testimony of appellant's alibi witness because of appellant's failure to give the commonwealth timely notice of the witness' testimony, appellant was convicted of sodomy. The superior court affirmed the order; however, the court reversed and remanded the order of the superior court holding that the rule which required notice of the testimony to the commonwealth, Pa. R. Crim. P. 312, was unconstitutional, as violative of the Due Process Clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court further held that Rule 312 was unconstitutional because it did not give reciprocal discovery rights to appellant, and such one-sided discovery was fundamentally unfair to appellant's defense as well as violative of his due process rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded a judgment of the superior court, which affirmed a judgment of the trial court excluding the testimony of an alibi witness and convicting appellant of sodomy. The rule of procedure under which the alibi testimony was excluded was unconstitutional.

 

69 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Uniontown Area Sch. Dist. v. Pa. Human Rels. Comm'n


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


455 Pa. 52; 313 A.2d 156; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 415


Argued March 12, 1973  
December 4, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals, Nos. 55, 58 and 59, March T., 1973, from orders of Commonwealth Court, Nos. 744, 769 and 931 C.D. 1971, in cases of Uniontown Area School District v. Pennsylvania Human Relations Commission; New Castle Area School District v. Same; New Kensington-Arnold School District v. Same. Orders of Commonwealth Court affirmed.

Same cases in Commonwealth Court: 6 Pa. Commonwealth Ct. 281.

Appeal by school districts to Commonwealth Court from orders of Pennsylvania Human Relations Commission.

Order entered affirming orders of Commission in part and remanding for further proceedings, opinion by WILKINSON, J., dissenting opinion by MENCER, J. School districts appealed.



DISPOSITION: The orders of the Commonwealth Court are affirmed. 29 



29 Although the appellant-districts have not raised the issue, we observe that there is some question whether it can be said that the second precondition to the validity of a Legislative rule -- "issued pursuant to proper procedure", see Davis, supra, at 299 -- is fairly met here. The Commission's definition of a segregated school, notwithstanding its having been labeled a "finding of fact" in the final orders issued by the Commission, is clearly a substantive rule of law; we have so regarded it in this opinion.

Prior to the effective date of the new Commonwealth Documents Law, Act of July 31, 1968, P.L. 769, No. 240, 45 P.S. § 1101 et seq. (Supp. 1973-74), the rules and regulations of the Commission were required by section 21 of the Administrative Agency Law, Act of June 4, 1945, as amended, 71 P.S. § 1710.21, to be placed on file with the Department of State for the examination of interested citizens. The Commission's definition of de facto segregation does not appear to have been published as a rule or regulation; certainly it was not among those existing rules and regulations brought over into the Pennsylvania Code under the Commonwealth Documents Law. Under that new Law, an agency preparing to adopt a rule must give public notice of its intention, 45 P.S. § 1201, must "review and consider any written comments submitted", 45 P.S. § 1202, and "may hold such public hearings as seem appropriate". Although 45 P.S. § 1204 does exempt certain types of regulations from this process, legislative (as contrasted with interpretative) rules are not excepted.

In NLRB v. Wyman-Gordon Co., 394 U.S. 759, 22 L. Ed. 2d 709 (1969), six Justices of the Supreme Court of the United States indicated that the parallel provisions of the federal Administrative Procedure Act were mandatory and that federal agencies could not bypass the legislatively specified procedures for policy formulation (procedures designed to bring a broad spectrum of opinion to bear) in favor of a procedure of developing policy by adjudicatory proceedings.

In exercising a power legislative in nature, we think it necessary to comply fully with legislatively-prescribed procedures. See L. L. Jaffe, Judicial Control of Administrative Action 566 (1965). Whether or not the Commission's definition, formulated in an administrative procedure of unspecified nature, adopted without the broad public notice required under the Documents Law, and unpublished as a rule or regulation so complies are questions which we do not now pass upon and which must await proper presentation.

 [***2]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school districts sought review of the judgment of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the finding of appellee state human relations commission ordering appellants to develop and submit plans to combat racial imbalance, under a definition of racial imbalance devised by appellee, pursuant to the Human Relations Act, 43 P.S. § 955 (Supp. 1973-74).


OVERVIEW: Appellee state human relations commission found appellant school districts in violation of the Human Relations Act (Act), 43 P.S. § 955 (Supp. 1973-74), and ordered appellants to develop and submit plans to eliminate racial imbalance as defined by appellee using a mathematical formula. The lower court affirmed the finding and appellants sought review. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court first noted that appellee was empowered to correct de facto segregation occurring in state public schools and that the Act itself did not provide a definition for racial imbalance. Appellee had the power to adopt its definition of racial imbalance. The court considered definitions used in other contexts, including federal law, state law, and other authorities, and concluded that appellee's definition was without support in the materials examined. However, the Act empowered appellee to supply the definition because appellee was permitted to do more than merely interpret the Act. Thus, the definition was within appellee's authority and was neither arbitrary nor unreasonable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court, which affirmed appellee state human relations commission's definition of racial imbalance as applied to appellant school districts because, although the definition did not have support in analogous materials, appellee was empowered by its enabling statute to adopt the definition.

 

70 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Di Girolamo v. Apanavage


No. 100, Jan. T., 1973


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


454 Pa. 557; 312 A.2d 382; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 795


April 24, 1973, Argued  
December 4, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, Oct. T., 1972, No. 26, affirming order of Court of Common Pleas of Northampton County, Oct. T., 1970, No. 303, in case of Lawrence DiGirolamo and Sarah DiGirolamo Apanavage v. Anthony R. Apanavage.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, injured wife and her father, sought review of an order from the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed an order sustaining appellee husband's preliminary objections and dismissed the complaint in appellants' negligence action against appellee. Appellants contended that the action could have been maintained against appellee for personal injuries caused by a tort committed prior to marriage.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, injured wife and her father, brought an action against appellee husband for damages arising from an automobile accident in which appellee was the driver of the automobile. At the time of the accident, appellant injured wife and appellee were not married. The appellate court affirmed the order from the trial court sustaining appellee's preliminary objections. The lower court found that appellant injured wife lacked the capacity to sue him because she was appellee's wife, and accordingly, the complaint was dismissed. On appeal, the court affirmed the order from the appellate court. The court found that appellant injured wife could not sue appellee because appellee was protected by interspousal immunity pursuant to 48 P.S. § 111. The court determined that an unliquidated tort claim was not "separate property" within the meaning of § 111, and appellee was therefore protected by interspousal immunity. The court determined that "separate property" as defined in § 111 was lesser in scope than an all-encompassing definition of property.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order which sustained appellee husband's preliminary objections and which dismissed the complaint brought by appellants, injured wife and her father. The court found that appellee was protected by interspousal immunity from the action for damages resulting from an automobile accident, and it found that unliquidated tort damages were not separate property.

 

71 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Cherney


No. 140, March T., 1973


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


454 Pa. 285; 312 A.2d 38; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 759


September 24, 1973, Submitted  
November 26, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Clarion County, Feb. T., 1972, No. 14, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Charles R. R. Cherney.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed and record remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Prosecution sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Clarion County (Pennsylvania), granting appellee's motion to quash a summary conviction proceeding and ruling that Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 75, §§ 1002(b)(8) and 618(b)(2) were unconstitutional as unlawful delegations of legislative power.


OVERVIEW: Appellee was found guilty of speeding and ordered to pay a fine. The prosecution appealed an order from the appellate court granting appellee's motion to quash the summary conviction and ruling Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 75, §§ 1002(b)(8) and 618(b)(2) unconstitutional as unlawful delegations of legislative power. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded for further proceedings. The court explained the constitutionality of Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 75, § 618(b)(2) was not a justiciable issue before the court because appellee had not been affected by the operation of this statute, which dealt with the suspension of an operator's license. As for Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 75, § 1002(b)(8), the court determined that it was a conferring of power on the Secretary of Highways in connection with the execution of the speed limit law. This grant of authority was valid because it guided and restrained the exercise of the administrative functions delegated to the secretary. The legislature was attempting to promote safety on the highways, and it provided guidance on what it believed to be safe speeds in certain areas. The statute also mandated the secretary to give notice of the allowable speed increase.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the order from the appellate court granting appellee's motion to quash the summary conviction. The constitutionality of one statute was not a justiciable issue before the court because appellee had not been affected by its operation, and the other statute was a valid grant of authority to the Secretary of Highways to set speed limits guided and restrained by the legislature.

Philadelphia Life Ins. Co. v. Commonwealth


No. 28, May T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


454 Pa. 157; 309 A.2d 811; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 742


May 23, 1972, Argued  
October 4, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, sitting as Commonwealth Court, No. 331 Commonwealth Docket 1961, No. 2487, in re Philadelphia Life Insurance Co. v. Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, Charles M. Dougherty, Secretary of Revenue, Thomas Z. Minehart, Auditor General, Anne X. Alpern, Attorney General and their Deputies, Agents and Successors in Office.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a decree of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), that the Gross Premiums Tax Act of 1961, Pa. P. L. 33, § 2, was not unconstitutionally retroactive, as applied to renewals of non-participating policies written prior to 1961.


OVERVIEW: Appellant instituted an equitable action, seeking to restrain the collection of the Gross Premiums Tax Act of 1961 (Act), Pa. P.L. 33, § 2, alleging that it violated the due process clauses of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and Pa. Const. art. I, § 9, in that it was unconstitutionally retroactive, as applied to renewal premiums appellant received on nonparticipating policies written prior to 1961, the effective date of the tax. On appeal, the court held that on its face the law was constitutional, because the retroactive effect of the tax covered a period of one month, which was within the bounds of Pennsylvania constitutional law and that the tax was constitutional as applied. Appellant did not sustain the burden of overcoming the presumption of the validity of tax legislation. A tax was not considered retroactive merely because its application depended upon events which pre-dated the tax.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decree of the lower court, holding that the statute was constitutional on its face and as applied to renewal premiums received by insurance companies from non-participating life insurance policies.

Commonwealth v. Wayman


No. 218, Jan. T., 1973


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


454 Pa. 79; 309 A.2d 784; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 736


April 25, 1973, Submitted  
October 3, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, Oct. T., 1971, Nos. 1369 to 1375, inclusive, affirming judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Lackawanna County, May T., 1967, Nos. 7 (a, b, c, d, & e) and 10, Oct. T., 1967, No. 20B, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. George Wayman.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence in No. 7e reversed and new trial granted; records in Nos. 7a, 7b, 7c, 7d, 10 and 20B remanded to trial court for filing of post-trial motions, with instructions to hold an appropriate evidentiary hearing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On grant of allocatur on appeal allowed as if timely filed, appellant challenged the decision of the Pennsylvania Superior Court, which affirmed the decision of the trial court, which convicted appellant of second degree burglary following a jury trial, denied his post-trial motions, and convicted him of five other burglary charges following his guilty plea.


OVERVIEW: After having been detained and interrogated for 24 hours, appellant, a 19-year-old with no prior police contact, confessed to his complicity in seven burglaries. Immediately thereafter, he was preliminarily arraigned. On grant of allocatur, appellant challenged the decision of superior court, which affirmed the decision of the trial court, which convicted him of a second degree burglary charge, denied his post-trial motions, and convicted him of five other burglary charges following his guilty pleas. On appeal, the court reversed and granted a new trial, holding that appellant had not waived error in the admission of his confession, although it was not included in his motion to suppress, because it was based on a subsequently announced legal principle. The court held that the unjustified 24-hour delay between appellant's arrest and arraignment was unnecessary and thus violated Pa. R. Crim. P. 116(a) (1965). His confession was reasonably related to the delay, invalid, and inadmissible. The court remanded for post-trial motions and a hearing as to whether appellant's guilty pleas to the six remaining burglary charges were primarily motivated by the illegally obtained confession.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and granted a new trial, holding that the unjustified 24-hour delay between appellant's arrest and arraignment violated Pa. R. Crim. P. 116(a) (1965), and his confession at the end of the delay was reasonably related to the delay, invalid, and inadmissible. The court remanded for a hearing on whether appellant's guilty pleas to six burglary charges were primarily motivated by the illegally obtained confession.

McIlvaine v. Pa. State Police


No. 151, Jan. T., 1973


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


454 Pa. 129; 309 A.2d 801; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 740; 6 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 768; 6 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P8860


April 26, 1973, Argued  
October 3, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Commonwealth Court, No. 282 C.D. 1971, in case of Joseph F. McIlvaine v. Pennsylvania State Police.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed. *



* Reporter's Note: On March 25, 1974, the Supreme Court of the United States dismissed an appeal from this judgment for want of a substantial federal question.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant police officer sought to overturn the order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which upheld his involuntary retirement from appellee employer at age 60, pursuant to 71 P.S. § 65(d), and rejected appellant's claim in mandamus.


OVERVIEW: Appellant police officer was forced into retirement by appellee employer at age 60 solely because of his age pursuant to 71 P.S. § 65(d). Appellant brought a declaratory judgment action in the lower court claiming that his retirement violated his constitutional rights under both the federal and state constitutions, and his rights under the Human Relations Act, 43 P.S. 952(b), but the lower court ruled that mandamus was the proper means of challenging appellee's actions. Appellant then brought an action in mandamus and, while appellant's action was pending, the court issued an opinion which would have required that appellant's claim be brought as a declaratory judgment action. Therefore, in the interests of fairness, the court treated appellant's action as if it were a declaratory judgment action. Thereupon the court affirmed the order of the lower court that upheld appellee's involuntary retirement of appellant on the basis of the opinion written by the lower court.


OUTCOME: The court treated appellant police officer's mandamus action as though it were a declaratory judgment action and affirmed the order of the lower court, holding that the involuntary retirement of appellant, solely on grounds of age, by appellee employer did not violate appellant's statutory or federal or state constitutional rights.

Commonwealth v. Nat'l Gettysburg Battlefield Tower


No. 365, Jan. T., 1973


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


454 Pa. 193; 311 A.2d 588; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 747; 5 ERC (BNA) 1949; 3 ELR 20876


June 27, 1973, Argued  
October 3, 1973, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused December 5, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of Commonwealth Court, Nos. 120 and 753 C.D. 1972, affirming decree of Court of Common Pleas of Adams County, July T., 1971, No. 2, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, by the Honorable Milton J. Shapp, Governor, and the Honorable J. Shane Creamer, Attorney General and as trustee for the people of the Commonwealth v. National Gettysburg Battlefield Tower, Inc. et al.



DISPOSITION: Order of Commonwealth Court affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal from an order of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) affirming a decree by the Court of Common Pleas of Adams County finding that appellant, the Commonwealth, failed to show that the Gettysburg environment would be injured by appellee's construction of an observation tower.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, the Commonwealth, sued to enjoin construction by appellee of a 307-foot tower near the Gettysburg Battlefield, alleging that the tower would despoil the Gettysburg environment. The lower courts held that appellant failed to carry its burden of proof. On appeal, the Supreme Court held that Pa. Const. art. I, § 27 was not self-executing and merely granted appellant the power to protect the natural, scenic, historic, and esthetic values of its environment. If the amendment were self-executing, action taken under it would pose serious equal protection and due process concerns. Accordingly, before the environmental protection amendment could be made effective, supplemental legislation would be required to define the values that the amendment sought to protect and to establish procedures by which the use of private property could be regulated to protect those values.


OUTCOME: Affirmed order allowing appellees to build observation tower near Gettysburg battlefield where Pa. Const. art. I, § 27 merely gave appellant power to protect natural, scenic, historic, and esthetic values of environment; Pa. Const. art. I, § 27 is not self-executing.

Johnson v. Pa. Hous. Fin. Agency


No. 347, Jan. T., 1973


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


453 Pa. 329; 309 A.2d 528; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 681


May 3, 1973, Argued  
September 19, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, No. 139, C.D. 1973, in re Joyce Johnson v. Pennsylvania Housing Finance Agency, John Chately, Jr., William H. Wilcox, Robert R. LaVelle, Leon E. Hickman, James A. Lineberger, Richard J. Fox, John M. O'Brien, and John McCoy.



DISPOSITION: Judgment on pleadings entered for defendant-appellants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, housing finance agency and individuals, sought review of the judgment of the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, in appellee taxpayer's suit challenging the constitutionality of the Housing Finance Agency Law, 35 P.S. § 1680.101 et seq. (Supp. 1973).


OVERVIEW: Appellee taxpayer challenged the Housing Finance Agency Law (Act), 35 P.S. § 1680.101 et seq. (Supp. 1973). The court assumed plenary jurisdiction and found the Act constitutional. The Act served a public purpose because it administered housing programs for persons in need. The Act did not delegate legislative power because the agency involved was a governmental entity and the Act's guidelines did not permit broad or unusual discretion. The Act did not authorize a debt or pledge of credit by Pennsylvania because by its own terms, bonds and notes issued under its auspices were not deemed debts or pledges. The Act did not constitute an irrevocable grant of special privileges and immunities because the challenged provision was simply an amplification of the prohibition against the impairment of contracts. The Act did not provide for a prohibited exemption from taxation because bonds issued under the Act were not the sort of property contemplated by the prohibition against exemption. The Act did not create a special or local law because it had no geographical distinction and was intended to apply statewide. The Act's title was proper as it gave adequate notice as to its subject.


OUTCOME: The court upheld a state housing finance agency law because the law served a public purpose, was not an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power, did not authorize a debt or pledge of credit by the state, did not constitute an irrevocable grant of privileges and immunities, did not provide for a prohibited exemption from taxation, did not create a special or local law, and did not fail, in its title, to state the full subject of the law.

Akron v. Pennsylvania Public Utility Com.


No. 20, May T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


453 Pa. 554; 310 A.2d 271; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 706


November 21, 1972, Argued  
September 19, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Commonwealth Court, No. 262A, Transfer Docket 1970 (original jurisdiction initially commenced in Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, No. 2823, Equity Docket No. 367, Commonwealth Docket 1967), in case of Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission v. Borough of Akron.



DISPOSITION: Decree vacated and matter remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant utility commission challenged a judgment of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a judgment finding that appellant did not have power to order appellee municipality to extend appellee's services to customers located beyond appellee's certificated area.


OVERVIEW: Appellee municipality brought an action against appellant utility commission seeking to enjoin appellant from ordering appellee to extend utility services to customers located beyond appellee's certificated area. The trial court awarded a preliminary injunction to appellee. The intermediate appellate court granted appellee's motion for judgment on the pleadings and appellant sought review. The court vacated the judgment, holding that the trial court was not entitled to grant the injunction, because the injunction was, in essence a writ of prohibition. Courts of common pleas were not entitled to award writs of prohibition, and appellee was not entitled to a writ of prohibition, because appellee had an alternative statutory remedy available under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 66, § 1441. The court remanded the case with instructions to dismiss appellee's complaint in equity.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment and remanded with direction to dismiss appellee municipality's complaint in equity, holding that appellee was not entitled to the judgment because an alternative statutory remedy was available to appellee against appellant utility commission.
