Allegheny Airlines v. Phila.


No. 288, Jan. T., 1973


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


453 Pa. 181; 309 A.2d 157; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 670


April 30, 1973, Argued  
September 5, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Commonwealth Court, No. 830, C.D. 1972, dismissing decree of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, June T., 1972, No. 3604, in case of Allegheny Airlines, Inc. et al. v. City of Philadelphia.



DISPOSITION: Decree reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant airlines sought review of the order form the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which dismissed appellants' appeal for want of jurisdiction. Appellants asserted that the head tax imposed by appellee city was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellee city enacted an ordinance which provided for the imposition of a head tax on all passengers arriving or departing from appellee's airports and required appellant airlines to be liable for the tax. Appellants challenged the validity of the tax, and the trial court sustained the departure tax but struck down the arrival tax as an unconstitutional burden on interstate commerce. Subsequently appellee enacted an ordinance which imposed a head tax on certain passengers departing from the airport and required appellants to collect the tax with the price of the ticket. On stipulated appeal of both laws the lower court determined that it did not have jurisdiction because the trial court had never litigated the validity of the second ordinance. On review the court assumed plenary jurisdiction and reversed the decree of the trial court. Pub. L. 93-44, Title XI, § 1113(a), 87 Stat. 90 (1973), amended the Federal Aviation Act, 49 U.S.C.S. §§ 1301 et seq., and preempted any state or local intervention in the field of airport head taxes. The taxes did not fall into the limited exception created by the federal statute and were constitutionally impermissible and invalid.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the allowance of a head tax by appellee city on passengers departing on appellant airlines and held that the tax was unconstitutional because federal law preempted the right of appellee to levy any airport head tax.

Alco Parking Corp. v. Pittsburgh


No. 90, March T., 1973


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


453 Pa. 245; 307 A.2d 851; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 414


March 14, 1973, Argued  
July 2, 1973, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused, August 7, 1973, Order Per Curiam, Mr. Justice Eagen Dissenting. *


* Reporter's Note: On January 7, 1974, the Supreme Court of the United States granted a petition for a writ of certiorari. On June 10, 1974, the Supreme Court of the United States reversed and remanded the cause to the Supreme Court of Pennsylvania for further proceedings.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Allegheny County, April T., 1970, No. 1699, and decision of Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, No. 643 C.D. 1971, in re Alco Parking Corporation, Arena Parking, Inc. et al. v. City of Pittsburgh.



DISPOSITION: Order of Commonwealth Court and decree of Allegheny County Court of Common Pleas reversed and case remanded to the Allegheny County Court of Common Pleas.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parking lot operators sought review of judgments of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the decree of the trial court that dismissed appellants' complaint that a parking receipts tax ordinance was a taking of property in violation of due process and otherwise violated the Pennsylvania and United States constitutions.


OVERVIEW: Appellant parking lot operators filed a complaint in equity that challenged appellee city's gross receipts parking tax ordinance. Appellants asserted that the ordinance was so excessive and unreasonable that it amounted to a confiscation of appellants' property without due process of law because appellee was in direct economic competition with appellants. Appellants also claimed that the ordinance was unreasonable because most commercial parking owners were operating at a loss. Appellants also asserted that the ordinance violated Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1 and the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV in that appellee had no reasonable basis for separately classifying appellants' commercial parking operations for the purpose of taxation. The court reversed the dismissal of appellants' complaint. The court held that, while commercial parking lots were a proper subject for local taxation, nevertheless, the gross receipts tax ordinance, coupled with the special competitive circumstances, constituted an unconstitutional taking of private property without due process of law in violation of U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decree dismissing appellant parking lot operators' complaint and remanded. The court held that commercial parking lots were a proper subject for local taxation but that the gross receipts tax, coupled with competition from parking lots operated by appellee city, constituted an unconstitutional taking of private property without due process of law.

Commonwealth ex rel. Specter v. Moak


No. 312, Jan. T., 1973


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


452 Pa. 482; 307 A.2d 884; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 468


April 30, 1973, Argued  
July 2, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, March T., 1973, No. 2124, in re Commonwealth of Pennsylvania ex rel. District Attorney Arlen Specter and ex rel. Eugene V. Allessandroni, Julian Brereton, Walter W. Cohen, Inez Lundy, William J. Stevens, Jr., Charles W. Sweeney and Esther R. Sylvester, individually and as Assistant District Attorneys v. Lennox L. Moak, Finance Director of the City of Philadelphia.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant assistant district attorneys sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), dismissing their complaint in mandamus seeking an order declaring appellants the rightful holders of positions as assistant district attorneys and requiring appellee finance director to compensate them.


OVERVIEW: Appellant assistant district attorneys became candidates for elective judgeships, so appellee finance director withheld their pay under Philadelphia, Pa., Home Rule Charter § 10-107(5), which provided that no city employee, except an elected officer running for re-election, was permitted to run for office without first resigning his employment. Appellants filed a complaint in mandamus seeking an order declaring that they were rightful holders of positions as assistant district attorneys and requiring appellee to compensate them. The trial court dismissed appellants' complaint, and the court affirmed. The court concluded that appellants were city employees and were subject to § 10-107(5). The court held that the prohibition on political candidacy was a constitutionally permissible restriction on the political activity of city employees. The court concluded that the city had a compelling governmental interest eliminating neglect of official duties and undue influence and intimidation of employees under a candidate's supervision that justified requiring city employees to resign before becoming. Moreover, the approach adopted in was the least drastic means to achieve the desired end.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order dismissing appellant assistant district attorneys' complaint in mandamus because appellants were city employees and subject to the requirement that city employees resign before becoming candidates for public office. The court found that the requirement being a constitutionally permissible restriction on the political activity of city employees justified by a compelling governmental interest.

Commonwealth v. Bighum


No. 405, Jan. T., 1971


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


452 Pa. 554; 307 A.2d 255; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 480


April 27, 1972, Argued  
July 2, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia, Trial Division, May T., 1968, No. 1225, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Hubert Bighum.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence as modified affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of first degree murder and imposed the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder and sentenced to death. On appeal, the court affirmed, subject to modification of the sentence to life imprisonment. The trial court had jurisdiction because the victim's death occurred and a number of wounds were inflicted within the city. The trial judge did not err in failing to instruct the jury on jurisdiction because the facts overwhelmingly supported jurisdiction. The trial court's refusal to challenge a prospective juror for cause was proper where the juror's reservation about serving was based upon schedule conflicts. The Commonwealth's summation statement that all of its evidence was taken "without a word from the defendant" was not grounds for reversal. Defendant was not coerced into deciding not to take the stand because there was no constitutional violation in impeachment by prior convictions. Finally, as the imposition of the death penalty under statutes such as the one in which the death penalty was imposed upon defendant was violative of U.S. Const. amends. VIII, XIV, it was unnecessary to consider defendant's claim of non-compliance with the Witherspoon standard.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction, subject to modification to life imprisonment, because the trial court had jurisdiction where the victim's death occurred inside the city limits, and defendant's decision not to take the stand based upon his fear of the introduction of prior convictions for impeachment purposes did not violate the Constitution.

Philadelphia Marine Trade Asso. v. International Longshoremen's Asso.


Nos. 128, 165, 175, 176, 177 and 178, Jan. T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


453 Pa. 43; 308 A.2d 98; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 660; 84 L.R.R.M. 2300


November 9, 1972, Argued  
July 2, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from decrees of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, Oct. T., 1971, No. 1769, in case of Philadelphia Marine Trade Association v. International Longshoremen's Association et al.



DISPOSITION: Decrees in appeals Nos. 128, 165, 175, 177 and 178, reversed; decree in appeal No. 176 affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant unions sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which granted appellee trade association a preliminary injunction and issued contempt citations against appellants for violation of a no-strike clause in a collective bargaining agreement.


OVERVIEW: Appellee trade association sought an injunction against appellant unions for violation of a collective bargaining agreement (CBA). The trial court granted the injunctions and held appellants in contempt for failure to comply. On appeal, the court held that appellee's unsuccessful attempt for relief in federal court was not res judicata because a final determination as to the contract's existence was not made. The trial court erred in finding that a valid CBA existed because the contract was not ratified. Although the preliminary injunction was invalid, the legality of the contempt citations was still to be determined. Two of the contempt citations issued for failure to work were in error as there was insufficient evidence to hold appellants in contempt as a consequence of the walkouts, under 43 P.S. § 206h. The trial court erred in holding certain appellants in contempt because they were not parties to the original action. The trial court did not err in issuing a decree that appellants' refusal to cross an outside union's picket lines was not grounds for contempt, conditioned on appellants' promise of future compliance. Thus, the court affirmed in part, and reversed in part.


OUTCOME: The court reversed in part because the injunction against appellant unions was invalid as the collective bargaining agreement had not been ratified and some of the contempt citations were improper because appellants could not be held responsible for their members' actions under the circumstances. The trial court's decree as to appellant's refusal to cross an outside picket line was proper, where the decree did not hold appellants in contempt.

Adler v. Montefiore Hospital Asso.


No. 52, March T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


453 Pa. 60; 311 A.2d 634; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 661


March 16, 1972, Argued  
July 2, 1973, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused August 13, 1973. *


* Reporter's Note: On January 7, 1974, the Supreme Court of the United States denied a petition for a writ of certiorari.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Allegheny County, July T., 1970, No. 1501, in case of Lawrence N. Adler v. The Montefiore Hospital Association of Western Pennsylvania.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant physician sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which entered a final decree on the chancellor's denial of appellant's request that appellee hospital be enjoined from denying him access to appellee's cardiac catheterization laboratory.


OVERVIEW: Appellant physician brought a suit to enjoin appellee hospital from denying him the right to perform certain procedures in appellee's laboratory. After trial, the chancellor denied the requested relief. A court en banc dismissed appellant's exceptions and entered a final decree. On appeal, the court affirmed. Appellee's policy of permitting only its full-time laboratory director to perform certain procedures was reasonable and did not violate due process. No patient rights were infringed because there was no right that would permit a patient to insist on his own doctor's performance of the procedures conducted in a laboratory equipped and staffed by appellee. Appellant was not denied equal protection of the law because appellee's policies for the laboratory reasonably treated cardiologists, such as appellant, and surgeons differently. Appellant was not entitled to a hearing under U.S. Const. amend. XIV because his claim of denial of procedural due process was based on nothing more than a unilateral expectation. Similarly, appellant was not entitled to a hearing under appellee's by-laws because exclusion from the laboratory was not a triggering event for a hearing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed because appellee hospital's refusal to permit appellant physician from performing certain procedures in appellee's laboratory did not violate the Fourteenth Amendment's proscription against equal protection or due process where the policy was reasonable and appellant was not due a hearing based upon his unilateral expectation. Appellant also was not entitled to a hearing pursuant to appellee's by-laws based on the facts.

 

86 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


McMullan v. Wohlgemuth


No. 77, May T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


453 Pa. 147; 308 A.2d 888; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 669; 1 Media L. Rep. 2333


April 28, 1972, Argued  
July 2, 1973, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused July 30, 1973. *


* Reporter's Note: On March 18, 1974, the Supreme Court of the United States dismissed the appeal in this case "for want of a federal substantial question." McMullan v. Wohlgemuth, 415 U.S. 970, 94 S. Ct. 1647 (1974).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of Commonwealth Court, No. 640 C.D. 1971, in re John McMullan, James Steele and Philadelphia Newspapers, Inc. v. Helene Wohlgemuth, Secretary of Welfare of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, and Clarence P. Jenkins, Executive Director of Philadelphia County Board of Public Assistance, and the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, state officials, sought review of the judgment of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which ordered appellants to grant access to department information regarding welfare recipients to appellees, a newspaper and its editor.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, state officials, were ordered by the trial court to provide appellees, a newspaper and its editor, access to information on welfare recipients. On appeal, the court reversed the order. Pursuant to 62 P.S. §§ 404 and 425, the inquirer of such information had to be an adult resident and had to supply the names of recipients subject to the inquiry. Appellee newspaper, a corporation, was not an "adult resident" and appellee editor was acting on its behalf. Appellees did not assert any non-commercial or non-political use, as required by the statute, and its provision of the telephone book failed to satisfy the requirement regarding the production of the recipients' names. The information was subject to an exception of the Right to Know Act, 65 P.S. §§ 66.1 et seq., that barred access to public records where the publication was prohibited, restricted or forbidden by statute. Access to the information was not compelled by either U.S. Const. amend. I or Pa. Const. art. I, § 7, because the preponderant interest of the Commonwealth in protecting the privacy of its citizens outweighed the non-absolute right of the press to gather news by compelling the material it sought.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court, holding that information on welfare recipients was protected from disclosure by statute, because the records were exempt from disclosure by an exception to access by the Right to Know Act. The Commonwealth's interest in protecting the privacy of its citizens outweighed the non-absolute right of the press to gather news; therefore, disclosure was not compelled by the First Amendment.

In re Articles of Incorporation of Defender Asso.


No. 102, Jan. T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


453 Pa. 353; 307 A.2d 906; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 683


January 18, 1972, Argued  
July 2, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, Oct. T., 1970, No. 989, affirming order of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, June T., 1930, No. 10005, in the matter of Amendments to the Articles of Incorporation of the Defender Association of Philadelphia.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed. *



* Reporter's Note: On December 3, 1973, the Supreme Court of the United States denied a petition for a writ of certiorari.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant association members sought review of the order of a Pennsylvania superior court, which affirmed the order of the court of common pleas approving the amendments of appellee Defender Association of Philadelphia to its articles of incorporation.


OVERVIEW: Appellee Defender Association of Philadelphia applied to the court of common pleas pursuant to the Non-Profit Corporation Law, 15 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 7707, for approval of amendments to its articles of incorporation. After considering the objections of appellant association members, the court of common pleas entered its order approving the amendments. The trial court affirmed. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that any potential conflict of interest presented by the fact that the mayor appointed both the commissioner of police and the city solicitor as well as one-third of appellee's board of directors was speculative. The court noted that the bylaws of appellee contained safeguards against the creation of an impermissible conflict of interest.The fact that the mayor appointed members of the board did not give the city control of appellee because it was unlikely that appellee's directors would accept nominees for seats on the board that would act as a rubber stamp for the mayor. The court held that there was sufficient evidence in the record to sustain the lower court's finding that the articles of incorporation, as amended, comported with the requirements of § 7707.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court approving the amendments submitted by appellee Defendant Association of Philadelphia to its articles of incorporation because the conflict of interest appellant association members argued existed was speculative and the articles, as amended, comported with the requirements of the Non-Profit Corporation Law.

Commonwealth v. Davis


No. 9, Jan. T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


452 Pa. 171; 305 A.2d 715; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 434


November 8, 1971, Argued  
May 23, 1973, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued November 21, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County, March T., 1969, Nos. 531, 532, and 533, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Curtis Wilbur Davis.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence reversed and new trial granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which denied his post-trial motions in arrest of judgment and for a new trial. Defendant was adjudged guilty, by a jury, of murder, voluntary manslaughter and two counts of robbery and was sentenced to death on the murder conviction, and from 10 to 20 years' imprisonment on the robbery convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder, voluntary manslaughter, and two counts of robbery, and was sentenced to death on the murder conviction, and from 10 to 20 years on the robbery convictions. Defendant appealed the post-verdict court's denial of his motion for a new trial based on the state's repeated emphasis on "uncontroverted" evidence, which highlighted his failure to testify. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment of sentence and ordered a new trial, because the state's emphasis on defendant's failure to testify violated defendant's right not to testify under U.S. Const. amend. V and Pa. Const. art. I, § 9. The state's comments implied that defendant himself was the only one who could and should have denied the charges against him, and the jury might reasonably have inferred from these statements that his failure to do so was evidence of his guilt. The court held that the use of the word "uncontroverted" in the prosecutor's closing argument also was error, because defendant was not required to affirmatively deny any allegation at all.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's convictions and granted a new trial because the state's emphasis on defendant's failure to testify and its use of the term "uncontroverted" evidence were harmful constitutional error.

Ayala v. Philadelphia Bd. of Public Education


No. 271, Jan. T., 1973


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


453 Pa. 584; 305 A.2d 877; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 708


March 12, 1973, Argued  
May 23, 1973, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused June 25, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of Superior Court, Oct. T., 1972, Nos. 687 and 749, affirming order and judgment of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, Dec. T., 1971, No. 2726, in case of William A. Ayala, Jr., a minor by his father and guardian, William Ayala, and William Ayala, in his own right v. Philadelphia Board of Public Education.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, and record remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged an order from the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), dismissing their negligence suit for personal injury damages against appellee school district because appellee was immune from suit under the doctrine of governmental immunity.


OVERVIEW: Appellants brought a personal injury action in negligence to recover damages against appellee school district when their son's arm was amputated after being caught in a shredding machine during a class at school. Appellants challenged an order dismissing their action because appellees were protected from liability under the doctrine of governmental immunity. The court abolished the doctrine of governmental immunity in Pennsylvania, because the doctrine was out of accord with modern conditions of life. The court held that it had the power to abolish the judicially created doctrine of governmental immunity notwithstanding the legislature's failure to adopt corrective legislation. The court reversed and remanded, holding that local governmental units, municipal corporations and quasi-corporations, were no longer immune from tort liability.


OUTCOME: The court abolished the doctrine of governmental immunity in Pennsylvania, and reversed and remanded, holding that local governmental units, municipal corporations and quasi-corporations, were no longer immune from tort liability.

 

90 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Kates


Nos. 472 and 473, Jan. T., 1971; No. 368, Jan. T., 1971


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


452 Pa. 102; 305 A.2d 701; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 427


January 14, 1972, Argued  
May 8, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from judgment of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, Aug. T., 1969, Nos. 213 and 214, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Daisey Kates.

Appeal from order of Superior Court, Oct. T., 1970, No. 679, affirming judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, June T., 1969, No. 54, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Cleo McClellan.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated appeal, two appellants challenged the judgments of the lower courts (Pennsylvania), which revoked their probation prior to the trial for the criminal charges that were based on the same activities that gave rise to the alleged probation violation. One appellant sought a writ of prohibition to postpone a probation revocation hearing until after a trial on the substantial criminal charge.


OVERVIEW: In a consolidated appeal, two appellants challenged judgments revoking their probation following arrests, but prior to trial on the charges, and one appellant sought a writ of prohibition to require that the revocation hearing be postponed until trial of the charges. The court affirmed the judgments and denied the writ, holding held that there were no statutory or constitutional prohibitions against conducting the hearings prior to the trial. Because the purpose of the hearing was to determine whether the probation had been an effective vehicle to accomplish rehabilitation and a sufficient deterrent against future antisocial conduct, a prompt hearing was necessary and evidentiary standards were relaxed. Due process required a revocation hearing, but the exclusionary rule was inapplicable. The court held, as to the instant cases, a statement by one appellant was admissible and because it was not raised during the hearing, a claim that the statement was coerced was barred. In the other appellant's hearing, it was not required that the victim appear in court where there was eyewitness testimony of an assault.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments revoking probation and denied the writ of prohibition, holding that there were no statutory or constitutional prohibitions against conducting a probation violation hearing prior to the trial of the subsequent offense. Due process required a hearing, but evidentiary standards were relaxed therefore admissions of criminal conduct was properly considered and the appearance of the victim was not required.

Commonwealth v. Goldsmith


No. 239, Jan. T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


452 Pa. 22; 304 A.2d 478; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 418


April 20, 1972, Argued  
May 4, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, May T., 1957, No. 577, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Walter Goldsmith.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence reversed and new trial ordered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a direct appeal of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which convicted defendant of murder in the first degree and sentenced defendant to life imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder in the first degree and sentenced to life imprisonment. Defendant subsequently filed a direct appeal. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment of sentence and ordered a new trial. The court ruled defendant was entitled to a new trial since he was unable to effectively and meaningfully exercise his direct appeal rights because he had not been furnished a full transcript or other equivalent picture of what transpired during his trial. The court found that, following a conviction of murder in the first degree, it was the duty of the official stenographer to make, certify, and file of record a typewritten copy of the stenographic notes of trial. In defendant's case, trial counsel made repeated requests but never received a full transcript because substantial and pertinent portions of the notes of testimony were never transcribed due to the stenographer's death. The court found that defendant was entitled to a new trial because specific prejudice or harm would have resulted from not having the entire transcript of testimony as meaningful appellate review was impossible without a complete transcript.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's judgment of sentence and ordered a new trial because defendant could not effectively and meaningfully exercise his direct appeal rights where he had not been furnished a full transcript or other equivalent picture of what transpired during his trial due to the stenographer's death prior to transcribing the trial.

Commonwealth v. Campana


Nos. 205, 206 and 207, Jan. T., 1971, No. 21, Jan. T., 1972, and No. 127, March T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


452 Pa. 233; 304 A.2d 432; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 440


April 30, 1971, and October 1, 1971, Argued  
May 4, 1973, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued September 29, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from orders of Superior Court of Pennsylvania, Oct. T., 1970, No. 251, Oct. T., 1970, No. 410, and April T., 1971, No. 328, affirming the judgments of sentences of Court of Common Pleas of Lycoming County, Sept. T., 1968, No. 151, Court of Common Pleas of Berks County, Sept. T., 1968, No. 255, and Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Allegheny County, Oct. T., 1968, No. 325, and April T., 1969, No. 520, in cases of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Peter Campana; Same v. John Doe, et al.; Same v. Robert Earl King.



DISPOSITION: Judgments of sentences reversed; judgment of sentence at No. 325 Oct. T., 1968, Allegheny County, affirmed. *



* Reporter's Note: On October 9, 1973, the Supreme Court of the United States granted a petition for writ of certiorari. On November 6, 1973, the matter was ordered remanded to the Supreme Court of Pennsylvania, following the vacating of its judgments in these cases, to consider whether its judgments were based on federal or state constitutional grounds or both. On January 24, 1974, the Supreme Court of Pennsylvania filed an Addendum Opinion which stated that the majority of the Court viewed its May 4, 1973 judgments as State law determinations pursuant to its supervisory powers. See Commonwealth v. Campana, 455 Pa. 622, 314 A. 2d 854 (1974).

 [***2]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants in three unrelated cases consolidated for argument sought review of the judgments of the Pennsylvania Superior Court which affirmed the denial of their respective appeals from three trial courts, contending that they were, by virtue of second prosecutions, subject to double jeopardy in violation of U.S. Const. amend. V and XIV.


OVERVIEW: Defendants in three separate cases were charged with summary offenses before trial courts and were then bound over to a grand jury. In all three cases, the defendants were tried twice for crimes which occurred during one period of time. The court reversed all convictions that arose from a second prosecution, holding that the second prosecutions were barred by the double jeopardy clause of U.S. Const. amend. V, as applied to the states by U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court held that the double jeopardy clause required a prosecutor to bring, in a single proceeding, all known charges against a defendant arising from a "single criminal episode." First, the court determined that all charges brought in the two successive prosecutions against each defendant originated from but one criminal episode. Because the record established but a single criminal episode for each defendant, the court held that all charges brought against defendants should have been consolidated in single proceedings. The court affirmed one conviction of one defendant because that charge arose from an "entirely separate occurrence" and sufficient evidence was offered to support the conviction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed all convictions of defendants that arose from a second successive prosecution of the same criminal "episode," but affirmed the conviction of one defendant that arose from an entirely separate occurrence. The court held that all charges that resulted from a criminal "episode" should have been consolidated at one trial and that the second prosecutions violated the double jeopardy clause of the Fifth Amendment.

 

93 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth ex rel. Parrish v. Cliff


No. 160, Jan. T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


451 Pa. 427; 304 A.2d 158; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 545


April 17, 1972, Submitted  
May 4, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County, Nos. 108 to 111, inclusive, Misc. Dkt., No. 19, in case of Commonwealth ex rel. Gary Benedict, Belva Jean Baker, Steven Root and Wilkerson Woods v. Kenneth Cliff, Warden, Lancaster County Prison.



DISPOSITION: Portions of judgment vacated and cases remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmates sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County (Pennsylvania) that denied and dismissed their petition for a writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: Several appellant inmates had been immediately incarcerated after the imposition of a fine as part of sentencing because they could not pay the entire fine immediately in a lump sum payment. Appellants argued that under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, the Equal Protection Clause, a defendant could not be incarcerated for failure to pay a find without an opportunity to pay the fine where immediate compliance was prevented by indigency. The court found that a state was prohibited from committing its citizens for fines without a reasonable opportunity to meet the court's directive consistent with their respective financial situation. The court held that because in appellants' cases there was no determination of immediate ability to meet the mandates imposed, nor was there a showing that a reasonable opportunity had been given to allow the appellants to comply without immediate incarceration, the appellants should have been given the opportunity to establish that they were unable to pay the fine. Upon a showing of indigency, the appellants could make payments in reasonable installments.


OUTCOME: On the denial and dismissal of appellant inmates petition for writ of habeas corpus, the court found that the appellants should be given the opportunity to establish whether they were unable to pay the imposed fines because they were not given such an opportunity immediately after sentencing but before incarceration. Upon proof of indigency, each appellant would be allowed to make installment payments suitable to their financial situation.

Commonwealth v. Riggins


No. 386, Jan. T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


451 Pa. 519; 304 A.2d 473; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 558


November 21, 1972, Argued  
May 4, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, March T., 1969, No. 253, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Louis Riggins.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence reversed and new trial granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which denied defendant's motion to suppress his confession and convicted defendant of first degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of first degree murder and sentenced to life imprisonment. Defendant contended that the trial court improperly refused to suppress an oral and written confession obtained through unlawful and coercive means. Defendant, who was 17 years old at the time of the arrest, had been informed of his Miranda rights enroute to the police administration building. Defendant was questioned at various times throughout the day and eventually confessed after more than 17 hours since the Miranda warnings had passed and defendant had not had any sleep. The court reversed the trial court and held that the confession of defendant was not voluntary. The court also held that the police were constitutionally required to re-advise defendant of his rights under U.S. Const. amends. V and VI prior to the oral confession. The court held that defendant's involuntary confessions, oral and written, should have been suppressed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, which convicted defendant of first degree murder, because the police were constitutionally required to re-advise defendant, who was 17 years old, of his Fifth and Sixth Amendment rights prior to the oral confession,as 17 hours elapsed between appellant's initial Miranda advisement and his oral confession. Therefore, defendant's confession should have been suppressed.

Commonwealth v. Page


No. 95, Jan. T., 1971, and No. 163, March T., 1971


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


451 Pa. 331; 303 A.2d 215; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 533


January 17, 1972, Argued  
March 29, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from order of Court of Common Pleas of Centre County, No. 353 of 1968, and judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Allegheny County, March T., 1968, No. 37, in cases of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Barry Graham Page; Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Benjamin A. King, M.D.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed and judgment of sentence reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant doctor appealed the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of violating § 719 of the anti-abortion statute, 18 P.S. § 4719. The Commonwealth also sought review of the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Centre County (Pennsylvania), which granted defendant mechanic's motion for postconviction relief after he pleaded guilty to violating § 718 of the anti-abortion law, 18 P.S. § 4718.


OVERVIEW: Defendant mechanic pleaded guilty to violating § 718 of the anti-abortion statute, 18 P.S. § 4718, but later filed a petition for relief under the Post Conviction Hearing Act. The commonwealth appealed the order that granted defendant's motion because § 718 was unconstitutional. Defendant doctor was prosecuted and convicted for violating § 719 of the anti-abortion statute, 18 P.S. § 4719, and he appealed the judgment. The court, upon consolidating the two cases for review, reversed the convictions of both defendants. The court held that § 718 and § 719 were unconstitutional as violative of the due process clause of the U.S. Const. amend. XIV because the provisions failed to comply with the permissible scope of state regulation of abortion. The court determined that as the provisions were unconstitutional, the prosecutions of defendants based on these sections could not sustain their convictions.


OUTCOME: The court consolidated the two appeals and held that because the criminal statutes proscribing abortions were unconstitutional as violative of the due process clause, the prosecutions of defendants commenced under these statutes could not sustain their convictions.

Moore v. Jamieson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


451 Pa. 299; 306 A.2d 283; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 530


January 16, 1973, Argued  
March 19, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals, Nos. 245 and 246, Miscellaneous Docket No. 19, from writ of prohibition of Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia, Petition for Assumption of Plenary Jurisdiction and for Writ of Prohibition, in case of Cecil B. Moore v. D. Donald Jamieson, President Judge, Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Joseph Sloane, Chairman, Criminal Justice Committee, and Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia.



DISPOSITION: Writ of prohibition against enforcement of Rule 301 of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia is granted and the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia is restrained from proceeding against petitioner under its provisions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney sought a writ of prohibition against the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania) from the enforcement of Rule <*>301 of the Philadelphia Court of Common Pleas, which prohibited petitioner from entering appearances on behalf of criminal clients because he had an excessive number of cases pending trial. Petitioner claimed the rule was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner sought a writ to prohibit the court from invoking against him a rule which prohibited the entry of appearance on behalf of criminal clients where an attorney had a certain number of criminal cases pending trial within a given amount of time. The court granted the writ. The court first stated that in the interest of judicial economy it would consider the challenge to the rule on the basis that it restricted the rights of criminal defendants to counsel of their choice, even though petitioner's standing to assert this argument was questionable. The court held that the rule was a reasonable infringement on the defendant's rights and the right of attorneys to practice criminal law given the backlog of cases and the state's duty to provide speedy trials and expeditiously dispose of criminal cases. However, while the rule permitted disputes as to the accuracy of the list of pending cases, there was no procedure to challenge the reason for delay, such as the prosecution's failure to be prepared or the failure of witnesses to appear. Because of the lack of standards, the rule was held to be vague and overbroad.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner attorney's writ. The rule was a reasonable infringement on the rights of criminal defendants to the attorney of their choice and the petitioner's right to practice criminal law, given the state's interest in the expeditious disposition of criminal cases. However the rule was vague and overbroad, in that it established no procedure or standards to challenge the reason for delay of the pending cases.

Commonwealth v. Harmar Coal Co.


Nos. 89 and 90, May T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


452 Pa. 77; 306 A.2d 308; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 426; 5 ERC (BNA) 1190; 3 ELR 20336


May 25, 1972, Argued  
March 16, 1973, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused July 23, 1973. *


* Reporter's Note: On February 19, 1974, the Supreme Court of the United States dismissed appeals from this judgment for want of substantial federal questions.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from orders of Commonwealth Court, Nos. 261 and 24 C.D. 1970, in cases of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Harmar Coal Company, and Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Pittsburgh Coal Company.



DISPOSITION: Orders of Commonwealth Court reversed and orders of Sanitary Water Board affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the commonwealth, sought review of judgments of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) which granted the issuance of mine drainage permits under the Clean Streams Law (Law), 35 P.S. § 691.1 et seq. (1972), from the Department of Environmental Resources, formerly the Sanitary Water Board, to appellee coal mining companies in separate proceedings.


OVERVIEW: Appellee coal mining companies separately applied for mine drainage permits under the Clean Streams Law (Law), 35 P.S. § 691.1 et seq. (1972). Appellant, the commonwealth, through the former Sanitary Water Board (Board), now Department of Environmental Resources, issued adjudications and orders that denied both appellees' applications. The trial court reversed the Board's denial of one appellant's application, which appellant appealed to the lower appellate court. The other appellee appealed directly to the lower appellate court. Appellant contended that the Board could have required that each appellee have treated the entire "discharge from the mine" as provided by § 315 of the Law, whether caused from other inactive mines or caused by whatever source. The court reversed the lower appellate court and affirmed the Board's orders. The court held that the Clean Streams Law required an operator of an active mine to have treated the entire discharge from an active mine and a discharge from an adjacent inactive mine necessary to protect the active workings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders that reversed the Sanitary Water Board's refusal to grant permits to appellee coal mining companies. The court held that the Clean Streams Law required an operator of an active mine to have treated the entire discharge from an active mine and any discharge from an adjacent inactive mine necessary to protect the active workings.

 

98 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Zellefrow Estate


No. 98, March T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


450 Pa. 302; 299 A.2d 248; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 609


September 25, 1972, Argued  
January 19, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Armstrong County, No. 3-71-030, in re estate of Flora M. Zellefrow; appeal of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania.



DISPOSITION: Decree of Orphans' Court vacated and matter remanded to court below.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commonwealth sought review of the decree of the Court of Common Pleas of Armstrong County (Pennsylvania), that rejected appellant's tax assessment and held that the inheritance tax on the specifically devised realty to appellee devisees was deductible before valuing the residue for tax purposes.


OVERVIEW: Decedent left her estate to appellees devisees. Appellant commonwealth calculated inheritance tax on the estate. The court held that the question presented by this appeal was whether the amount of the inheritance tax, calculated to be due on specifically devised property, was to be deducted from the value of the residue before computing the total inheritance tax due. Appellant's assessment did not allow such a deduction. The lower court rejected appellant's tax assessment and held that the inheritance tax on the specifically devised realty was deductible before valuing the residue for tax purposes. The court reversed the lower court's decree that rejected appellant's tax assessment because the trial court erred in allowing the tax on the specific devise to be deducted for the purpose of computing the total inheritance tax due. The court held that to hold otherwise would have led to anomalous tax consequences in that the tax on the same total gift would vary depending on the presence or absence of a specific devise.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's decree that rejected appellant's tax assessment and held that the inheritance tax on the specifically devised realty was deductible before valuing the residue for tax purposes because the lower court erred in allowing the tax on the specific devise to be deducted for the purpose of computing the total inheritance tax due. The court remanded the case to the lower court.

Commonwealth v. Jones


Nos. 376 and 384, Jan. T., 1971


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


450 Pa. 442; 299 A.2d 288; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 630


January 10, 1972, Submitted  
January 19, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, June T., 1967, Nos. 970 to 973, inclusive in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. George Washington Jones.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed; appeal in No. 384 quashed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which after his conviction for arson and murder in the first degree sentenced him to life imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of arson and first-degree murder and sentenced to life imprisonment as to the murder conviction. Defendant challenged the judgment of sentence on several grounds. On appeal, the court quashed the appeal of the arson conviction, holding that there was no final judgment and therefore the appeal was premature. As to the murder conviction and subsequent sentence, the court affirmed the judgment of the lower court. The court held that defendant was not prejudiced by the 32 month delay until the trial. The court concluded that defendant's right to a speedy trial were not violated because the case was removed from the trial list in one instance for purpose of plea negotiations and in another for reasons not discernable from the record. The court determined that defendant failed to raise the claim until the day of the actual trial and defendant did not object to the continuances granted to the state. The court also held that while the lower court inadequately explained criminal causation, it was undisputed throughout the trial that the fire caused the death.


OUTCOME: The court quashed the appeal on the arson conviction, holding that since a final judgment was not entered on the arson conviction, the appeal was premature. The court affirmed the conviction and sentence of the lower court on the issue of murder in the first-degree. The court held that defendant suffered no prejudice by the 32 month delay until trial and, in fact, defendant was not ready to proceed to trial within that time.

101 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Schmidt


No. 151, March T., 1971


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


452 Pa. 185; 299 A.2d 254; 1973 Pa. LEXIS 435


September 27, 1971, Submitted  
January 19, 1973, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Allegheny County, Sept. T., 1964, No. 111, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. George Schmidt.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed his petition filed under the Post Conviction Hearing Act challenging his conviction for murder in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and others committed four burglaries, and during one of them, a person was killed. Defendant confessed to committing the burglaries and the murder, but at trial he denied involvement in the burglary during which the murder occurred. The jury convicted him of murder in the first degree, and he was sentenced to life imprisonment. The lower court dismissed his petition filed under the Post Conviction Hearing Act, 19 P.S. § 1180-1 et seq. The court affirmed the order of the lower court, holding that defense counsel's decision not to challenge the legality of the arrest was binding on defendant and constituted a waiver of the issue under 19 P.S. § 1180- 4(b). The court ruled that evidentiary errors did not entitle defendant to relief because they did not rise to the level of constitutional error. It held that any illegality in the search complained of by defendant was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt, that he was not prejudiced by being denied a juvenile certification hearing, and that the admission of testimony of his silence in response to certain statements did not raise a constitutional issue because it did not occur in the context of a custodial interrogation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court, holding that defendant's challenge to the legality of his arrest was waived because his attorney's decision not to raise an issue at the trial or on direct appeal was binding on defendant, and that other alleged errors did not entitle defendant to relief because they were not constitutional in nature or were harmless beyond a reasonable doubt.

Philadelphia v. Pennsylvania Public Utility Com.


No. 88, Jan. T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


449 Pa. 402; 296 A.2d 804; 1972 Pa. LEXIS 392


January 10, 1972, Argued  
November 17, 1972, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused December 18, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from orders of Commonwealth Court, Nos. 996 and 997, Transfer Docket, 1970, in re City of Philadelphia and Pennsylvania Department of Transportation v. Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission et al.



DISPOSITION: Order of Commonwealth Court affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants telephone company and electric company challenged the orders of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which vacated the portion of the order of the Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission that had obligated appellees city and public transportation authority to reimburse appellants for the costs incurred by them pursuant to facility relocation caused by subway construction.


OVERVIEW: Appellees city and public transportation authority requested permission to extend the subway, which would require street crossings over the subway line at 15 separate locations. It would also require facilities relocations of various utilities. Appellants telephone company and electric company had agreements with appellee city that in the event their facilities needed to be relocated they would bear the costs of such relocations. The Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission (Commission) conducted a full hearing and assigned 75 percent of appellants' relocation costs to appellees. The lower court vacated the portion of the Commission's order that required appellees to pay appellants' costs. The court held that the agreements between appellee city and appellants were binding, and that the Commission did not have the broad authority to abrogate them or to allocate the costs of facilities relocation in a manner inconsistent with the agreements. The court affirmed the lower court's order.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's order because the Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission (Commission) exceeded its authority by requiring appellees city and public transportation authority to reimburse 75 percent of the facilities relocation costs incurred by appellants telephone company and electric company.

Pennsylvania State Bd. of Pharmacy v. Cohen


No. 88, May T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


448 Pa. 189; 292 A.2d 277; 1972 Pa. LEXIS 449


May 2, 1972, Argued  
June 28, 1972, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Commonwealth Court, No. 253 C.D. 1971, affirming the order of State Board of Pharmacy, in re Pennsylvania State Board of Pharmacy v. Leonard S. Cohen, t/a Senate Drug Store.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) affirming the order of State Board of Pharmacy suspending appellant's pharmacist's license for one year and revoking indefinitely his pharmacist's permit.


OVERVIEW: The State Board of Pharmacy issued a citation to appellant charging that he had openly sold vast quantities of drug containers and supplies to dilute drugs to drug traffickers. After hearing, the board concluded that appellant had violated the Pharmacy Act, 63 P.S. §§ 390-5(a)(9) and 390-5(b)(2), suspended appellant's pharmacist's license for one year, and revoked indefinitely his pharmacist's permit. The commonwealth court affirmed. On appeal, the court granted allocatur and reversed because appellant's conduct did not violate any of the thirteen prohibitions constituting "grossly unprofessional conduct" in § 390-5(a)(9), any other provision of the Pharmacy Act, or any rule or regulation promulgated thereunder, or any other law of the Commonwealth. The court found that 63 P.S. § 390-5 contained a very specific listing of the violations that could result in suspension of a license. The court concluded that because the offense alleged did not constitute a violation of one of the listed prohibitions, it could not support a suspension under that statute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order affirming the order of State Board of Pharmacy suspending appellant's pharmacist's license for one year and revoking indefinitely his pharmacist's permit finding that appellant's conduct did not violate any of the thirteen prohibitions constituting "grossly unprofessional conduct" in the Pharmacy Act or any other law of the Commonwealth.

 

105 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


In re Green


No. 315, Jan. T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


448 Pa. 338; 292 A.2d 387; 1972 Pa. LEXIS 466; 52 A.L.R.3d 1106


April 25, 1972, Argued  
June 28, 1972, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, Oct. T., 1971, No. 908, reversing order of Court of Common Pleas, Family Court Division of Philadelphia, No. 174612, in re Ricky Ricardo Green.



DISPOSITION: Order of Superior Court reversed and matter remanded to Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia, Family Court Division, for proceedings consistent with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent mother appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Pennsylvania, which reversed the family court order that had dismissed petitioner director's application for appointment of a guardian for respondent's 16-year-old son on grounds that he was a neglected child under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 11, § 243 et seq. because respondent refused, for religious reasons, to consent to spinal surgery to treat the child's paralytic scoliosis.


OVERVIEW: Respondent mother had a son who suffered from paralytic scoliosis, which prevented him from standing or walking. Respondent refused to give consent to the physician-advised surgery on the grounds that the proposed spinal fusion procedure required her son to receive blood transfusions, to which respondent was religiously opposed. Consequently, petitioner director sought a declaration that the boy was a neglected child under Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 11, § 243 et seq., which required the appointment of a guardian who would give consent to the operation. The trial court dismissed the petition, but the superior court reversed. On review, the court reversed, noting that there was no evidence that the boy's life was in any danger that required immediate surgery. The court accordingly found that state's parens patriae interest was not sufficient to outweigh respondent's religious beliefs and rights under U.S. Const. amend. I. However, the court remanded the case for an evidentiary hearing to allow the boy an opportunity to be heard, noting that the ultimate question was whether respondent's religious beliefs were paramount to the possible adverse wishes of her son.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the case for an evidentiary hearing to determine the child's wishes, finding that because the teenager's life was not immediately imperiled by his physical condition, the state's parens patriae interest was not, in itself, of sufficient magnitude to outweigh respondent mother's religious beliefs, although the child's personal decision might be paramount to respondent's beliefs.

 

106 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Ravenell


No. 112, Jan. T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


448 Pa. 162; 292 A.2d 365; 1972 Pa. LEXIS 446


January 13, 1972, Argued  
June 28, 1972, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, May T., 1969, No. 378, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. James Ravenell.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), that convicted him of murder and sentenced him to imprisonment for not less than 5 years and not more than 20 years.


OVERVIEW: A man was shot and killed in a gang fight. Police arrested appellant after interrogating gang members. Appellant gave a confession and was later convicted of murder after an unsuccessful motion to suppress, which was alleged to have been the product of police coercion. Appellant challenged the conviction claiming error in denial of the motion to suppress and that there were multiple errors within the trial. The court found that the motion to suppress was properly denied as the commonwealth met its burden, the trial court did make findings of fact, and consonant statement was inadmissible to corroborate appellant's testimony regarding police misconduct. Additionally, the court found that the petition for mistrial was properly denied as hearsay statement had no influence on the verdict, appellant's confession although read into the record was an exhibit, under Pa. R. Crim. P. 323(g) it was permissible to allow appellant's statements from suppression hearing in where at trial he testified contrary, and that there was no abuse of discretion in the trial court's denial of hearing for continuance based on allegations of unfair trial due to pre-trial publicity of an unrelated gang member.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the sentence of the trial court holding after consideration of each and every asserted assignment of error that nothing warranted the grant of a new trial. The motion to suppress was properly denied as the commonwealth met its burden, there was no abuse of discretion where trial court refused to admit consonant statement to corroborate testimony, and confession exhibit was properly allowed during jury deliberation.

Commonwealth ex rel. Specter v. Levin


Nos. 54, 56 to 59, inclusive, 61 to 71, inclusive, 73 and 74


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


448 Pa. 1; 293 A.2d 15; 1972 Pa. LEXIS 427


February 2, 1972, Argued  
June 5, 1972, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from final reapportionment plan of Pennsylvania Legislative Reapportionment Commission, in cases of Commonwealth ex rel. Arlen Specter v. Levin; appeal of Robert A. Rovner et al.; appeal of Lawrence Sager; appeal of C. V. Afflerback et al.; James H. J. Tate et al. v. Legislative Reapportionment Commission; Elmer Berkebile et al. v. Legislative Reapportionment Commission; Thomas P. McCreesh et al. v. Legislative Reapportionment Commission; Joseph Pavlak et al. v. Legislative Reapportionment Commission; Lawrence H. Curry et al. v. Legislative Reapportionment Commission; Robert Oravetz et al. v. Legislative Reapportionment Commission; Bernard F. Sherer v. Legislative Reapportionment Commission; Fred L. Lebder v. Legislative Reapportionment Commission; appeal of Baldwin Borough; Michael von Moschzisker v. Levin; George O. Piazzi et al. v. Legislative Reapportionment Commission; Robert J. Devers et al. v. Legislative Reapportionment Commission; Joseph T. Doyle et al. v. Legislative Reapportionment Commission; and appeal of J. E. Fleming, Jr.



DISPOSITION: Ordered that Plan shall be used in the forthcoming Primary and General Elections of 1972 and thereafter shall remain in force and [***2] effect until constitutionally altered. *



* Reporter's Note: On October 10, 1972, the Supreme Court of the United States dismissed an appeal for the want of a substantial federal question.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the commonwealth, sought review of a final reapportionment plan of the Pennsylvania Legislative Reapportionment Commission.


OVERVIEW: The Pennsylvania Legislative Reapportionment Commission developed a reapportionment plan. The commonwealth sought judicial review of that plan. The court found that the Equal Protection Clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, required that the commonwealth make an honest and good faith effort to construct districts, in both houses of its legislature, as nearly of equal population as practicable but allow for divergences from a strict population standard based on state policy. The court also found that the Pennsylvania Constitution, Pa. Const. art. II, § 16 required the Commonwealth to be divided into 50 senatorial and 203 representative districts, which would be composed of compact and contiguous territory as nearly equal in population as practicable. Further, unless absolutely necessary no county, city, incorporated town, borough, township or ward would be divided in forming either a senatorial or representative district. The court affirmed the final apportionment plan of the Pennsylvania Legislative Reapportionment Commission, finding that the plan comported with the mandates of the Federal and Pennsylvania Constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the final apportionment plan of the Pennsylvania Legislative Reapportionment Commission as constitutional.

Commonwealth v. Butler


No. 126, Jan. T., 1971


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


448 Pa. 128; 291 A.2d 89; 1972 Pa. LEXIS 441


January 14, 1972, Argued  
May 25, 1972, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, April T., 1968, No. 1, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Freddy Butler.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania) finding him guilty of first degree murder in the stabbing death of an elderly man.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first-degree murder and sentenced to life imprisonment for his alleged role as the attacker in the stabbing death of an elderly victim. Evidence indicated that four men participated in the attack; one of the four alleged assailants identified appellant and a second man as responsible. During investigation by appellee state, a warrant was obtained to search appellant's residence. The search yielded bloodstained clothing matching the victim's blood. The jury selected for the trial was drawn from a 98 percent white pool; the county wherein the crime was committed had a 6-8 percent black population. During trial, the judge examined one of appellant's alibi witnesses whom he believed to be lying, and looked up at the witness from the bench. Appellant's post-trial motions for relief were denied. On appeal, the court affirmed, concluding that the victim's wounds gave authorities probable cause to suspect that the clothing worn by appellant would be covered with blood; that appellant missed the deadline to challenge the grand jury array; and that an alleged grimace of the trial judge while examining appellant's alibi witness did not warrant a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the first degree murder conviction of appellant for the stabbing death of a man; the victim's wounds gave authorities probable cause to search appellant's residence for bloody clothing, appellant missed deadline to challenge the grand jury array, and a new trial was not warranted by judge's examination of alibi witness.

Balsbaugh v. Rowland


No. 32, May T., 1971


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


447 Pa. 423; 290 A.2d 85; 1972 Pa. LEXIS 547


January 20, 1971, Argued  
April 20, 1972, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued January 18, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, No. 3043 of 1970, in case of Walter Balsbaugh et al. v. James H. Rowland, Sr. et al.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania) sustaining a demurrer to appellant's complaint in equity seeking declaratory relief and to enjoin the Board of School Directors and the superintendent of schools from implementing a plan to correct a racial imbalance in the school district.


OVERVIEW: Appellees, the Board of School Directors and the superintendent of schools, developed a plan for quality desegregated education for the city school district designed to correct a racial imbalance in the district. Appellant taxpayer brought an action in equity for declaratory relief and injunction to prevent appellee from implementing the plan. The trial court denied appellant's request for a preliminary injunction and sustained a demurrer to appellant's complaint. Appellant sought review, arguing that the complaint did set forth a cause of action upon which relief could have been granted. Specifically, appellant argued that the adoption of the plan was not the result of properly exercised discretion, and the plan violated the equal protection clause of the U.S. Const. amend. XIV and, therefore, the expenditure of tax monies was illegal and could be enjoined. The court disagreed, finding that the plan was not adopted by the board under duress but rather was the product of board discretion. The court also found that the plan was constitutional and was carefully crafted to make the assignment of pupils and busing fair and equitable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of demurrer, finding that the plan to correct racial imbalances was constitutional and the result of the proper exercise of discretion.

 

110 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. La Londe


No. 144, March T., 1971


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


447 Pa. 364; 288 A.2d 782; 1972 Pa. LEXIS 540


September 28, 1971, Argued  
March 21, 1972, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of the Superior Court affirming the judgments of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Allegheny County, March T., 1968, No. 211, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Louis LaLonde, Charles Mitchlen, William Schrin.



DISPOSITION: Order of Superior Court and judgments of court of original jurisdiction reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of an order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed their convictions and sentences for illegal possession and sale of obscene literature in violation of the Pennsylvania Obscenity Statute, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 18, § 4524.


OVERVIEW: Testimony from the detective who purchased allegedly obscene books from appellants and the books themselves were commonwealth's only evidence as to the question of obscenity vel non. Appellants were convicted of illegal possession and sale of obscene literature in violation of the Pennsylvania Obscenity Statute, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 18, § 4524. The superior court affirmed the convictions and sentences. On appeal, the court reversed because commonwealth failed to sustain its burden of proof. Because it was not hard core pornography, proof other than the books was required to establish that the dominant theme of the material taken as a whole appealed to a prurient interest in sex, that the material was patently offensive, and that the material was utterly without redeeming social value. Determination of obscenity was for the trial court on the basis of contemporary community standards.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that upheld appellants' convictions for illegal possession and sale of obscene literature in violation of the Pennsylvania Obscenity Statute, due to a failure of proof. Commonwealth did not introduce evidence of the challenged books' prurient appeal, utter lack of redeeming social value, or its patent offensiveness as judged by contemporary community standards.

 

111 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Merrick v. Jennings


No. 32, Jan. T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


446 Pa. 489; 288 A.2d 523; 1972 Pa. LEXIS 318


January 10, 1972, Submitted  
March 20, 1972, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Chester County, No. 2220 of 1970, in case of John R. Merrick v. William Jennings and Jackson M. Ivins.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant public defender sought review of the order from the Court of Common Pleas, Chester County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed appellant's complaint in equity brought on behalf of present and future indigent defendants in order to enjoin the county sheriff and warden from applying and enforcing three state statutes that permitted imprisonment for nonpayment of fines.


OVERVIEW: Appellant public defender brought a complaint in equity on behalf of present and future indigent defendants and sought to enjoin the county sheriff and warden from applying and enforcing three state statutes that permitted imprisonment for nonpayment of fines. The trial court dismissed appellant's complaint on the ground that defendants threatened by imprisonment for nonpayment of fines had an adequate remedy at law. Appellant sought review of the dismissal order. The appellate court affirmed the judgment of the trial court and held that the indigent defendants had a full, complete, and adequate remedy at law. Therefore, appellant's complaint was not cognizable in equity. The court reasoned that such indigent defendants could raise the issue at trial and could fully pursue their constitutional rights. The court categorically rejected appellant's contention that the county courts would not enforce the law on this issue. The court found that appellant failed to show that the county courts would refuse to fully enforce the law or any controlling appellate decision.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed an order that dismissed appellant public defender's complaint in equity. The court held that appellant did not have standing to sue to enjoin county officials from enforcing statutes that permitted imprisonment for nonpayment of fines because the indigent defendants who were threatened with such imprisonment had an adequate legal remedy.

Glancey v. Casey


No. 406, Jan. T., 1971


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


447 Pa. 77; 288 A.2d 812; 1972 Pa. LEXIS 510


November 11, 1971, Argued  
March 20, 1972, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania, Commonwealth Docket, 1970, No. 338, in case of Joseph R. Glancey, President Judge of Municipal Court of Philadelphia on behalf of himself and all other Judges of said Court v. Robert P. Casey, Auditor General, and Grace M. Sloan, State Treasurer.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant municipal and traffic court judges sought review of the judgment from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania) in favor of appellee auditor general and appellee state treasurer after that court sustained appellees' preliminary objections to appellants' complaint in mandamus which sought retroactive salary payments.


OVERVIEW: Appellant municipal and traffic court judges, who had previously held magistrate positions, were appointed to newly created judicial positions on January 1, 1969. Appellants submitted monthly vouchers requesting appellee auditor general and appellee state treasurer pay appellants a higher salary than they had received as magistrates. Appellees refused to pay any salary increases. A statute, 17 P.S. § 711.2, was passed which provided a salary scale for appellants in the amounts appellants had requested from July 1, 1969. Appellants sued appellees to compel appellees to pay appellants from January 1, rather than July 1, as the statute stated. The lower court granted appellees' preliminary objections and entered judgment for appellees. Appellants sought review of the judgment. The court affirmed the judgment in favor of appellees. The legislature had the exclusive constitutional authority to fix judicial compensation. Further, the court found that the salary-fixing statute was valid insofar as it made appellants' salaries retroactive only to July 1, rather than to January 1. The court refused to sever the retroactive clause from the salary-fixing clause.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of appellee auditor and appellee state treasurer because appellees were under a duty to follow the legislative directive to pay appellant municipal and traffic court judges the compensation fixed by law, and the salary-fixing statute was valid.

Commonwealth v. Shaffer


Nos. 67 and 68, Jan. T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


447 Pa. 91; 288 A.2d 727; 1972 Pa. LEXIS 511; 77 A.L.R.3d 1124


November 8, 1971, Argued  
March 20, 1972, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from order of the Superior Court, Oct. T., 1970, Nos. 1311 to 1316, inclusive, affirming judgments of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County, March T., 1969, Nos. 611, 612, 613, 615, 616 and 617, in cases of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. John A. Shaffer, Frank J. Crutchley, Joseph E. Shaffer and Michael P. Dougherty.



DISPOSITION: Order of Superior Court in Appeal No. 68 affirmed; order in Appeal No. 67 reversed. *



* Reporter's Note: On May 24, 1972, a petition for allowance of appeal was denied by the Supreme Court of Pennsylvania, and on November 6, 1972, the Supreme Court of the United States denied a petition for a writ of certiorari.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of judgments of sentence of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), based on their convictions for burglary, larceny, receiving stolen goods, and conspiracy.


OVERVIEW: One of two indicted appellants was unavailable for trial. During the trial, the trial court on its own motion declared a mistrial over appellant's objections. Both appellants were retried on the same indictments and both were convicted of burglary, larceny, receiving stolen goods, and conspiracy. The superior court affirmed both judgments of sentence. On appeal, the court reversed as to the available appellant because the convictions at the second trial constituted double jeopardy. There was no manifest necessity for the mistrial and the trial court abused its discretion in directing one because appellant's desire to proceed should have been respected. The court affirmed the judgment as to the unavailable appellant because there was no question of double jeopardy. All of the occupants of the vehicle in which appellant was riding acted in concert and the act of throwing property out of the window sufficiently warranted the conclusion that appellant exercised control over the property. The facts supported a connection between appellant's unexplained possession of recently stolen property and an inference that appellant had stolen the property.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of sentence as to appellant who had been retried. Double jeopardy attached because there was no manifest necessity for a mistrial and appellant had desired to continue. Judgment of sentence affirmed as to appellant who had been unavailable for the first trial because there was no question of double jeopardy and his unexplained possession of stolen property supported the inference that he had stolen it.
