Duggan v. 807 Liberty Ave., Inc.


No. 3, March T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


447 Pa. 281; 288 A.2d 750; 1972 Pa. LEXIS 531


September 30, 1971, Argued  
March 20, 1972, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Allegheny County, Oct. T., 1971, No. 1383, in case of Robert W. Duggan, District Attorney of Allegheny County v. 807 Liberty Avenue, Inc. and Thomas Benanati.



DISPOSITION: Decree reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant adult movie theater sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which, in an action brought by appellee district attorney, temporarily enjoined appellant from showing certain movies the court judged obscene.


OVERVIEW: Appellee district attorney brought an action seeking to enjoin appellant adult movie theater from showing certain movies he claimed were obscene. The trial court viewed one of the movies. Appellant declined to offer testimony. The court preliminarily enjoined appellant from showing the four films as to which evidence was offered. Appellee promptly asked for a date for a permanent injunction hearing. The court held that it had jurisdiction over the appeal because appellee was not an "officer of the Commonwealth" as contemplated by P.S. § 211.402. The court held that where a state sought to exercise its power to prevent commercial dissemination of allegedly obscene material, it must have established precise objective standards and procedural safeguards. The court held that the injunctive procedure provided to appellant was unconstitutional because there was no assurance as to the time period for final disposition of the case. The court held that the procedure was an undue prior restraint. Therefore, the court reversed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's temporary injunction, requested by appellee district attorney, enjoining appellant adult movie theater from showing certain movies because the action was an undue prior restraint.

Commonwealth v. Armao


No. 584, Jan. T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


446 Pa. 325; 286 A.2d 626; 1972 Pa. LEXIS 306


November 12, 1970, Argued  
January 20, 1972, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused February 14, 1972.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of Superior Court, Oct. T., 1969, Nos. 1075 and 1076, affirming judgments of sentence of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, Dec. T., 1963, No. 152, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Eugene Armao and Arnold Orsatti.



DISPOSITION: Order of Superior Court reversed and defendants discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, newspaper publisher and writer, sought review of an order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed appellants' judgments of sentence based on their convictions for criminal libel in violation of Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 18, § 4412.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, newspaper publisher and writer, were convicted of criminal libel in violation of 18 Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 18, § 4412 and their judgments of sentence were affirmed. On appeal, the court reversed and discharged appellants because § 4412, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 19, § 801, and a portion of Pa. Const. art. I, § 7 did not satisfy the demands of U.S. Const. amend. I. The statutes did not include a provision for truth as an absolute defense or recognize the federal standard of proof that alleged libel was published with a knowing falsity or reckless disregard of the truth. The statutes were limited in application to criticisms of public officers and should have extended to public issues and events of general interest. The statutes' inclusion of negligence as actionable civil defamation violated fundamental principles of justice. The "maliciously or negligently" standard of Pa. Const. art. I, § 7 did not satisfy the stringent requirement of actual malice or a reckless disregard for the truth.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order and discharged appellants, newspaper publisher and writer, because the criminal libel statutes and state constitution provision that appellants were convicted under did not meet the requirements of the First Amendment. Their standards were limited in application and did not comport with the federal standard that required alleged libel to be published with a knowing falsity or reckless disregard of the truth.

 

116 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Matthews


No. 384, Jan. T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


446 Pa. 65; 285 A.2d 510; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 604


January 19, 1971, Argued  
December 29, 1971, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, April T., 1968, No. 888, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. William Matthews.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), that convicted him of murder in the first-degree and sentenced him accordingly.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested in connection with a robbery that resulted in the stabbing death of the victim. Pursuant to a search warrant, police officers retrieved a kitchen knife that they believed was used in the commission of the crime. The lower court convicted defendant of murder in the first-degree. Defendant challenged his conviction asserting that there was insufficient probable cause to authorize the issuance of the search warrant and that the knife was illegally seized because it did not match the description of the knife within the warrant. The court affirmed defendant's conviction because the affidavit of one of the participants of the crime was reliable to show that defendant was involved in the crime and, therefore, there was sufficient probable cause to support the issuance of the warrant. The court held that the search was not exploratory but rather it was in good faith toward the object specified in the warrant and, therefore, the seizure of the kitchen knife was not illegal. The court found that defendant was not prejudiced by excusing certain potential jurors that were against the death penalty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for murder in the first-degree because the search warrant issued was supported by an affidavit from one of the participants in the crime and, therefore, there was sufficient probable cause. The court also held that knife seized during the search did not amount to an illegal seizure because officers exercised the search in good faith toward the knife specifically described in the warrant.

 

117 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Beaver Gasoline Co. v. Osborne Borough


No. 148, March T., 1971


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


445 Pa. 571; 285 A.2d 501; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 712


September 30, 1971, Argued  
December 29, 1971, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Commonwealth Court, No. 983 of 1970, in re appeal of Beaver Gasoline Company, and Antonio DiGirolamo and Nancy DiGirolamo, and Santo DiGirolamo and Rosemarie DiGirolamo v. Zoning Hearing Board of the Borough of Osborne, and the Borough of Osborne.



DISPOSITION: Orders vacated and case remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant borough challenged the order from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which overturned the zoning ordinance prohibiting service stations in commercial districts in appellee company's challenge to the constitutionality of the ordinance and the denial of its application for a building permit.


OVERVIEW: Appellant borough denied appellee company a permit to erect a service station in a commercial district. Appellee challenged the denial to the zoning board, claiming the ordinance prohibiting service stations in commercial districts was unconstitutional. The zoning board affirmed the denial, and the common pleas court affirmed that decision. The reviewing court, however, reversed the denial, and appellant challenged. The court refused to abandon the policy that a zoning ordinance was presumed valid and that the burden of establishing its invalidity was upon the party challenging it. However, the requirement that an applicant establish by affirmative evidence the nonexistence of a proper zoning purpose in the total prohibition of an otherwise legitimate business activity was an unrealistic burden. Thus, the court held that appellee met its burden by showing the total ban. Appellant then had the burden of sustaining the ordinance by presenting evidence which established the public purpose served thereby. However, until the date of this opinion, appellant was not aware of this duty. Thus, the case was remanded to allow appellant to produce additional evidence.


OUTCOME: The court neither reversed nor affirmed the order overturning the zoning ordinance prohibiting service stations in commercial districts. Rather, the court remanded the case to allow appellant borough to produce additional evidence based on the court's holding that appellee company satisfied its burden of overcoming the presumption of the ordinance's validity by showing a total prohibition of an otherwise legitimate business activity.

Commonwealth v. Sheehan


No. 129, Jan. T., 1971


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


446 Pa. 35; 285 A.2d 465; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 598


January 5, 1971, Submitted  
December 20, 1971, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, Oct. T., 1969, No. 841, affirming the order of Court of Common Pleas of Chester County, June T., 1963, No. 67, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. James Sheehan, a/k/a James F. Sheehan.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed and case remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment from the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), affirming the order of the trial court in a criminal action for drunk driving, where appellant had petitioned for relief pursuant to the Post Conviction Hearing Act, 19 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1180-1 et seq., challenging the validity of his 1963 drunk driving conviction, asserting that he had been unconstitutionally denied his right to counsel.


OVERVIEW: In 1963, appellant, without counsel, pled guilty to driving under the influence. In 1968, appellant was arrested for driving under the influence. Prior to trial, the trial judge informed appellant that the invariable policy of the court was three months in county prison on all second offenders. Appellant then filed a petition under the Post Conviction Hearing Act 19 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1180-1 et seq., challenging the validity of his 1963 conviction, asserting that he had been unconstitutionally denied his right to counsel. The trial court, without holding a hearing, denied relief. The Superior Court affirmed the denial. The court reversed. The offense of drunken driving in Pennsylvania, though a misdemeanor, was punishable by a fine of $ 100 to $ 500 and imprisonment up to three years, or both. 75 Pa Cons. Stat. § 1037. A person charged with that offense was entitled to representation by counsel, and, if indigent, that such representation to have been without cost to him; it was not necessary that the offense be in the felony category. The case was remanded to the trial court to hold an evidentiary hearing to determine if appellant knowingly waived his right to counsel.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment from the superior court affirming the denial of appellant's petition in a criminal action for drunk driving, where appellant had petitioned for relief pursuant to the Post Conviction Hearing Act, challenging the validity of his 1963 drunk driving conviction, because he had been unconstitutionally denied his right to counsel.

Matus v. Triangle Publications, Inc.


No. 372, Jan. T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


445 Pa. 384; 286 A.2d 357; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 689


May 6, 1970, Argued  
December 20, 1971, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused February 4, 1972. *


* Reporter's Note: On June 26, 1972, the Supreme Court of the United States denied a petition for writ of certiorari.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County, No. 5225 of 1966, in case of Raymond Matus v. Triangle Publications, Inc., and James Gerhart.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, radio station owner and talk show host, sought review of a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania). The trial court entered judgment on a jury verdict in favor of appellee snowplow operator, who brought an action in trespass against appellants after appellant talk show host uttered false statements about appellee on a radio show.


OVERVIEW: Appellant talk show host expressed displeasure on the air with appellee snowplow operator; appellant claimed that appellee had overcharged appellant's wife after plowing appellant's driveway. The following morning, appellant retracted his criticism after learning that a different person had actually plowed his driveway. Appellee brought an action in trespass against appellants, talk show host and radio station owner. A jury awarded damages to appellee. The court affirmed the judgment and held that the trial court properly instructed the jury that because the radio station had a conditional privilege, appellee could not recover unless he proved, by a preponderance of the evidence, that the statements were false and were made with the knowledge that they were false or without reasonable care to ascertain the truth. The dispute was not one of public concern and did not contribute to debate on public issues. Thus, the trial court properly did not apply the rule that the conditional privilege could be defeated only by clear and convincing proof that the defamatory falsehood was published with knowledge that it was false or with reckless disregard of whether it was false or not.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of appellee snowplow operator, who brought an action in trespass against appellants, radio station owner and talk show host. The trial court instructed the jury on the correct standard that appellee had to satisfy to recover because the statements made on the air concerned a private, not a public, matter.

Demczuk Estate


No. 124, Jan. T., 1971


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


444 Pa. 212; 282 A.2d 700; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 780


April 26, 1971, Argued  
October 12, 1971, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas, Orphans' Court Division, of Bucks County, No. 32750 of 1970, in re estate of John Demczuk.



DISPOSITION: Decree reversed and record remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimant sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas, Bucks County (Pennsylvania) which denied his petition to release estate funds being held in the state treasury pursuant to Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 1314. The lower court held that appellant, a resident of the Soviet Union, had failed to establish sufficiently his identity as the son and residuary legatee under the testator's will.


OVERVIEW: Appellant claimant, a resident of the Soviet Union, contended that he was the testator's son and residuary legatee. Invoking the Iron Curtain Act, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 20, § 1155, et seq. (Supp. 1971), an auditor filed a report authorizing payment of the funds into the state treasury. Some years after an initial dismissal of his exceptions, appellant petitioned for release of the funds. The trial court acknowledged the Iron Curtain Act was unconstitutional, but nonetheless denied appellant's petition on grounds that he had failed to supply clear, precise, and definite proof of his identity. It was ordered the funds remain in the state treasury pursuant to Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 1314. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded. Because this was not a case of intestacy, the court held appellant had to establish his identity only by a preponderance of competent evidence. Appellant's evidence included a certificate of acknowledgement executed by the United States Consul in Moscow, as well as affidavits from residents of the village from which the testator had emigrated. Viewed in the aggregate, the court concluded the evidence was sufficient to satisfy appellant's burden of proof.


OUTCOME: Holding that appellant claimant had adequately proven his identity was therefore entitled to take under his father's will, the court reversed the trial court's judgment and remanded.

Incollingo v. Ewing


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


444 Pa. 299; 282 A.2d 206; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 786


May 6, 1971, Reargued  
October 12, 1971, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reported at: 282 A.2d at 225. Opinion of October 7, 1977, Reported at; 474 Pa. 527.


PRIOR HISTORY: Original Opinion of January 25, 1971, Reported at: 1971 Pa. LEXIS 785.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants reargued a Supreme Court of Pennsylvania judgment challenging disparity in measure of damages for personal injury resulting in death depending solely upon whether suit was commenced before or after the death of the appellee decedent.


OVERVIEW: Appellants challenged disparity in measure of damages for personal injury resulting in death, depending solely upon whether suit was commenced before or after death. Before death, damages were awarded on the basis of deceased appellee's anticipated gross earnings, with no deduction of cost of maintenance. After death, damages would be loss of net earning power -- gross earnings diminished by the amount deceased appellee would have had to expend for his own maintenance had he not died, reduced to present worth. Lower court did not deduct cost of maintenance from damages. On appeal, court affirmed only because circumstances of this particular case were so exceptional as to dictate a purely prospective application of net earnings damages holding. As applied to prospective survival actions, economic loss to appellee's estate should be measured by appellee's total estimated future earning power less estimated cost of personal maintenance, reduced to present value.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed because circumstances of this particular case were so exceptional as to dictate a purely prospective application of net earnings damages holding.

Commonwealth v. Myers


No. 203, Jan. T., 1971


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


444 Pa. 465; 282 A.2d 347; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 814


April 19, 1971, Submitted  
October 12, 1971, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, March T., 1958, No. 1323, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Jack R. Myers.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), convicting him of first-degree murder and sentencing him to life imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first-degree murder upon his guilty plea and sentenced to life imprisonment. Appellant filed a petition for post conviction relief alleging that his plea of guilty was involuntary, that he was represented by incompetent counsel, and that an unconstitutionally obtained confession was used at his degree-of-guilt hearing. After an evidentiary hearing, appellant's petition was denied. On appeal, the court found that neither appellant nor his counsel challenged the admissibility of his confession at trial. Moreover, the court held that appellant admitted that the primary motivation for his guilty plea was the advice of his counsel that he plead guilty to avoid the death penalty. Thus, the court reasoned that appellant could not challenge his plea on the grounds of an alleged unconstitutional confession. The court also rejected appellant's contention that his counsel was ineffective. The court reasoned that there was a reasonable basis on counsel's part to not pursue an insanity defense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court convicting appellant of murder and sentencing him to life imprisonment. The court held that appellant could not challenge the constitutionality of his confession on appeal because appellant failed to object at trial and appellant failed to show his guilty plea was primarily motivated by the alleged involuntary confession.

McGill v. Leverington-Roxborough Sav. & Loan Asso.


No. 388, Jan. T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


444 Pa. 483; 282 A.2d 280; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 818


December 2, 1970, Argued  
October 12, 1971, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, June T., 1969, No. 5404, in case of Francis E. McGill, for himself and on behalf of others similarly situated, v. Leverington-Roxborough Savings & Loan Association et al.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant savings and loan members sought review of a decree of the Court of Common Pleas for Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) which entered final decree in favor of appellee association, in appellants' case to require a membership vote on a proposed merger of their association with appellee as a pre-condition to the merger.


OVERVIEW: Appellant savings and loan members sought review of the decision to dismiss their complaint. The principal issue presented by appeal was whether a member of a saving and loan association had a right, enforceable in a court of equity, to compel a membership vote on a proposed merger of his association with another as a pre-condition to the merger. The complaint alleged that appellee association's directors refused to notify the members of their association concerning the proposed merger, or to furnish appellants with a membership list so that they could communicate with them. Appellants argued that important property rights of members were involved, and that as a matter of fundamental fairness and in the exercise of its supervisory control over corporations, a court of equity should have intervened to protect those rights by allowing a membership vote. The court held that appellants failed to demonstrate that such property rights as they may have were in danger of being adversely affect. Thus, as a matter of law the essential element of irreparable injury to appellants' property rights was not present.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decrees of the Court of Common Pleas which dismissed appellant savings and loan members' complaint to compel a membership vote on a proposed merger. The court reasoned that appellants failed to demonstrate that their property rights were in danger of being adversely affected if the court did not compel a membership vote. Costs were taxed on appellants.

Amidon v. Kane


Nos. 8, 9 and 10, May T., 1972


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


444 Pa. 38; 279 A.2d 53; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 757


May 26, 1971, Argued  
June 24, 1971, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from decree of Commonwealth Court, Nos. 255, 260, and 275, May T., 1971, in case of D. Curtis Amidon, Rodney D. Mayheim and Marvin M. Zimmerman v. Robert Kane, Secretary of Revenue, Robert P. Casey, Auditor General and J. Shane Creamer, Attorney General of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania; Richard A. Tilghman et al. v. Robert Kane, Secretary of Revenue; concerned taxpayers of Allegheny County et al. v. Commonwealth of Pennsylvania and Robert Kane, Secretary of the Department of Revenue.



DISPOSITION: Decree reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers challenged a decree of the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), dismissing complaints in equity that challenged the constitutionality of the Personal Income Tax enacted by Article III of the Tax Reform Code of 1971, 72 P.S. § 7101 et seq. Appellants contended that the tax offended the constitutional mandate of uniformity set forth in Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayers argued that the personal income tax enacted under Article III of the Tax Reform Code of 1971, 72 P.S. § 7101 et seq., was repugnant to the Pennsylvania Constitution. On appeal from a decree dismissing appellants' complaints in equity, the court held that the structure of the personal income tax failed to conform to the requirements of the Uniformity Clause set forth in Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1 which required all taxes be uniform, upon the same class of subjects, within the territorial limits of the authority levying the tax. In explaining its holding, the court concluded that although the income tax imposed a flat rate of tax upon taxable income, the concept of taxable income reflected a myriad of tax preferences which created an unequal burden upon persons enjoying identical privileges of receiving earnings or otherwise acquiring the same dollar amount of annual income from any source whatsoever. Accordingly, the court held that the pervasive and impermissible discriminations between similarly situated taxpayers rendered the tax invalid.


OUTCOME: The decree dismissing appellant taxpayers' challenge to the constitutionality of the personal income tax provided by Article III of the Tax Reform Code of 1971 was reversed on the ground that the scheme of taxation discriminated between similarly situated taxpayers in violation of the Uniformity Clause of the Pennsylvania Constitution, thus rendering it unconstitutional.

Creamer v. Twelve Common Pleas Judges


No. 529, Miscellaneous Docket, No. 491, Miscellaneous Docket No. 18 and No. 446, Miscellaneous Docket No. 18

Twelve Common Pleas Judges, a Judge of the Commonwealth Court and a Justice of the Supreme Court of Pennsylvania; James H. J. Tate, Mayor of City of Philadelphia, and a taxpayer of the City of Philadelphia, on his behalf and on behalf of all the taxpayers of the said City v. Honorable D. Donald Jamieson, President Judge, Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia, John E. Walsh, Jr., Herbert W. Salus, Jr.; and William J. C. O'Donnell, as an individual, as an officer of court of the Court of Common Pleas of the 15th Judicial District, on his own behalf and on behalf of that class of persons who have matters pending before said court v. Thomas A. Pitt, Jr., an additional law Judge of the 15th Judicial District.



DISPOSITION: Gubernatorial appointments to judicial vacancies of Justice Barbieri, Judge Rogers, Judge Salus, Judge Stefan, Judge Jerome, Judge Wright, Judge Cody and Judge Gladden unanimously held to be valid; as to the gubernatorial appointments of Judge Morgan, Judge Mountenay, Judge Pitt, Judge Dowling, Judge Aston, and Judge Walsh, the relief requested by petitioners was [***2] not granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated cases, arising either by a writ of mandamus or by quo warranto or by petition to take original and plenary jurisdiction, petitioner, the commonwealth's attorney general, challenged the validity of the governor's appointment of respondents, fourteen common pleas judges. It was contended that the appointments of the new judges without senatorial approval was in violation of the constitution.


OVERVIEW: The governor appointed respondents, fourteen common pleas judges, when the senate was not in session. Petitioner, the commonwealth's attorney general, challenged the validity of the appointments either by a writ of mandamus or by quo warranto or by petition to take original and plenary jurisdiction. The court verified eight appointments, but the court was equally divided in its determination of the validity of the remaining six gubernatorial appointments. The court held that the principle that, when an appellate court was equally divided, the judgment, order or decree of the court below would be affirmed. However, because the court assumed plenary jurisdiction without the previous adjudication of any other court, there was no judgment, order or decree to affirm, modify or reverse. Accordingly, the court denied petitioner's request for relief.


OUTCOME: The court unanimously determined that the gubernatorial appointments to judicial vacancies of eight judges were valid, and was equally divided in its determination of the validity of the gubernatorial appointments of the remaining six. The relief requested by petitioner attorney general was not granted because there was no previous judgment.

American Federation of State, County & Municipal Employees v. Shapp

Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


443 Pa. 527; 280 A.2d 375; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 945


May 25, 1971, Argued  
June 24, 1971, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused August 16, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of Commonwealth Court, No. 310 C. D. of 1971, in case of American Federation of State, County and Municipal Employees, AFL-CIO by its Trustee ad litem, Gerald W. McEntee et al. v. The Honorable Milton J. Shapp et al.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant governor sought review of an order from the Commonwealth Court (Pennsylvania), which granted a preliminary injunction retaining the status quo during the pendency of the litigation brought by appellee state employees' labor union to prevent the discharge of appellee's members from employment with the state without notice and hearing.


OVERVIEW: Appellee state employees' labor union sought injunctive relief against appellant governor to prevent the discharge of appellee's members from employment with the state without notice and hearing. The court reversed the order granting the injunction, finding no reasonable ground for the issuance of the order. The court found that appellees were also not able to establish any statutory right to retention of their jobs. The court concluded that appellees, who obtained their jobs through political patronage, were essentially employees-at-will with no right to due process before their discharge.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that granted a preliminary injunction that retained the status quo during the pendency of the litigation brought by appellee state employees' labor union to prevent the discharge of appellee's members from employment with the state without notice and hearing, finding that appellees had no constitutional or statutory claim of protection for their jobs, which were essentially at-will.

Commonwealth v. McCloskey


No. 157, Jan. T., 1971


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


443 Pa. 117; 277 A.2d 764; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 892


December 1, 1970, Argued  
May 13, 1971, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused June 14, 1971. *


* Reporter's Note: On December 20, 1971, the Supreme Court of the United States denied a petition for a writ of certiorari.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of Superior Court, Oct. T., 1970, Nos. 1377 to 1384, inclusive, affirming order of Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Philadelphia, Nos. 2416 to 2421, inclusive, Oct. T., 1969, 266, Jan. T., 1970, 2674 to 2679, inclusive, Oct. T., 1969, 2723 and 2724, Oct. T., 1969 and 879 to 897, inclusive, Sept. T., 1969.



DISPOSITION: Orders of Superior Court reversed in part and affirmed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution appealed the order of Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the orders of the Court of Common Pleas that quashed indictments against and suppressed evidence obtained from the several defendants.


OVERVIEW: The trial court quashed defendants' indictments and suppressed evidence obtained and the superior court affirmed. The court reversed the quash of indictments because an indictment based upon an investigating grand jury's presentment directly to an indicting grand jury with leave of the court was lawful, even where a preliminary hearing was not held as defendants were not prejudiced by the lack of a preliminary hearing because of the limited quantum of evidence necessary to bind a defendant over and a defendant's opportunity to testify on his own behalf. The court affirmed the suppression of evidence, concluding that subpoenaed witnesses who gave testimony before an investigating grand jury without a warning that they could have counsel if confused about their rights against self-incrimination were denied their rights against self incrimination. The court also affirmed the quash of indictments and presentments based in part on the constitutionally impermissible testimony, as defendants were judicially compelled to testify without sufficient warning or protection concerning their right against self-incrimination.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders of the superior court quashing the indictments against defendants as indictments based on an investigating grand jury's presentment directly to an indicting grand jury was legal. The court affirmed the orders which suppressed evidence, as defendants were not warned of their right to have counsel to protect right against self-incrimination and affirmed the quash of those indictments based on impermissible testimony.

Commonwealth v. Morris Half Hour Laundromat


No. 22, May T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


442 Pa. 543; 277 A.2d 148; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 1053


January 11, 1971, Argued  
April 22, 1971, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused May 21, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, Commonwealth Docket No. 125 of 1967, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Morris Half Hour Laundromat.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant laundromat owner sought review of a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), which sustained appellee state's imposition and calculation of sales and use taxes upon appellant's business. Appellant asserted that the Tax Act of 1963 for Education, 72 P.S. § 3403-1 et seq., did not apply to his business, that the method of assessment did not comply with the Tax Act, and that the Act was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellee state conducted an audit of appellant laundromat owner's business and calculated that it owed additional taxes. Appellant prosecuted unsuccessful administrative appeals and then sought judicial review in the trial court, which sustained the state's position in all respects. On appeal, the court held that appellant did work for his customers -- through his machines -- and that work was a service covered by the Tax Act of 1963 for Education, 72 P.S. § 3403-1 et seq. The court found that the record was devoid of any evidence that the total tax due under appellant's theory would be any less than that found due under the appellee's auditor's theory, so appellant failed to sustain his burden of showing that the assessment was incorrect. Further, rounding off the tax due from any particular sale at retail to the next higher full cent was the only practicable way in which appellee could ensure that it received the basic tax rate. That being so, the bracket schedule's classification of separate sales at retail in terms of purchase price was reasonable and the Act did not violate the uniformity, due process, or equal protection clauses of the state or federal constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court held that appellant laundromat owner's business provided a service covered by the Tax Act of 1963 for Education and that appellant did not satisfy his burden of showing that the state's calculation of his taxes was unreasonable or unconstitutional. Therefore, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which sustained appellee state's imposition and calculation of the taxes.

Ray v. Commonwealth


No. 39, May T., 1971


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


442 Pa. 606; 276 A.2d 509; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 1062


January 12, 1971, Submitted  
April 22, 1971, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused August 31, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, Commonwealth Docket No. 351 1/2 of 1969, in case of Robert Allen Ray v. Commonwealth of Pennsylvania.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prisoner sought review of a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed appellant's complaint challenging the constitutionality of 25 P.S. § 2602(y), a statute that prohibited appellant from voting.


OVERVIEW: Appellant prisoner sought review of a judgment dismissing appellant's complaint challenging the constitutionality of 25 P.S. § 2602(y). Section 2602(y) excepted any person committed to and confined in a penal institution from the definition of "absentee elector." The court affirmed. Assuming that appellant's claim was not mooted by the fact that it was concerned with a past election or that injunction rather than mandamus was the proper form of remedy, appellant's claim still failed. Appellant misplaced reliance on Pa. Const. art. I, § 5, concerning the free exercise of suffrage. Elections were free and equal within the meaning of the constitution when they were public and open to all qualified electors alike. Article I, § 5 was subject to the same condition as was the right to an absentee ballot guaranteed in Pa. Const. art. VII, § 14, that the voter must be a qualified elector. Section 2602(y) did not violate any provision of either the Pennsylvania or United States Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment dismissing appellant prisoner's complaint contesting the constitutionality of a statute that excepted persons confined in penal institutions from the definition of "absentee elector." The court concluded that the statute lawfully defined the qualifications of electors.

Commonwealth v. Leaming


No. 169, Jan. T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


442 Pa. 223; 275 A.2d 43; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 1001


April 21, 1970, Argued  
March 18, 1971, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Philadelphia, May T., 1965, No. 795, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Joseph Leaming.



DISPOSITION: Nolle prosequi vacated and case remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which granted an application for nolle prosequi by the Commonwealth and denied defendant's demands for trial following the reversal of defendant's conviction for first degree murder. Defendant claimed that the delay violated his right to a speedy trial under U.S. Const. amend. VI.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the trial court's grant of an application for nolle prosequi filed by the Commonwealth. The trial court had previously ordered the case put on a deferred indictment status for a period not to exceed six months. Defendant was awaiting his new trial following the reversal of his conviction for first degree murder and claimed that the delay violated his right to a speedy trial under U.S. Const. amend. VI. The court agreed and vacated the nolle prosequi. The justifications provided by the Commonwealth for the nolle prosequi were insufficient to warrant the delay. Where one witness was hopelessly insane, and the only other known potential witness denied that he had any evidence to offer, there was scant basis to expect the possibility of trial in the foreseeable future. The Commonwealth contended that since appellant stood indicted for murder, which had no statute of limitations, a greater delay in bringing him to trial was permissible. The court found that factor relevant, but insufficient to warrant extension of the delay, which already exceeded 10 months. The court ordered the Commonwealth either proceed to trial or be barred from further prosecution.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the trial court's order of nolle prosequi and ordered that the Commonwealth proceed to trial or be barred from further prosecuting defendant. The reasons given by the Commonwealth for the nolle prosequi, including the insanity of one witness and statement by another that he had no evidence to offer, were insufficient to justify a further delay in bringing defendant to trial.

Conestoga Nat'l Bank v. Patterson


No. 13, May T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


442 Pa. 289; 275 A.2d 6; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 1011


May 26, 1970, Argued  
March 18, 1971, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Pennsylvania Department of Banking, May T., 1969, approving application of American Bank and Trust Co. of Pa., for letter of authority to establish a branch office at 42-44 North Prince Street, Lancaster, Lancaster County, Pennsylvania, in case of The Conestoga National Bank of Lancaster and The Fulton National Bank of Lancaster v. G. Allen Patterson, Secretary of the Pennsylvania Department of Banking and American Bank and Trust Company of Pennsylvania, Reading, Pennsylvania.



DISPOSITION: Order vacated and case remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, protesting banks, sought review of an order from the Pennsylvania Department of Banking, which authorized American Bank and Trust Company of Pennsylvania to establish a branch bank without any input from appellants.


OVERVIEW: American Bank and Trust Company of Pennsylvania was given authority to establish a branch bank by the Pennsylvania Department of Banking (Department). On appeal, appellants, protesting banks, contended that they were deprived of due process of law by the refusal of the Department to permit either a hearing on the bank branch application or access to the contents of the branch applications and other supporting data relating to the application. The court concluded that appellants should have been afforded the rights of procedural due process. The court determined that a decision on an application for a branch bank was judicial in nature and involved substantial property rights and that the Department's action represented judicial action by an administrative agency. The court then determined that appellants' should have been afforded notice, a hearing, the opportunity to present evidence, and access to the application and supporting data when confronted with a proposal for a new branch. The court vacated the order of the Department and remanded the case for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order of the Pennsylvania Department of Banking authorizing the establishment of a branch bank. The court held that the procedural due process rights of notice, a hearing, the opportunity to present evidence, and access to the application and supporting data of the proposed branch should have been provided to appellants, protesting banks, because the process was judicial in nature and involved substantial property rights.

Misitis v. Steel City Piping Co.


Nos. 183 and 191, March T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


441 Pa. 339; 272 A.2d 883; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 1125


October 8, 1970, Argued  
January 25, 1971, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from orders of Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Allegheny County, Jan. T., 1968, No. 2022, in case of Frank Misitis v. Steel City Piping Company et al.



DISPOSITION: Orders affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, sewage plant general contractor and consulting engineer, sought review from a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) that refused to grant appellants leave to amend their answers by adding as new matter the affirmative defense of the 12-year limitation on claims arising out of the negligent design or construction of improvements to real estate. Appellee individual's action was for personal injuries.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, general contractor and consultanting engineer of a sewage plant, challenged a trial court's refusal to grant leave to appellants to amend their answers by adding as new matter the affirmative defense of the 12-year limitation on claims arising out of the negligent design or construction of improvements to real estate as provided by 12 P.S. § 65.1. The court affirmed the decision of the trial court. Appellants were not entitled to amend their answers. The act creating the 12-year limitation took effect July 1, 1966. Appellee individual's alleged personal injury occurred on December 17, 1965, five days prior to the passage of § 65.1 and more than 6 months prior to § 65.1's effective date. Section 65.1 was prospective only in application and was not given retroactive effect. Appellee's personal injury action was governed by a two-year statute of limitation period, which appellee complied with, and was not challenged by § 65.1.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of the motion of appellants, sewer plant general contractor and consulting engineer, to amend their answers in appellee individual's personal injury action to include as a new affirmative defense the 12-year limitation on claims arising out of the negligent design or construction of real estate improvements. Such limitation period did not become effective until after appellee's accident.

Pennsylvania State Board of Pharmacy v. Pastor


No. 51, May T., 1969


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


441 Pa. 186; 272 A.2d 487; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 1105; 44 A.L.R.3d 1290


May 27, 1970, Argued  
January 7, 1971, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, March T., 1968, No. 70, affirming order of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, No. 224, Commonwealth Docket 1965, in case of Pennsylvania State Board of Pharmacy v. Edward Robert Pastor.



DISPOSITION: Orders of Superior Court and of Court of Common Pleas reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant pharmacist sought review of an order entered by the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the trial court's order suspending appellant's pharmacy license.


OVERVIEW: The Pennsylvania State Board of Pharmacy revoked appellant pharmacist's professional license after finding that he had advertised the price of certain dangerous or narcotic drugs in violation of § 8(11) of the Pharmacy Act, 63 P.S. § 390-8(11). On review, the trial court found that § 8(11) was constitutional, but remanded the matter to the Board for specific findings on whether the drugs advertised were, in fact, dangerous or narcotic drugs. The trial court affirmed the findings in part and reduced the revocation to a six-month suspension. The superior court affirmed. OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the superior court, which upheld an order suspending appellant pharmacist's professional license, because the provision of the Pharmacy Act that appellant was found to have violated was unconstitutional under the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment.

Commonwealth v. Bittner


No. 148, March T., 1969


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


441 Pa. 216; 272 A.2d 484; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 1109


October 7, 1970, Argued  
January 7, 1971, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Allegheny County, Sept. T., 1966, No. 39, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Edward Bittner.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, convicted of murder in the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), sought review, arguing that the 21 month delay between arrest and trial denied him speedy trial rights; 17 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 1276-77 unconstitutionally discriminated against potential jurors who were nonproperty holders; and it was error to charge the jury that it could infer murder from appellant's use of a deadly weapon.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of murder. Twenty-one months elapsed between arrest and trial. He appealed, arguing that he was denied a speedy trial; 17 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 1276-77, requiring selection of potential jurors from a list of taxables, unconstitutionally discriminated against nonproperty holders; and the trial court erred by charging the jury that it could infer first degree murder from the fatal use of a deadly weapon upon a vital body part. The court affirmed, holding that many of the trial delays were of appellant's own making. The court noted that during the delay, appellant filed three petitions for writ of habeas corpus, and applications to set bail, change venue, for pretrial discovery, for medical examination, to suppress evidence, and to quash the indictment, often requiring an evidentiary hearing. The court held that appellant failed to preserve for review his challenge to §§ 1276-77 by not raising the issue in post-trial motions; and the jury charge placed the burden upon appellee to prove specific intent to kill and the trial court instructed the jury that it decided what inferences it was to draw from use of a deadly weapon.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's murder conviction, holding that the 21 month delay between appellant's arrest and trial did not deny him speedy trial rights, because many of the delays were of appellant's own making; and the charge to the jury that it could infer murder from use of a deadly weapon was not error where the trial court charged the jury that appellee state had the burden to prove specific intent to kill.

State Board of Chiropractic Examiners v. Life Fellowship of Pennsylvania


No. 10, May T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


441 Pa. 293; 272 A.2d 478; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 1121


November 12, 1969, Argued  
January 7, 1971, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, No. 44, March T., 1969, affirming order of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, No. 186 Commonwealth Docket, 1968, in case of State Board of Chiropractic Examiners v. Life Fellowship of Pennsylvania.



DISPOSITION: Order of Superior Court reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant chiropractic professional association challenged the order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed that 63 Pa. Stat. § 615, stating that attendance at a conference of a particular chiropractic society would satisfy chiropractic license and registration standards, was unconstitutional under Pa. Const. art. III, § 32 and art. II, § 1, but could be made valid by severing the reference to the particular society.


OVERVIEW: The legislature enacted 63 Pa. Stat. § 615 which provided that chiropractors could fulfill their annual continuing educational requirement for licensing purposes by attending a conference held by the Pennsylvania Chiropractic Society ("PAS"), or an equivalent conference; a PAS conference did not have to be approved as meeting continuing educational requirements, but any other conference did. Appellant, a chiropractic professional association, argued that section 615 was unconstitutional. The court held that section 615 was an unlawful special law under Pa. Const. art. III, § 32 because there was no necessity for the specific exemption from certified compliance with professional educational requirements which the statute extended to a PAS conference; was an unlawful delegation to PAS under Pa. Const. art. II, §1 of the legislative power to determine the requirements of chiropractic continuing education because the statute did not provide any guide or criterion; and that the statute could not be saved by severance of the unconstitutional reference to PAS because this reference was essentially and inseparably connected in substance to the rest of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the Superior Court because the statute in question (stating that attendance at a conference of a particular chiropractic society would satisfy chiropractic license and registration standards), which was unconstitutional under the Pennsylvania Constitution as a special law and improper delegation of legislative power, could not be made valid by rewriting it so as to avoid constitutional infirmities.

De Paul v. Kauffman


No. 329, Jan. T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


441 Pa. 386; 272 A.2d 500; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 1128; 40 A.L.R.3d 810


May 6, 1970, Argued  
January 7, 1971, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, Oct. T., 1968, No. 1045, in case of Peter DePaul et al. v. Samuel Kauffman.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of the decree of the Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), that found the Rent Withholding Act (the Act), 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1700-1, was a valid exercise of the state's police power.


OVERVIEW: Appellants sought review when their rental property was declared unfit for human habitation, and pursuant to the Rent Withholding Act (the Act), 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1700-1, their tenants put their rent monies in an escrow account. Appellants argued that the Act was unconstitutional because it did not define "unfit for human habitation", was vague, and permitted the taking of their property without due process of law. The court affirmed, finding that the Act was constitutional. The court found that the legislature did not have to include every detail of administration in a statute, but could confer discretion in connection with the execution of the statute. It found that the Act was not unconstitutionally vague by failing to include a definition for "unfit for human habitation" because other statutes defined the term so that ample notice was available to a landlord of the standards to which his property must conform. The court found it was not an unconstitutional taking of appellants' property to require them to maintain it to a certain standard because there was a legitimate public purpose in imposing such requirements, which was to insure affordable and safe housing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decree finding the statutory procedure for rent withholding was not an unconstitutional delegation of legislative authority because it failed to define "unfit for human habitation", was not vague, did not permit the taking of appellants' property without due process of law, and was, therefore, a proper exercise of police power.

John Wanamaker, Philadelphia v. School Dist.


No. 645, Jan. T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


441 Pa. 567; 274 A.2d 524; 1971 Pa. LEXIS 1146

January 7, 1971, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia, June T., 1970, No. 3609, in case of John Wanamaker, Philadelphia, John A. Capuzzi, The Budd Company et al. v. The School District of Philadelphia (and The Board of Education of the School District of Philadelphia) and Thomas W. Rogers.



DISPOSITION: Decree reversed and complaint dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district sought review of a decree entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (County), which sustained appellee property owners' challenge to the constitutionality of a municipal business use and occupancy tax.


OVERVIEW: Appellee property owners filed suit to challenge the constitutionality of a municipal tax imposed on the use or occupancy of commercial or industrial real estate. Appellees alleged that the tax was, in actuality, a tax on the real estate itself, not a use tax, and that it violated the Uniformity Clause of the Pennsylvania Constitution, Pa. Const. art. Appellant school district sought review of the trial court's decision. The court determined that the use of property was distinguishable from the ownership of property and that the former could be taxed without running afoul of the Uniformity Clause.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decree entered by the trial court because the municipal tax in question was a valid privilege or excise tax on the use of commercial or industrial real estate and not a tax on the real estate itself.

State Real Estate Com. v. Roberts


No. 32, May T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


441 Pa. 159; 271 A.2d 246; 1970 Pa. LEXIS 513


May 1, 1970, Argued  
November 12, 1970, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court affirming order of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, Commonwealth Docket 1967, No. 190, in case of State Real Estate Commission v. Lenerte Roberts.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant broker sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania) which affirmed the trial court's judgment which affirmed the ruling of appellee State Real Estate Commission, that appellant's refusal to allow appellee to inspect his escrow account was a violation of 63 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 440(a)(11)(v) of the Real Estate Brokers License Act.


OVERVIEW: Appellee State Real Estate Commission found that appellant broker violated 63 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 440(a)(11)(v) of the Real Estate Brokers License Act, by refusing to allow appellee's investigator to inspect appellant's escrow account. The court affirmed, holding that the act required no alleged wrongdoing before an investigation; the privilege against self-incrimination did not apply under the required record doctrine, wherein certain records were required to be kept by law, and the doctrine applied to appellant because he voluntarily entered a field that required state licensing; and the administrative search was within appellee's authority, not too indefinite, and the information was relevant as the act required that the accounts be carefully kept.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment that appellant broker's refusal to allow appellee State Real Estate Commission to inspect appellant's escrow accounts violated the Real Estate Brokers License Act, holding that no allegation of wrongdoing was required for such investigation, the privilege against self-incrimination did not apply, and the search was a reasonable administrative search.

Commonwealth v. Owens


No. 123, March T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


441 Pa. 318; 271 A.2d 230; 1970 Pa. LEXIS 515


May 6, 1970, Argued  
November 12, 1970, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, April T., 1969, No. 193, affirming judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, April T., 1967, No. 50, in case of Commonwealth v. Thomas Lee Owens.



DISPOSITION: Order of Superior Court reversed and judgment of sentence vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the decision of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which had affirmed the trial court's denial of his motion for a new trial and for leave to file a motion for arrest of judgment nunc pro tunc in his conviction for receiving stolen goods.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of receiving stolen goods when the police found him in possession of a stolen gun based on the presumption that his unexplained possession was sufficient proof of his guilt. Although the statute that defined the crime required that appellant knew or had reason to know the goods were stolen, this element was presumed from evidence of mere possession. The court reversed the conviction holding that the knowledge presumption was constitutionally infirm. There was no proof that showed how appellant came into possession of the gun because there was the possibility of possession by innocent acquisition. The court found that the presumption was judicially created, not legislatively, and that there was no basis to invest it with any presumptive merit. Thus, the court granted the motion to arrest and the judgment of sentence was vacated.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower courts' decisions holding that the knowledge presumption was constitutionally infirm because there was no showing how appellant came into possession of the gun, which could have been by innocent acquisition.

It held that the contentions raised in appellant's petition had been either waived or finally litigation and therefore she was not entitled to relief.

Crosson v. Downingtown Area School Dist.


Nos. 262, 263 and 264, Jan. T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


440 Pa. 468; 270 A.2d 377; 1970 Pa. LEXIS 599


April 29, 1970, Argued  
October 9, 1970, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused November 16, 1970.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Chester County, No. 2025 of 1968 and Nos. 2048 and 2049 of 1969, in case of Harry S. Crosson et al. v. Downingtown Area School District.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of a decree entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania). The decree dismissed appellants' actions to enjoin appellees, a school district and its tax collectors, from collecting occupation taxes.


OVERVIEW: Appellee school district levied a tax on occupations as assessed by its county. Attacking both the assessments and the tax resolution, appellant taxpayers brought actions to enjoin appellees, the school district and its tax collectors, from collecting the tax. The trial court entered a final decree that dismissed the complaints, and appellants sought review. The court affirmed. The court stated that there were no substantial contentions that concerned the constitutionality of the assessment; it was the irregular exercise of the power that was questioned. The legislature provided an adequate remedy in the form of appeal from the assessment to a Board of Assessment and Revision of Taxes and from that body's order to a court of common pleas. Thus, a court of equity was not to countenance a direct attack upon the assessments. In the absence of any showing that the tax was based solely on taxpayers' income, appellants' contention that the tax was a disguised income tax that violated state law was without merit. The Uniformity Clause, Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1, applied to occupation taxes. The tax was not in violation of it, for a classification based on the age of 21 was reasonable.


OUTCOME: The trial court's decree that dismissed appellant taxpayers' actions to enjoin appellees, a school district and its tax collectors, from collecting occupation taxes was affirmed because there was no logic in allowing the presence of a constitutional challenge to a tax resolution to justify a collateral, non-constitutional attack in equity on an assessment and because the resolution was not a disguised income tax.

Campbell v. Coatesville Area School Dist.


No. 333, Jan. T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


440 Pa. 496; 270 A.2d 385; 1970 Pa. LEXIS 602


April 30, 1970, Argued  
October 9, 1970, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused November 16, 1970.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Chester County, No. 2038 of 1968, in case of John Campbell et al. v. Coatesville Area School District et al.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania), which held that appellee school district's imposition of an occupation tax was valid.


OVERVIEW: The chancellor entered a decree nisi that dismissed appellant taxpayers' complaint as to appellee school district's imposition of occupation taxes under the Pennsylvania Local Tax Enabling Act, 53 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 6901 et seq. (1970). The trial court dismissed appellants' exceptions to that decree and entered a final decree in appellee's favor. The court affirmed that decree on appeal, and ordered appellants to pay the costs of appeal. The court found that a court of equity did not have jurisdiction or competency to determine the controversy as to appellee's allegedly improper assessment procedures because appellants did not substantially question either the constitutionality of the assessment or that appellee lacked the power to make the assessments. In addition, a statutory appeals route was available to them. The court also rejected appellants' argument that the tax was not timely assessed because the statutory time limit was directory rather than mandatory. The court further held that appellants' did not discharge their heavy burden of proving that the tax resolution violated the Uniformity Clause of Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1 by providing age-based exclusions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed both the trial court's final decree in appellee school district's favor in appellant taxpayers' suit as to occupation taxes and a decree that dismissed appellants' exceptions to an earlier decree and ordered appellant to pay the costs of appeal. The court found that a court of equity lacked jurisdiction because appellants failed to either substantially question the assessment's constitutionality or appellee's power to make it.

 

146 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Appeal of Gro


No. 216, Jan. T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


440 Pa. 552; 269 A.2d 876; 1970 Pa. LEXIS 612


January 15, 1970, Argued  
October 9, 1970, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused November 4, 1970.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County, No. 8699 of 1968, in re appeal of Joseph Gro from decision of Board of Adjustment of Township of Upper Darby.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant residents sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which granted a variance to appellee landowner to permit the erection of 59 apartment units.


OVERVIEW: Appellee landowner applied for a variance from the existing zoned scheme of single home residential use in order to construct 59 apartment units on his land. The trial court granted the variance because it concluded that the property involved was subject to a unique and unnecessary hardship. On review sought by appellant residents, the court found that appellee could not prove that the hardship, which burdened his land, was unnecessary rather than self-inflicted. There was evidence that appellee could not develop his land for residential purposes at a profit because there was no market for homes in the price range that he needed to construct in order to make a profit on his investment. The court held that this reason for requesting a variance did not comprise an unnecessary hardship but rather poor judgment on the part of appellee. The court reversed the order of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the lower court that granted a variance to appellee landowner to construct apartment units over the objection of appellant residents because there was no evidence that the strict application of the terms of the existing zoning statute would result in unnecessary hardship to appellee. Rather, the evidence showed that any hardship to appellee was of his own making and thus, he could not be heard to complain.

 

147 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Riccobene Appeal


No. 425, Jan. T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


439 Pa. 404; 268 A.2d 104; 1970 Pa. LEXIS 711


May 4, 1970, Argued  
July 13, 1970, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused August 5, 1970.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, Feb. T., 1970, No. 213, in re grand jury investigation.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant witness sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which committed him to prison for six months unless he sooner purged himself of contempt by testifying before a grand jury.


OVERVIEW: Asserting his privilege against self-incrimination under U.S. Const. amend. V, defendant witness refused to answer questions put by a grand jury, even though he had been granted immunity under the Pennsylvania Immunity Act, 19 P.S. § 640.1-6. A common pleas court ordered defendant to prison for six months unless he sooner purged himself of contempt by testifying before the grand jury. Defendant sought review, and the court affirmed. The court stated that answers could be compelled regardless of the privilege against self-incrimination if there was immunity from federal and state use of the compelled testimony or its fruits in connection with a criminal prosecution against the person testifying. The court held that 19 P.S. § 640.1-6 provided transactional immunity, which precluded prosecution for any transaction that concerned the testimony that was compelled. The court stated that the conditional nature of the contempt sentence meant that it was for civil contempt. Thus, defendant was not entitled to a jury trial of the matter. The common pleas court had legal and constitutional authority to use its remedial power of civil contempt to force compliance with its lawful order.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order that committed defendant witness to prison for six months unless he testified before a grand jury was affirmed because defendant had no privilege against self-incrimination when he had immunity, and courts have inherent power to force compliance with their lawful orders through civil contempt.

 

148 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


De Jesus v. Liberty Mut. Ins. Co.


No. 222, Jan. T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


439 Pa. 180; 268 A.2d 924; 1970 Pa. LEXIS 676


May 6, 1970, Argued  
July 2, 1970, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, Dec. T., 1961, No. 2834, in case of Epifanio DeJesus v. Liberty Mutual Insurance Company.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured employee sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which granted appellee insurance company's motion for summary judgment in appellant's action alleging that appellee had contractually bound itself to appellant's employer to offer, institute and establish safety procedures, and that appellant had been injured as a result of appellee's failure to fulfill the contract.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employee was injured while in the course of his employment. Appellant brought an action against appellee insurance company, alleging that appellee had contractually bound itself to appellant's employer to establish safety procedures, and by appellee's failure to perform its promise, appellant was injured. Relying upon Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 77 § 481, the trial court granted appellee's motion for summary judgment. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment. Pa. Const. art. III, § 18, precluded the enactment of legislation limiting the amount of compensation payable to employees for injuries other than those arising in the course of their employment. Provisions of the Workmen's Compensation Act, found in Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 77 § 481, limited payment to injured employees by those other than employers, or employers and employees jointly. The court found that the purpose of art. III, § 18, was to permit the general assembly to enact a workmen's compensation program, but to preclude the enactment of general legislation covering injuries other than those arising out of the course of employment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which granted appellee insurance company's motion for summary judgment in appellant employee's action to recover for injuries he sustained in the course of his employment. Provisions of the Workman's Compensation Act that limited an employee's recovery was not violative of the Pennsylvania Constitution, which prohibited legislation that limited payments by those other than employers.

Commonwealth v. Clark


No. 266, Jan. T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


439 Pa. 192; 266 A.2d 741; 1970 Pa. LEXIS 678


March 17, 1970, Argued  
July 2, 1970, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, April T., 1952, No. 287, in case of Commonwealth v. Charles C. Clark.



DISPOSITION: Order granting new trial vacated and case remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution sought review of the grant by the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) of a new trial for defendant as a forum for defendant to raise the issue of denial of speedy trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought review of his conviction for murder, contending that he was denied his right to speedy trial. The trial court, believing that no evidentiary hearings were possible on a post-conviction hearing, ordered a new trial to permit defendant to raise the issue in a pretrial motion and the prosecution sought review. On appeal the court vacated the order and remanded the case for an evidentiary hearing on the motion. The court held that the trial court had the authority to hold evidentiary hearings on post-conviction motions and that a new trial was not an appropriate remedy because if defendant was denied a speedy trial the proper remedy was absolute discharge, rather than another opportunity for the prosecution to bring him to trial.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order for a new trial and remanded for an evidentiary hearing on the speedy trial issue. An evidentiary hearing rather than a new trial was the proper forum to resolve the issue raised by defendant's petition.

 

150 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Haugh


No. 373, Jan. T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


439 Pa. 212; 266 A.2d 657; 1970 Pa. LEXIS 681


April 29, 1970, Argued  
July 2, 1970, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, Oct. T., 1968, No. 1152, affirming judgment of sentence of Criminal Court of Centre County, No. 370 of 1967, in cases of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Rochelle Janoff and Stephen H. Haugh.



DISPOSITION: Order of Superior Court reversed, judgment of sentence of Criminal Court of Centre County reversed, and appellant discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed defendant's conviction for desecrating the American flag under 18 P.S. § 4211 after defendant exposed the United States flag in public, on which was written words regarding war, during a demonstration that protested the involvement of the United States in the Vietnam War.


OVERVIEW: During a demonstration that protested the involvement of the United States in the Vietnam War, defendant exposed a United States flag on which was printed the words, "make love not war" and "the new American revolutionaries." Defendant was convicted of desecrating the American flag under 18 P.S. § 4211. The court reversed the judgment of the lower court, which affirmed the conviction. The court did not reach defendant's argument that § 4211 was unconstitutional. The court found that defendant was not guilty under the statute because § 4211 stated that the section did not apply to political demonstrations. The court agreed with the lower court's finding that defendant was in fact participating in a political demonstration. The court found that if defendant's demonstration was not considered political, it would be difficult to determine if demonstrations were or were not political. Without fair warning and a standard to decide guilt, § 4211 would be violative of due process. The court held that it would not define a political demonstration as one only conducted by the government because such a narrow definition would be arbitrary and would result in a denial of equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's affirmance of defendant's conviction for desecrating the American flag. The court held that defendant's display of the United States flag, on which was written words about war, during a protest of the Vietnam War was a political demonstration, which was not illegal conduct under the statute. The court found that if defendant's conduct was illegal, the statute would have violated due process.

 

151 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Sullivan v. Peters


No. 96, Jan. T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


438 Pa. 460; 265 A.2d 799; 1970 Pa. LEXIS 808


April 22, 1970, Argued  
May 27, 1970, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, No. 67-13905, in case of Robert Sullivan, Frank A. Puchon et al. v. H. Walter Peters et al.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employees sought a declaration that the occupational privilege tax imposed by appellee employer constituted an unconstitutional method of tax collection. They also sought appropriate injunctive relief against the collection of the tax. The Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania) rendered judgment in favor of the employer. The employees sought review.


OVERVIEW: The employees claimed that the enabling statute, the Local Tax Enabling Act (Act 511), 53 P.S. § 6901 et seq. (Supp. 1970), did not authorize a local municipality to pass an ordinance requiring employers to collect an occupational privilege tax. Thus, they argued that the local ordinance was unconstitutional insofar as it provided that the tax was to be collected by employers such as their employer. The court rejected this claim and affirmed the lower court's judgment. The court found that Act 511 authorized certain political subdivisions to levy, assess, and collect or provide for the levying, assessment, and collection of such taxes as they determined. The court held that this was sufficiently broad to permit the tax to be collected directly by a taxpayer's employer, who would forward the taxes to the taxing authority. The court further found that the general provision for the collection of taxes under Act 511 was broad enough to permit employers' collection of the tax. The court held that the collection of the occupational privilege tax by the employer was an efficient and reasonable means of collection and did not prejudice the employees to any extent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment that upheld an employer's method of collection of an occupational privilege tax.
