Rochester & Pittsburgh Coal Co. v. Board of Assessment & Revision


No. 273, March T., 1969


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


438 Pa. 506; 266 A.2d 78; 1970 Pa. LEXIS 815


October 8, 1969, Argued  
May 27, 1970, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Indiana County, June T., 1969, No. 2, in case of Rochester & Pittsburgh Coal Company v. The Board of Assessment and Revision of Taxes of Indiana County.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant company challenged the decree of the Court of Common Pleas of Indiana County (Pennsylvania), which sustained appellee Board of Assessment and Revision of Taxes' objections to appellant's action in equity to enjoin application of a revised method of taxation undertaken by appellee.


OVERVIEW: Appellant company sought review of the decree of the trial court that denied its application for injunctive relief from the application of a revised method of taxation by appellant Board of Assessment and Revision of Taxes. Appellant asserted that it was entitled to injunctive relief because there was an allegation of unconstitutionality in its complaint and the statutory remedy under 72 P.S. § 5453.704 (1943), did not provide for the payment of interest therefore it was inadequate. The court affirmed the decree of the trial court because there was an adequate statutory remedy available to resolve the dispute under § 5453.704 and 12 P.S. § 781 (1859), which provided for the collection of interest on any verdict and entry of every general judgment. Equity jurisdiction applied to cases with a substantial question of constitutionality and an absence of adequate statutory remedy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decree of the trial court because appellant company had an adequate statutory remedy, including the collection of interest on tax payments wrongfully paid, and therefore the equity courts did not have jurisdiction.

Finally, the court remanded the case for further consideration of whether or not appellant should be held in contempt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed an order of the lower court, which issued an injunction restraining appellant husband from proceeding with a divorce action against appellee wife in another state where appellant was not domiciled; however, the court remanded the case for a determination of whether or not appellant was in contempt. Appellant's foreign divorce suit was a sham.

Concord Twp. Appeal


No. 218, Jan. T., 1969


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


439 Pa. 466; 268 A.2d 765; 1970 Pa. LEXIS 717; 48 A.L.R.3d 1190


May 6, 1969, Argued  
February 24, 1970, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused September 14, 1970.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County, No. 8961 of 1967, in re appeal of Kit-Mar Builders, Inc. from findings and order of Board of Adjustment of Concord Township.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant township sought reversal of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which overturned a zoning ordinance after the ordinance had been upheld by the zoning board upon a challenge to the constitutionality of the minimum lot requirements by appellee builder.


OVERVIEW: Appellee builder requested rezoning of land inside appellant township. The application was denied by the zoning board, and appellee challenged the decision in the trial court, arguing that the zoning ordinance as applied was unconstitutional. The trial court reversed, and appellant sought review. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment. Although the trial court erred in making new findings of fact without taking additional testimony, it had independent grounds for affirmance. The ordinance was unconstitutional. Appellant's scheme of zoning had an exclusionary purpose and result. It limited the minimum lot sizes to ones that were much larger than were reasonably necessary for houses. As such, the ordinance was not within appellant's police power and was, instead, a violation of U.S. Const. amends. V and XIV. Appellant's argument that a smaller lot size would create a sewerage problem were without merit, as were appellant's other arguments. The court refused to allow appellant to keep people out, rather than make community improvements A zoning ordinance whose primary purpose was to prevent the entrance of newcomers to avoid future burdens on public services was not valid.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that the zoning ordinance of appellant township was unconstitutional. Appellee builder's request for rezoning should have been approved by the zoning board because the ordinance was an unconstitutional exercise of appellant's police power because it violated the property rights in the fifth and fourteenth amendments. Its purpose was to keep people out rather than to make community improvements.

Warminster Township Municipal Authority v. Department of Highways


No. 8, May T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


437 Pa. 19; 261 A.2d 872; 1970 Pa. LEXIS 842


October 7, 1969, Argued  
January 30, 1970, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused February 27, 1970.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, No. 242 Commonwealth Docket, 1968, in case of Warminster Township Municipal Authority v. Department of Highways, Commonwealth of Pennsylvania et al.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant municipality sought review of the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), which sustained appellee highway department's demurrer to appellant's petition for a writ of mandamus and dismissed appellant's complaint. Appellant and appellee were unable to agree upon an acceptable division of the cost of relocating appellant's sewage and waterworks facilities from a right of way of a state highway.


OVERVIEW: Appellee highway department ordered appellant municipality to relocate its sewage and waterworks facilities from the right of way of a state highway, in connection with the widening of that highway, and offered to pay 50 percent of the relocation cost. Appellant rejected appellee's offer, and they were unable to agree upon an acceptable figure. Appellant requested appellee to petition the Public Utilities Commission (PUC) for a determination of the costs to be borne by each party. Appellee refused, and informed appellant that its offer was withdrawn. Appellant petitioned for mandamus. The trial court sustained appellee's demurrer and dismissed appellant's complaint. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that the statute, which provided that appellee could proceed with the highway work and petition PUC for a determination of the costs to be borne by each party, required appellee to submit any disagreement to PUC once it has made an offer, 36 P.S. § 670-412.1.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court and remanded the record with instructions to grant the writ of mandamus. The court held that the statutory provision that appellee highway department could proceed with the highway work and petition the Public Utilities Commission (PUC) for a determination of the costs to be borne by each party, required appellee to submit any disagreement to PUC once it made an offer.

Commonwealth ex rel. Specter v. Bauer


No. 526, Jan. T., 1969


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


437 Pa. 37; 261 A.2d 573; 1970 Pa. LEXIS 845


November 21, 1969, Argued  
January 30, 1970, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, Aug. T., 1969, No. 412, in case of Commonwealth ex rel. Arlen Specter, District Attorney, v. Edward G. Bauer, Jr., City Solicitor.



DISPOSITION: Decree vacated and case remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant district attorney sought review of the order from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which dismissed his equitable proceeding against appellee city solicitor to determine which party had the right to represent appellant in an action in federal court instituted by a criminal defendant to have a statute declared unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: A criminal defendant instituted an action in federal court to have a statute, under which he was to be prosecuted, declared unconstitutional. Both appellant district attorney and appellee city solicitor made appearances in the case on behalf of the district attorney's office, and both moved to strike the other's appearance. The federal court dismissed both motions, and appellant filed an action in equity in the lower court to enjoin appellee from representing him in federal court. The lower court dismissed the action, and appellant challenged the decision. On appeal the court reversed and held that, pursuant to Pa. Stat. Philadelphia's Home Rule Charter was inapplicable because the city, pursuant to Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 53, § 13133(b), could not interfere with appellant when engaged, pursuant to § 1402(a), in fulfilling his duty of representing the commonwealth's interests in the enforcement of its criminal laws.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order dismissing appellant district attorney's equitable action against appellee city solicitor and held that only appellant could represent the district attorney's office in federal court because appellant had the duty of representing the commonwealth's interests in the enforcement of its criminal laws.

In re Wash. Arbitration Case


No. 193, March T., 1969


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


436 Pa. 168; 259 A.2d 437; 1969 Pa. LEXIS 651; 72 L.R.R.M. 2847; 61 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P52,194


October 1, 1969, Argued  
November 28, 1969, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Washington County, Jan. T., 1969, No. 114, in the matter of arbitration between the City of Washington, Pennsylvania, and the Police Department of the City of Washington, Pennsylvania.



DISPOSITION: Judgment vacated and arbitration award modified.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Washington County (Pennsylvania), which ruled in favor of appellee police department, finding that appellant did not have the right to appeal an arbitration award that required appellant to provide hospitalization coverage to families of appellee. Appellant also filed a petition to allow an appeal under Pa. R. App. P. 68 1/2 directly from the arbitration award.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city challenged a judgment of the lower court that ruled in favor of appellee police department and found that the city did not have a right to appeal an arbitration award requiring, among other things, the city to provide hospitalization coverage to families of the police department. The city also filed a petition to allow an appeal under Pa. R. App. P. 68 1/2 directly from the arbitration award. On appeal, the court vacated the lower court's judgment, holding that the lower court had no jurisdiction to hear the appeal of the arbitration award because the statute under which arbitration was required provided that no appeal was allowed to any court. However, the court granted the city's petition under Rule 68 1/2 and vacated the portion of the award directing the city to pay hospitalization insurance premiums for members of the policemen's families. The court found that the city was prohibited by statute from paying hospitalization insurance premiums for the families of its employees and therefore, it was illegal for the city to comply fully with the arbitration award.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment of the lower court because it had no jurisdiction to hear the city's challenge of the arbitration award in favor of the police department. The court modified the arbitration award to exclude the requirement that the city provide hospitalization insurance coverage to families because it was prohibited by statute from paying hospitalization insurance premiums for the families of its employees.

Commonwealth ex rel. Kelly v. Santo


Nos. 266 and 267, Jan. T., 1969


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


436 Pa. 204; 259 A.2d 456; 1969 Pa. LEXIS 656


May 2, 1969, Argued  
November 28, 1969, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from order of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, Nov. T., 1967, Nos. 163 and 164, in cases of Commonwealth ex rel. Count Kelly v. Gerald Santo, Warden, and Commonwealth ex rel. Major Coxson v. Same.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which denied defendants' petitions for writs of habeas corpus contesting the legality of their arrests for grand larceny and conspiracy to commit grand larceny and proposed extraditions to New York under § 10 of the Uniform Criminal Extradition Act, 19 P.S. § 191.10 (1941).


OVERVIEW: Defendants were indicted in New York for grand larceny and conspiracy to commit grand larceny. Subsequently, defendants were taken into custody in Pennsylvania and confined while awaiting extradition. Defendants instituted habeas corpus proceedings contesting the legality of their arrests and proposed extraditions, pursuant to § 10 of the Uniform Criminal Extradition Act (Act), 19 P.S. § 191.10 (1941). The lower court denied the writs. On appeal, the court affirmed, finding that the record sufficiently established that defendants committed a crime under the laws of New York and that the extradition papers were in proper order. Evidence also established that the conspiracy was hatched by defendants' coconspirators in New York. As participants in the conspiracy, defendants could have been extradited to New York even though they were not in that jurisdiction at any time pursuant to § 6 of the Act, 19 P.S. § 191.6 (1941).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of defendants' petitions for habeas corpus, finding that extradition was properly based on evidence that defendants committed a crime in New York and that the extradition papers were in order. Because defendants participated in a conspiracy borne in New York, they were subject to extradition under the Uniform Criminal Extradition Act even though they had never been in New York.

Commonwealth v. Collins


No. 156, March T., 1969


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


436 Pa. 114; 259 A.2d 160; 1969 Pa. LEXIS 642


May 5, 1969, Argued  
November 11, 1969, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused December 19, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from judgment of Court of Oyer and Terminer of Erie County, No. 1234 of 1967, in case of Commonwealth v. Royall Collins.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Court of Oyer and Terminer of Erie County (Pennsylvania), which entered a jury verdict convicting defendant of second degree murder and sentenced defendant to a term of 10 to 20 years' imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of second degree murder and sentenced to a term of 10 to 20 years' in prison. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the trial court did not err in refusing to grant a new trial on the ground of after-discovered evidence because a witness's repudiation of his testimony at defendant's trial that defendant was involved in the crime was unlikely to compel a different result because the witness subsequently repudiated his repudiation. The court found that the trial court erred in failing to suppress defendant's oral statement because the "waiver" signed by defendant was ineffective as it was undisputed that, at the time he signed it, he had not been informed of the nature of the crime about which he was to be questioned. However, the error was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt because the statement could not have harmed defendant where his own testimony at trial was identical. The trial court also erred in permitting a witness to testify where defendant's evidence showed that the witness was incompetent. However, it was not a reversible error because the evidence pertaining to the witness's alleged mental disorder was submitted to the jury.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment because a witness's repudiation of his testimony at defendant's trial that defendant was involved in the crime was unlikely compel a different result because the witness subsequently repudiated his repudiation. Moreover, the admission of defendant's oral statement taken in violation of his Miranda rights was a harmless error where his own testimony at trial was identical to the statement.

Commonwealth v. Chapasco


No. 24, Jan. T., 1969


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


436 Pa. 143; 258 A.2d 638; 1969 Pa. LEXIS 647


January 6, 1969, Submitted  
November 11, 1969, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Oyer and Terminer of Bucks County, Dec. T., 1950, No. 55, in case of Commonwealth v. Harry Walter Chapasco.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Court of Oyer and Terminer, Bucks County, (Pennsylvania), which convicted defendant of murder in the first degree and sentenced him to life imprisonment without parole.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was jointly indicted with two other individuals for the unlawful killing of the deceased during the perpetration of an armed robbery. Each defendant was tried separately. Each was convicted of murder in the first degree and sentenced to life imprisonment without parole. Defendant sought review of the decision alleging that the admission into evidence of his prior criminal record during his trial constituted a denial of due process. The court affirmed holding that while the prior record and the lower court's action both involved crimes against the person, robbery, and the use or possession of a deadly weapon, that it could not find that the similarity was of itself so prejudicial as to amount to a denial of due process. The court determined that the cumulative weight of the evidence adduced at trial regarding defendant's guilt was overwhelming and in their view more than offset any likelihood that the disputed introduction of defendant's prior criminal record prejudiced the jury's determination on the issue of guilt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of murder and sentence to life imprisonment without parole because the introduction of defendant's prior criminal record did not prejudice the jury's determination on the issue of guilt. The court held that while defendant's prior record and the lower court's action were similar the similarity was not of itself so prejudicial as to amount to a denial of due process.

 

162 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Duggan v. Guild Theatre, Inc.


No. 256, March T., 1969


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


436 Pa. 191; 258 A.2d 858; 1969 Pa. LEXIS 655


October 1, 1969, Argued  
November 11, 1969, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Jan. T., 1969, No. 2692, in case of Duggan v. Guild Theatre, Inc. et al.



DISPOSITION: Decree vacated and case dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant theater sought review of the decree of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which ruled in favor of appellee district attorney, holding that the movie shown by appellant was obscene, and issued a permanent injunction barring its showing.


OVERVIEW: The trial court ruled in favor of appellee district attorney and held that the movie shown by appellant theater was obscene and issued a permanent injunction. On appeal, the court reversed and vacated the trial court's decree granting the injunction. The court held that although appellee had standing generally to initiate an injunctive proceeding against an allegedly obscene movie, the movie shown by appellant was not obscene. The court held that a different standard should not be used when dealing with motion pictures, reasoning that the impact of an obscene work is not always greater on film and that there was no precedent that movies were to be treated differently than other media. The court noted that there was testimony that the dominant theme of the movie was loneliness, and not sexually based. The movie's story of homosexual love, by present moral standards, did not appeal to a viewer's prurient interest in sex. Appellee failed to prove that the movie affronted community standards relating to sexual matters. The court held that the testimony of appellant's witnesses demonstrated that the film had redeeming social value in that it showed the effect of a lack of parental love.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and vacated the trial court's decree enjoining appellant theater from showing a particular film because appellee district attorney failed to demonstrate that the dominant theme of the film was sexual, that the film appealed to a prurient interest in sex, that the film affronted community standards, or that the film had no redeeming social value.

 

163 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


St. Michael Archangel Russian Orthodox Greek Catholic Church v. Uhniat


No. 351, Jan. T., 1967, and No. 254, Jan. T., 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


436 Pa. 222; 259 A.2d 862; 1969 Pa. LEXIS 658


November 12, 1968, Argued  
November 11, 1969, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused January 12, 1970.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from decrees of Court of Common Pleas No. 3 of Philadelphia County, Dec. T., 1962, No. 3343, in case of St. Michael and Archangel Russian Orthodox Greek Catholic Church et al. v. Peter Uhniat et al.



DISPOSITION: Decrees reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, hierarchical church organization and others, sought review of the order of the Court of Common Pleas No. 3 of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), holding that a church was within the ecclesiastic jurisdiction of a competing hierarchical group, in an action brought by appellees, priest and parishioners, seeking an injunction against appellant's interference with their alleged right to control the parish.


OVERVIEW: Appellees, priest and parishioners, brought an action against appellants, hierarchical church organization and others, seeking an injunction against appellants' alleged interference with their right to control the parish. Appellants counterclaimed, seeking an injunction to restrain a competing hierarchical church organization's influence over the church. The lower court ruled that the church was under the jurisdiction of the competing hierarchical church organization. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded, holding that under the Lay Control of Church Property Act, 10 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 81 (1935), the church property remained subject to the control of appellants. The court held that the constitutional protection of hierarchical organization precluded not only legislative and judicial action that would have destroyed church government, but also action by church members themselves that effected a transfer of church property from the control of one hierarchy into the hands of a competing hierarchy.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court transferring control of the church property from appellants, hierarchical organization and others, to a competing group. The court held that the Lay Control Church Property Act precluded the transfer of control of the church property from appellants, and that the church property could not have been transferred simply because the majority of parishioners so desired.

St. John Chrysostom Greek Catholic Church v. Elko


No. 126, March T., 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


436 Pa. 243; 259 A.2d 419; 1969 Pa. LEXIS 660


November 14, 1968, Argued  
November 11, 1969, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused December 31, 1969. *


* Reporter's Note: On June 29, 1970, the Supreme Court of the United States denied a petition for a writ of certiorari.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, April T., 1964, No. 3846, in case of St. John Chrysostom Greek Catholic Church of Pittsburgh et al. v. Nicholas T. Elko et al.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant church member sought review of the decree of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which ruled in favor of appellees, a bishop of the Catholic diocese and the pastor of the church, and held that, for purposes of determining who controlled the church property under the Lay Control of Church Property Act, Act of June 20, 1935, 10 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 81, the church was united spiritually and hierarchically with Rome.


OVERVIEW: Appellant church member initiated suit after appellee pastor abolished the selection of trustees by parish election and instituted a body composed of parishioners appointed by the parish priest. This action was in response to the Lay Control of Church Property Act (Act), Act of June 20, 1935, 10 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 81, which transferred control of church property from the hands of the church laity to the authorized representatives of the church to which the church property was dedicated. The trial court decreed that the church since its founding was united with the Greek Catholic Church under the Pope, or a Uniate church. Thus, under the Act, the church had to remain under the authority of the Roman Pontiff. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the trial court's finding that the church was a Uniate church was supported by evidence in the record of the church's history, including the manner in which the church members redressed their grievances through the channels of the Uniate church, the recitation of faith by new members showing their allegiance to the Pope of Rome, the ordination of Uniate priests at the church, and the church's contributions to the Holy See at Rome.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decree of the trial court finding in favor of appellees, a bishop of the Catholic diocese and the pastor of the church, in appellant church member's action because the trial court's finding that the church was a Uniate church, for purposes of the Lay Control of Church Property Act, was supported by evidence in the record of the church's history.

Shankey v. Staisey


No. 22, March T., 1970


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


436 Pa. 65; 257 A.2d 897; 1969 Pa. LEXIS 634


September 30, 1969, Argued  
October 2, 1969, Decided; October 27, 1969, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Oct. T., 1969, No. 1090, in case of George W. Shankey, Jr. et al. v. Leonard C. Staisey et al.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed. *



* Reporter's Note: On January 19, 1970, a petition for writ of certiorari was denied by the Supreme Court of the United States.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Board of Elections of Allegheny County challenged the order of Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which found in favor of appellant candidates for elected office and held that 1937 Pa. Laws 1333, art. XIV, § 1405, 25 P.S. § 3155 (Supp. 1969) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellee candidates for elected office were members of the Constitutional Party. Appellant Board of Elections of Allegheny County refused to certify the votes cast for appellees in the county primary because the number of votes cast for each appellee did not satisfy the requirements of 1937 Pa. Laws 1333, art. XIV, § 1405, 25 P.S. § 3155 (Supp. 1969). The trial court found that the statute violated Pa. Const. art. I, § 5and the Equal Protection Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court noted that the statute created a classification as to placement on the general election ballot between those who had shown the mandated amount of public support and those who had not. The court found that the avoidance of an unduly complicated ballot was a reasonable basis for this distinction. The statute also distinguished between statewide elections and local elections because candidates of local elections needed to receive a larger percentage of votes cast in the general election to be considered a political party than candidates in a state election. The court reversed the order because the statute did not burden freedom of association and the regulations were valid and reasonable.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment declaring the election statute unconstitutional because the classifications created by the statute were reasonable and did not violate the Equal Protection Clause of the United States Constitution.

Silver v. Zoning Bd. of Adjustment

DISPOSITION: Order reversed and case remanded to board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant apartment building owner sought review of the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which denied appellant relief by upholding the constitutionality of Section 14-104(3)(b) of the Philadelphia Zoning Code of 1962.


OVERVIEW: Appellant apartment building owner owned a non-conforming apartment building in a district that was zoned residential. The building was constructed in 1926 and contained 33 units at that time. In April 1965, appellant applied for a use permit to increase the number of units to 50; the increase was to be accomplished solely by subdividing the larger apartments. Appellee zoning board denied appellant's use permit. The controversy before the courts focused on the constitutionality of § 14-104(3)(b) of the Philadelphia Zoning Code of 1962. The court found that this section violated due process and struck it as unconstitutional. It found that appellee could not indiscriminately forbid all natural expansion of non-conforming apartment buildings. The matter was remanded to appellee for proceedings consistent with the court's opinion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's order because appellee zoning board could not prohibit per se the natural expansion of a non-conforming use.

Harney v. Russo

435 Pa. 183; 255 A.2d 560; 1969 Pa. LEXIS 708; 71 L.R.R.M. 2817


March 19, 1969, Argued  
June 27, 1969, Decided

DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants borough council members sought review of the order of Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which denied appellants' preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer, and granted the motion of appellee, police officers and police chief, for a summary judgment in appellees' suit to compel enforcement of an arbitration award under 43 P.S. § 217.1.


OVERVIEW: Appellees, police officers and police chief, filed a suit against appellants borough council members for the enforcement of an arbitrator's award. The lower court denied appellants' demurrer and granted appellees' motion for summary judgement. Appellants challenged the order on the grounds that 43 P.S. § 217.1 violated the state and United States constitutions. The court held that as Pa. Const. art. III, § 31 as amended specifically permitted binding arbitration with regard to disputes involving police or firefighters, § 217.1 was insulated from constitutional overthrow. The court held that the one man, one vote rule was not violated because an arbitral panel was not a legislative office even though the arbitrators, through an award, could affect the spending of public funds. The court affirmed the order.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order because the statute authorizing binding arbitration in disputes between public employers and police officers or firefighters did not violate the state constitution or the fourteenth amendment of the United States constitution.

Commonwealth v. Bruno


Nos. 75, 76, January Term, 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


435 Pa. 200; 255 A.2d 519; 1969 Pa. LEXIS 710


January 11, 1968, Argued  
June 27, 1969, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued May 1, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from order of Court of Oyer and Terminer and General Jail Delivery of the Peace of Montgomery County, April T., 1966, No. 279, and order of Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, No. 67-8338, in cases of Commonwealth v. John Harry Bruno; and Commonwealth ex rel. John Harry Bruno v. Francis Nailon, Warden.



DISPOSITION: Orders affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which denied his petition for a writ of habeas corpus seeking to overturn his prior commitment to a state hospital based on a sanity commissions report which determined that he was incompetent to stand trial on five counts of murder. Defendant contended that the sanity commission violated U.S. Const. amend. VI and Pa. Const. art. I, §§ 6, 9.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, after a hearing before a sanity commission, was found incompetent to proceed to trial on five counts of murder. A lower court denied his writ of habeas corpus to overturn the order of commitment. Defendant contended that the order of commitment denied his U.S. Const. amend. VI and Pa. Const. art. I, §§ 6,9 rights to a jury trial. The court held that defendant was not entitled to a jury trial to determine whether he was competent to stand trial. The court denied that defendant's commitment was a penalty more severe than what a trial could impose and that his constitutional rights were violated by the commitment without a trial. The court also denied defendant's claim that he was entitled to a speedy trial, that hearsay was error in his commitment proceeding, and that his constitutional right to confrontation and Miranda rights were violated. The court held that none of these rights were applicable because the sanity commission hearing was not criminal in character because it was only used to determine defendant's competence, not his guilt or innocence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders of the lower court, which affirmed defendant's commitment to a state hospital after having been ruled incompetent to stand trial on five counts of murder. The court held that defendant's sixth amendment rights to a jury trial, that his speedy trial rights, right to confrontation, right to have hearsay excluded, and Miranda rights were not violated because the sanity commission hearing was not criminal in character.

Commonwealth v. Mayberry


Nos. 395, 396, 397, 398, 399, January Term, 1966


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


435 Pa. 290; 255 A.2d 548; 1969 Pa. LEXIS 721


November 11, 1968, Submitted  
June 27, 1969, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from judgment of Court of Quarter Sessions of Philadelphia County, Oct. T., 1963, Nos. 1586 and 1588, in case of Commonwealth v. Richard Mayberry.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the conviction of the Court of Quarter Sessions of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of contempt and sentenced him to a total of five years imprisonment, arising out of defendant's actions at his trial for perjury.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for contempt due to his actions that were committed in the presence of the trial court, which included throwing a book at the judge and disrespectful comments. The court of appeals found that pursuant to 17 P.S. § 2041 defendant's conduct was so offensive to the administration of justice that conviction and sentence were appropriate and justified. Defendant contended that the summary procedure used in his case was unconstitutional, because he was denied a trial by jury. The court affirmed, and found that the tradition of nonjury trials for contempts was more firmly established than the view that states could have dispensed with jury trials in normal criminal prosecutions. The court determined that defendant's case began long before a case came down that defendant relied on, which held that the constitutional guarantees of jury trial extended to serious criminal contempts. The court held that the case relied on by defendant did not need to be applied retroactively. The court also determined that, the adverse effects on the administration of justice of invalidating all serious contempt convictions would likely have been substantial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's contempt conviction, because a subsequent case that defendant relied on, which extended jury trials to serious criminal contempt cases did not need to be applied retroactively, and because of the likely adverse effects on the administration of justice that would have been caused by invalidating all serious contempt convictions.

 

170 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Mayberry Appeal


Nos. 102, 103, 104, 105, 106, 107, 108, 109, 110, 111, 112, 113, March Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


434 Pa. 478; 255 A.2d 131; 1969 Pa. LEXIS 481


November 12, 1968, Argued  
April 23, 1969, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from judgments of Court of Oyer and Terminer of Allegheny County, Sept. T., 1965, No. 4672, in case of Commonwealth v. Herbert F. Langnes, Richard Mayberry et al.



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Court of Oyer and Terminer of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) for criminal contempt based on appellant's verbal abuse of the trial judge during various phases of the trial to disrupt the proceedings.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was found guilty on counts of holding hostages in a penal institution and prison breach. Acting as his own trial counsel, appellant subjected the judge to verbal abuse and other misconduct to intentionally disrupt the trial. The judge did not warn appellant about his contemptuous conduct during trial. At sentencing, appellant was given consecutive sentences for the prison-related convictions, and 11 separate consecutive sentences for the contempt offenses of no less than one year and no more than two years. The contempt offenses were not tried to a jury; nor did appellant receive notice thereof prior to sentencing. On appeal, the court affirmed, finding that appellant's contempt convictions occurred two years before the U.S. Supreme Court imposed a jury requirement for criminal contempt cases punishable by a two year prison sentence. Due process is not required for contempt committed in the presence of a judge in open court, and the trial judge did not have a duty to warn appellant during the trial of his contemptuous conduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's convictions for criminal contempt. A jury trial was not required at the time sentences were imposed, due process is not required for contempt committed in the presence of a judge in open court, and the trial judge did not have a duty to warn appellant during the trial of his contemptuous conduct.

Pennsylvania Public Utility Com. v. Port Authority of Allegheny County

November 16, 1967, Argued  
April 23, 1969, Decided

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from order of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, No. 2813 Equity Docket, 1967, in case of Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission et al. v. Port Authority of Allegheny County.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, commission and corporation, sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), which granted a preliminary injunction in favor of appellee port authority in appellee's suit regarding an application for transportation rights.


OVERVIEW: Appellant corporation applied to appellant commission for group and party bus transportation rights. Appellee port authority objected, arguing that appellant commission lacked jurisdiction over the application. The reviewing court found that appellee was properly awarded a preliminary injunction. There were reasonable grounds for the actions of the court below. Injunctions against appellant commission were sanctioned under 66 P.S. § 1441 when its jurisdiction was in doubt. 55 P.S. § 563.1 clearly appeared to give exclusive jurisdiction over the application to appellee. Accordingly, appellee raised a very serious question of appellant commission's power to entertain the application and was entitled to the injunction.


OUTCOME: A preliminary injunction in favor of appellee port authority in its suit against appellants, commission and corporation, regarding an application for transportation rights was affirmed because appellee raised a very serious question of appellant commission's power to entertain the application.

Stander v. Kelley


No. 38, May Term, 1969


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


433 Pa. 406; 250 A.2d 474; 1969 Pa. LEXIS 576


January 6, 1969, Argued  
February 7, 1969, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, No. 197 Commonwealth Docket, 1968, No. 2859 Equity Docket, in case of Irvin Stander et al. v. Joseph J. Kelley, Jr., Secretary of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed. *



* Reporter's Note: On June 23, 1969, the Supreme Court of the United States dismissed an appeal to it for want of jurisdiction, and, treating the papers whereon the appeal was taken as a petition for a writ of certiorari, denied certiorari.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of a decision by the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), which denied the appellants relief in their action against appellee, the Secretary of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania. The appellant sought an injunction against a vote on a proposed amendment to the state Constitution, which wholly amended the Pa. Const. art. V, relating to the judiciary.


OVERVIEW: The eligible voters of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania (commonwealth) adopted several amendments to the commonwealth's Constitution, including a complete revision of Pa. Const. art. Appellant taxpayers filed an action against appellee, the Secretary of the Commonwealth, in an effort to enjoin a vote by the electorate on the proposed constitutional amendments and to hold the amendments null and void. The lower court found in favor of the appellee. On review, the court affirmed and held that the proposed constitutional amendment did not violate the traditional and fundamental concepts inherent in the separation of jurisdiction and powers among the three branches of government. The court explained that it would consider the constitutionality of a particular provision of the new Constitution, if properly raised, but such a determination would not affect the constitutionality of the judiciary article.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, holding that the proposed constitutional amendment did not offend Separation of Powers principles. Accordingly, appellant taxpayers were not entitled to relief in the form of an injunction against a vote by the electorate on the proposed constitutional amendment.

Commonwealth v. Littlejohn


Nos. 372, 445, January Term, 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


433 Pa. 336; 250 A.2d 811; 1969 Pa. LEXIS 573


November 11, 1968, Submitted  
January 24, 1969, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused March 21, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from orders of Court of Oyer and Terminer of Philadelphia County, Aug. T., 1959, Nos. 1062 and 1063, in case of Commonwealth v. John Littlejohn, and Sept. T., 1962, No. 1710, in case of Commonwealth v. Leonard Archambault.



DISPOSITION: Appeal, No. 372, order denying appeal vacated, appeal allowed, and judgment of sentence affirmed; appeal No. 445, order vacated and case remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, convicted murderers in unrelated cases, did not file direct appeals as of right under 19 P.S. § 1186 from their murder convictions in the Court of Oyer and Terminer of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania). Appellants alleged they did not file appeals because they feared receiving the death penalty on retrial. Appellants sought relief under the Post Conviction Hearing Act.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were each convicted of murder and sentenced to life, in unrelated cases. The appellants alleged that they had chosen not to appeal as of right, as they were allowed under 19 P.S. § 1186, because they feared the death penalty if they were retried. Each appellant sought relief under the Post Conviction Hearing Act, and their cases were consolidated. The court concluded that, in the event appellants or any convicted murderers filed direct appeals and gained a retrial, the commonwealth could not seek the death penalty in the retrial, because such a retrial violated appellants' constitutional guarantees of due process, equal protection, and the right to be free from double jeopardy. As to one appellant, the court allowed the appeal, considered the merits of the appeal, and affirmed the sentence. The other case was remanded for a factual determination as to whether the appellant had foregone his right to appeal because of the threat of the death penalty.


OUTCOME: As to one appellant, the court allowed his appeal, and affirmed the sentence. The other case was remanded to determine whether the appellant had foregone his right to appeal because of the threat of the death penalty. The court held that the prosecution may not seek the death penalty upon a retrial of a murder case in which the defendant received a life sentence, based on due process, equal protection, and double jeopardy.

 

175 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


McGrath v. Staisey


No. 225, March Term, 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


433 Pa. 8; 249 A.2d 280; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 480


October 9, 1968, Argued  
November 27, 1968, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused February 6, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Jan. T., 1968, No. 196, in case of James F. McGrath v. Leonard C. Staisey et al.



DISPOSITION: Order vacated and case remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant patrolman challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania),which sustained appellee county commissioners' demurrer to appellant's mandamus action seeking to compel his promotion to sergeant and damages.


OVERVIEW: Appellant patrolman took a written civil service examination for the position of sergeant. Appellant placed third on the eligible list, but three positions for sergeant were subsequently filled by patrolmen lower on the eligible list. Appellant sought a writ of mandamus to compel his promotion by appellee county commissioners to sergeant and for damages due to the pay differential. The trial court sustained appellees' demurrer. On appeal, the court vacated and remanded. The court, accepting appellant's interpretation of Pa. Stat. Ann. tit 16, § 4516, stated that the only way to ascertain merit was by a written test. Other criteria were not authorized. Further, the court found that language in the Second Class Cities Code supported its position. Next, the court found no support for appellees' argument that the interpretation adopted by appellant was an unconstitutional restriction for the discretion of the appointing authority. Finally, the court found that it was not necessary for appellant to join as indispensable parties those individuals appointed from lower positions on the list.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the trial court's denial of appellant patrolman's request for a writ of mandamus for promotion to sergeant because appellee county commissioners were not authorized under the promotions statute to use any criteria for promotion other than by written test. The cause was remanded to the trial court.

Basehore v. Hampden Industrial Dev. Authority


No, 91 Miscellaneous Docket No. 16, No. 167, March Term, 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


433 Pa. 40; 248 A.2d 212; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 481


April 24, 1968, Argued  
November 22, 1968, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued June 27, 1968.


PRIOR HISTORY: Special certiorari to Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County, Jan. T., 1968, No. 3, in equity, in case of Mark A. Basehore, in his own right and on behalf of all other citizens, residents, and taxpayers of township, v. Hampden Industrial Development Authority et al.; Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Butler County, June T., 1968, No. 6, in equity, in case of James E. Walker, in his own right and on behalf, etc., v. Butler County Industrial Development Authority et al.



DISPOSITION: In Basehore, injunction denied; in Walker, decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In this consolidated case appellant taxpayers sought review of judgments rendered by the Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County (Pennsylvania) and the Court of Common Pleas of Butler County (Pennsylvania), which were in favor of appellee industrial development authorities.


OVERVIEW: Two cases were consolidated for the purpose of this appeal. In both, appellant taxpayers sought to enjoin appellee industrial development authorities acting on acquisition agreements entered into by appellees. Pursuant to the agreements, appellees were to purchase industrial development property. The companies were to use funds generated by the sale of the revenue bonds issued by appellees to construct manufacturing plants. The companies were committed to long term leases of the property and were to pay an amount equal to the taxes they would have paid had the property not been owned by a tax exempt entity. The court determined that the bonds constituted neither debt nor an extension of credit to a private entity. The court determined that public funds were being used for a public purpose because of the generation of industry and employment. The court ruled in favor of appellees, and denied one appellant's request for injunction and affirmed a decree in favor of the other appellee.


OUTCOME: The court denied appellant taxpayer's request for injunction against appellee industrial development authority in the first case, and affirmed the decree in favor of appellee industrial development authority in the second, having determined that appellees actions were constitutional.

 

177 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Guild Theatre, Inc.


No. 229, March Term, 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


432 Pa. 378; 248 A.2d 45; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 533


October 7, 1968, Argued  
November 12, 1968, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Oct. T., 1968, No. 888, in case of Commonwealth v. Guild Theatre, Inc. et al.



DISPOSITION: Injunction vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant theater sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which granted an injunction restraining defendant from exhibiting a movie and overruled defendant's preliminary objections to the injunction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant theater sought review of an order of the trial court which granted an injunction restraining defendant from exhibiting a movie and overruling defendant's preliminary objections. The district attorney filed a complaint alleging that the movie was obscene and requesting an injunction, defendant received no notice of the filing of the complaint, nor of the hearing which took place later that evening at which the injunction was issued. The court first admonished that the injunction was improperly granted because there was no attempt to give notice of the hearing despite there being no irreparable injury as to justify the abandonment of the precious safeguard of an adversary hearing. The court also admonished that even if a proper hearing had been held the motion picture was protected by the constitutional guarantee of freedom of speech until judicially adjudged obscene. The fact that defendant may have been offered a full dress hearing within four days of the original restraint was insufficiently prompt. Accordingly, the injunction was vacated.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the injunction because it was improperly granted where there was no attempt to give defendant theater notice of the hearing on the injunction, and even if there had been a proper hearing the motion picture was protected by the constitutional guarantee of freedom of speech until judicially adjudged obscene. Finally, a full hearing within four days of the original restraint was insufficient to justify the preliminary injunction.

Commonwealth v. Kirby Estates, Inc.


No. 40, May Term, 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


432 Pa. 103; 246 A.2d 120; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 490


May 21, 1968, Argued  
October 3, 1968, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, No. 44 Commonwealth Docket, 1966, in case of Commonwealth v. Kirby Estates, Inc.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Commonwealth appealed the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), which imposed a franchise tax upon appellee taxpayer. Appellee was in the business of investments and those activities were carried on outside Pennsylvania.


OVERVIEW: Appellee taxpayer was in the business of investments, which was conducted outside Pennsylvania. Appellee acquired real estate in Pennsylvania that it rented to tenants. In computing its 1962 franchise tax, appellee took the depreciated value of its real estate and subtracted therefrom its mortgage balance. Commonwealth settled the tax by including both the real estate and securities in determining the value of taxpayer's capital stock. On appeal, the trial court deemed the correct tax to be that which appellee reported. The court affirmed. The court found that for purposes of computing the franchise tax imposed under 72 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1871, the value of the taxpayer's capital stock not used in connection with the privilege of doing business in Pennsylvania had to be eliminated from the computation. Here, appellee's presence in Pennsylvania was limited to ownership of real estate and its investment activity outside Pennsylvania was unconnected therewith.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, which reduced appellee taxpayer's franchise tax, because the commonwealth could only not include both appellee's real estate and investment activities in the computation of the tax. The investment activities were conducted wholly outside Pennsylvania and bore no relation to the exercise of the privilege of doing business in the state.

Commonwealth v. Dillworth


Nos. 256, 278, January Term, 1965


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


431 Pa. 479; 246 A.2d 859; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 644


November 16, 1965, Argued  
October 3, 1968, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued May 3, 1966, and November 29, 1967.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from orders of Superior Court, Oct. T., 1964, No. 270, affirming order of County Court of Philadelphia, Feb. T., 1964, No. 4021, and Oct. T., 1965, No. 42, affirming order of County Court of Philadelphia, July T., 1964, No. 4084, in cases of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. David J. Dillworth, and Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Emmett A. Murphy.



DISPOSITION: Orders reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of orders from the superior court (Pennsylvania), which denied their requests for jury trials in two actions for child support consolidated for argument to determine whether the legislature unconstitutionally abrogated a putative father's right to a jury trial to determine paternity under 62 P.S. § 2043.31 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Petitions for child support were brought on behalf of two mothers against two defendants. Because the two actions involved the identical question, they were consolidated for argument. One defendant was charged with fornication and bastardy, that was later dropped, and the other petition did not cite any statute. The trial court, in both actions, denied defendants' requests for a jury trial, determined paternity, and ordered them to pay child support. The common question was whether the legislature, in enacting the Civil Procedural Support Act, 62 P.S. § 2043.31 et seq., intended to abrogate a putative father's right to a jury trial to decide paternity in violation of Pa. Const. art. I, § 9. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded, finding that statutes which tended to infringe on the right to trial by jury had to be strictly construed. The right to jury trial had been deemed so important that the court had previously held that absent specific legislative authorization, when a defendant pleaded not guilty, the courts had no power to try the case without a jury, even if the defendant waived his right to a jury trial.


OUTCOME: Consolidated orders that denied requests for a jury trial by putative fathers to determine paternity were reversed because statutes that tended to infringe on the right to trial by jury had to be strictly construed.

 

182 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


In re Absentee Ballots Case (No.1)


No. 1, January Term, 1969


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


431 Pa. 165; 245 A.2d 258; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 607


May 20, 1968, Argued  
September 4, 1968, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Lackawanna County, Nov. T., 1967, No. 469, in re canvass of absentee ballots of 1967 general election.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant candidates for county commissioner positions sought review of a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Lackawanna County (Pennsylvania). The trial court upheld a ruling by the county board of elections, which rejected a challenge by appellants to the manner in which absentee ballots were counted.


OVERVIEW: A general county election was held to elect three county commissioners. The county board of elections tallied the absentee votes and applied the tallies to the districts in which the absentee voters resided. Appellant candidates objected to the counting of the absentee ballots. The county board of elections ruled against appellants, and the trial court upheld the ruling. The court affirmed. The court rejected appellants' argument that 25 P. S. § 3146.8, which provided that the county board of elections had to canvass all absentee ballots, was unconstitutional. The framers of Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 19 (currently Pa. Const. art. VII, § 14) did not intend that the actual counting of absentee ballots had to occur in local election districts rather than on a county-wide basis. To require hearings on contested absentee ballots in 243 places, rather than in one centralized location, was highly impractical. The constitutional framers intended not that the local election districts would count the absentee ballots, but that the computation of the absentee votes would appear on the return in the district where it belonged. The county board of elections had complied with this procedure.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that rejected appellant candidates' challenge to the manner in which absentee ballots were counted in an election for county commissioners. As long as the computation of the absentee votes appeared on the local election district where it belonged, the absentee ballots could be tabulated on a county-wide basis, rather than in the local election districts.

In re Absentee Ballots Case (No.2)


No. 44, May Term, 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


431 Pa. 178; 245 A.2d 265; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 608


May 20, 1968, Argued  
September 4, 1968, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of York County, Miscellaneous Docket YY, 80, 81 and 82 of 1967, in re 223 absentee ballot appeals.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant candidate sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas of York County (Pennsylvania) that denied his constitutional challenge to the tabulation of absentee ballots by the county election board rather than the district election board in violation of Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 19.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was a candidate for a state senate seat. After ballots were cast, the county board of elections refused his motion to stop counting absentee ballots to prevent a violation of Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 19. The trial court also denied his petition to stop the board's tabulation of the absentee ballots in which appellant reasserted the unconstitutionality of the Election Code, 25 Pa. Const. Stat. Ann. §§ 3146.1-3146.9. Appellant argued that the district election board, and not the county election board, should have counted the absentee ballots. On appeal, the court affirmed, finding that a district election board only sat on one election day. After they tabulated the results on the voting machines or written ballots, their operation was at an end. The county board was a permanent body functioning continuously. Absentee ballots continued to arrive after the district election board ended. Nowhere in the constitution was there any requirement that absentee ballots be canvassed by a district election board and by no one else.


OUTCOME: The order denying a constitutional challenge to tabulation of absentee ballots by a county board of elections was affirmed because an act of the general assembly would not be declared unconstitutional unless it clearly, palpably and plainly violated the constitution.

Bethlehem Steel Co. v. Board of Finance & Revenue


No. 18, May Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


431 Pa. 1; 244 A.2d 767; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 581


May 24, 1967, Argued  
August 6, 1968, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued April 18, 1968.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, No. 318 Commonwealth Docket, 1962, in case of Bethlehem Steel Company v. Board of Finance and Revenue.



DISPOSITION: Case remanded to Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation challenged an order of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), dismissing appellant's complaint in mandamus seeking to compel appellee board of finance and revenue to refund corporate net income taxes paid by appellant. Appellant contended that its petitions seeking a refund were timely filed, under 72 P. S. § 503.


OVERVIEW: The question presented on this appeal was whether certain petitions seeking the refund of corporate net income taxes were timely filed with appellee board of finance and revenue board. The board denied appellant corporation's petitions for refund for lack of jurisdiction under 72 P. S. § 503 of the Fiscal Code, in that the petitions were not timely filed. Appellant instituted an action of mandamus against appellee to compel it to grant the refunds. Under the provisions of that section, ordinarily, a refund petition had to be filed within two years of the payment of the tax involved or the settlement of the tax involved, whichever date was later, but, in other special statutorily-delineated situations, a five-year period was available dating from the payment of the tax involved. It was appellant's position that its tax refund petitions were timely filed within both the five year limitation period and the two year limitation period. The court found that appellant was not entitled to a refund because it failed to timely file within the two-year limitation period.


OUTCOME: The court found that appellant corporation's refund petitions to appellee board of finance and revenue were not timely filed by appellant within the applicable statute of limitation, which provided for filing within two years of the payment of the tax involved, and the order of the court vacating a previous order was reaffirmed.

Philadelphia v. Depuy


Nos. 3, 4, 8, May Term, 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


431 Pa. 276; 244 A.2d 741; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 619


January 4, 1968, Argued  
August 6, 1968, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, No. 2390 Equity Docket and No. 555 Commonwealth Docket, 1959, in case of City of Philadelphia, United Gas Improvement Company and Borough of Chambersburg v. Warner M. Depuy, Secretary of Revenue.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant gas companies sought review of a decree from the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), which held that a provision of Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 2181 (1967) that imposed different tax treatment on gas and electric companies was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant municipal gas companies challenged the constitutionality of a statute that permitted the imposition of an annual tax on its gross receipts by appellee tax authority. Appellants alleged that Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 2181 (1967) violated the uniformity clause of Pa. Const. art. IX, § 1 and U.S. Const. amend. XIV because it unconstitutionally permitted different tax treatment between municipal gas and electric companies. The trial court determined that the different tax treatment was constitutionally permissible. The court affirmed the decree of the trial court, finding that appellants failed to carry their heavy burden of showing that there existed no reasonable basis for distinguishing gas from electric companies. The court held that the legislature could distinguish, for purposes of taxation, activities which were sufficiently unique despite the fact that the subjects so taxed were but one segment of a larger class engaged in similar activity. The court concluded that apart from the similarity of functions, it could not be gainsaid that gas and electricity were distinct power sources, each sufficiently unique to sustain separate tax treatment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a decree which held that a statute that imposed different tax treatment on appellant gas companies was constitutional. The court found that appellants failed to carry their heavy burden of showing that there existed no reasonable basis for distinguishing gas from electric companies. The court held that the legislature could distinguish for purposes of taxation activities that were sufficiently unique.

Dombrowski v. Philadelphia


No. 178, January Term, 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


431 Pa. 199; 245 A.2d 238; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 612


January 16, 1968, Argued  
August 6, 1968, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused September 16, 1968.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, March T., 1967, No. 2348, in case of Edwin L. Dombrowski v. City of Philadelphia, James H. J. Tate, Mayor, Paul D'Ortona, president of council, et al.



DISPOSITION: Order, as modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged a decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which found that the appropriations made by appellant were insufficient to maintain the retirement system in an actuarially sound condition and ordered appellant to allocate sufficient funds to the retirement system. Appellant contended that appellee employee's standing to institute a mandamus action and scope of the order entered.


OVERVIEW: Appellee employee, in employ of appellant city, instituted an action of mandamus to compel appellant to make the necessary appropriations. The lower court found that the appropriations made by appellant were insufficient to maintain the retirement system in an actuarially sound condition. The lower court ordered appellant to allocate sufficient funds until all of appellee's retirement benefits had been paid, so as to meet the normal cost plus interest on accrued liability. Appellant contended appellee's standing to institute a mandamus action and the scope of the order. On appeal, the court affirmed the modified order of the lower court, finding that appellee had a right independent of the public to support a mandamus action brought as a private litigant. The court stated that appellee had satisfied the conditions placed by a retirement system, his right to retirement benefits had become vested and that a contractual relationship had been established between the parties. The court vacated part of order that directed appellant to place sufficient funds to cover normal cost plus interest, and found it violative because it contained a directive for the performance of a future act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's order, as modified, finding that appellee employee had sufficient interest to support a mandamus action brought in his private capacity. Appellee's interest in his retirement benefits was vested and he had entered into a contractual relationship with appellant city. The court vacated part of the order that required appellant to place funds until all of appellee's retirement benefits had been paid.

Commonwealth ex rel. Goldsmith v. Myers


No. 199, January Term, 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


430 Pa. 385; 243 A.2d 429; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 714


April 29, 1968, Argued  
July 1, 1968, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas No. 6 of Philadelphia County, March T., 1965, No. 5224, in case of Commonwealth ex rel. Walter Goldsmith v. David N. Myers, Superintendent.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Court of Common Pleas No. 6 of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed defendant's petition for writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: The trial court denied defendant's petition for writ of habeas corpus, which defendant filed after being convicted of murder in the first degree. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's order because defendant's constitutional rights were not violated by the admission at trial of defendant's confessions. The court determined that the only conclusion that was supported by the evidence was that the confessions were wholly voluntary and not coerced by any force, threats of force, or by any other means. The failure of the court stenographer to completely transcribe the testimony at trial did not entitle defendant to a writ of habeas corpus because defendant did not allege how he had been harmed. The court ruled that defendant could not use the mere passage of time as an asserted basis of prejudice against him where defendant waited 10 years before taking any action with regard to alleged deprivation of rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order dismissing defendant's petition. The court held that there was no basis in the record for concluding that defendant's confessions were coerced and that defendant's failure to point to any harm that resulted from his not having a complete transcript of testimony was not justified by the mere passage of time.

Commonwealth v. Satchell


No. 306, January Term, 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


430 Pa. 443; 243 A.2d 381; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 727


April 15, 1968, Submitted  
July 1, 1968, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Oyer and Terminer of Philadelphia County, Dec. T., 1964, No. 627, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Ernest Satchell.



DISPOSITION: Order vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order from the Court of Oyer and Terminer of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) that dismissed a petition for post-conviction relief from a conviction of murder. Appellant contended that the Post Conviction Hearing Act, 19 P.S. § 1180-4, unconstitutionally permitted a court to infer a waiver of issues not previously raised.


OVERVIEW: Appellant pleaded guilty to murder and was serving a life sentence. After an evidentiary hearing, appellant's first post-conviction petition filed by counsel was denied. A second petition was denied, without a hearing. Appellant argued that the Post Conviction Hearing Act, 19 P.S. §§ 1180-4(b) and (c), was unconstitutional because it permitted the post-conviction court to infer a waiver of issues not raised by counsel in a first petition. On appeal, the court vacated and remanded with instructions that appellant was to be permitted to amend his petition. No petition was to be dismissed for want of particularity unless the petitioner was first given an opportunity to clarify his petition. The lower court's dismissal without a hearing amounted to a dismissal for want of particularity. The court found no merit in appellant's constitutional challenge to the presumption of waiver contained in the post-conviction relief statute, as previous courts had already upheld the provisions that were identical to the procedures employed by the federal courts.


OUTCOME: An order dismissing a petition was vacated and remanded because a dismissal of a post-conviction petition without giving leave to amend was a dismissal for want of particularity, and no petition was to be so dismissed unless the petitioner was first given an opportunity to clarify his petition.

Honey Brook v. Alenovitz


No. 286, January Term, 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


430 Pa. 614; 243 A.2d 330; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 752


April 29, 1968, Argued  
July 1, 1968, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused July 29, 1968.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from decree of Court of Common Pleas of Chester County, No. 1864 of 1966, in case of Township of Honey Brook v. George C. Alenovitz, Jean E. Alenovitz, his wife, Clement Alenovitz et al.



DISPOSITION: Decree affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged the decision from the lower court (Pennsylvania) granting a permanent injunction to appellee township enjoining appellants from operating, establishing or constructing a trailer park and to remove all facilities and equipment placed on the premises. The court found that appellants had failed to comply with both a zoning ordinance and a trailer park ordinance.


OVERVIEW: On appeal from a grant of injunction to appellee township prohibiting appellants from constructing a trailer park, the court affirmed, holding that the land was zoned "Farm Residential." Trailer parks were permitted only in districts zoned "Neighborhood Commercial," when allowed by special exception. The court held that questions involving the constitutionality of zoning ordinances should not be heard by courts of equity, but should be tested through the ordinary zoning channels, application to the board of adjustment for relief, followed by an appeal to the court. Also, the same presumption of constitutional validity that attended an act of the legislature was equally applicable to municipal ordinances. The trailer park ordinance was a valid exercise of the police powers in regulating sewage, water supply, fire hazards, electrical hazards, roadways, minimum lot sizes, and similar matters. In addition, a municipality could properly refuse a building permit for a land use repugnant to a pending and later lawfully enacted zoning ordinance even though the application for the permit was made when the intended use conformed to existing regulations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, holding that appellee township could properly refuse a building permit for a trailer park and that the zoning ordinance was constitutional.
