Commonwealth v. Daniel


No. 150, January Term, 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


430 Pa. 642; 243 A.2d 400; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 754


January 5, 1968, Argued  
July 1, 1968, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, Oct. T., 1966, No. 600, affirming judgment of Court of Quarter Sessions of Philadelphia County, June T., 1966, No. 2280, and No. 187, Jan. T., 1968, from order of Court of Quarter Sessions of Philadelphia County, June T., 1961, Nos. 312 and 313, in cases of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Jane Daniel and Same v. Daisy Douglas.



DISPOSITION: Judgments of sentence reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant women sought review of an order and judgment from the Court of Quarter Sessions, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) which affirmed their indefinite sentences under the Muncy Act (Act), Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 61, § 551 et seq., and rejected their equal protection challenges to the Act under U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: As women convicted of offenses punishable by imprisonment for more than one year, appellants fell within the mandatory and exclusive procedure and sentencing provisions of the Muncy Act (Act), Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 61, § 551 et seq. Pursuant to the Act, the trial courts sentenced appellants to indeterminate terms, with the maximum terms being those prescribed by law for the offenses committed. Appellants argued that the Act discriminated on the basis of sex in violation of the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The intermediate court upheld the sentences in both cases. On consolidated review, the court reversed the judgments and remanded for resentencing. Under the Act, the court noted, a judge sentencing a woman had no discretion in fixing the maximum term of imprisonment. On the other hand, the court explained, a judge sentencing a man for the same crime could consider extenuating facts and factors pursuant to Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 19, § 1057. Finding no reasonable grounds for the imposition of longer or greater sentences for women than for men, the court held that the Act was arbitrary, discriminatory, and invalid under U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: Finding that the Muncy Act's sentencing scheme was an unconstitutional violation of equal protection under the Fourteenth Amendment, the court vacated appellants' sentences and remanded their cases for resentencing.

 

192 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Williams & Co. v. School Dist.


Nos. 38, 39, March Term, 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


430 Pa. 509; 244 A.2d 37; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 737


March 14, 1968, Argued  
July 1, 1968, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused August 5, 1968.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeals from judgments of County Court of Allegheny County, Nos. A725 and A726 of 1964, in case of Williams and Company, Inc. v. The City of Pittsburgh, School District of Pittsburgh, and David A. Smith, City and School Treasurer.



DISPOSITION: Judgments reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district challenged an order of the County Court, Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) entering judgment in appellee taxpayer's favor regarding appellant's imposition of a tax on all mercantile sales pursuant to the Act of June 25, 1947, 53 P.S. § 6851 et seq. Appellant argued that the trial court erred in finding that appellee's sales were brokerage transactions instead of retail sales.


OVERVIEW: Appellant school district imposed a tax on all mercantile sales by the authority of the Act of June 25, 1947, 53 P.S. § 6851 et seq. Eighty percent of appellee taxpayer's sales were filled by delivery from its own warehouse and the remaining twenty percent were filled by shipment from suppliers directly to appellee's customers. The trial court ruled in appellee's favor and found that those remaining sales were brokerage transactions rather than retail sales. The court reversed, holding that appellee was more than a negotiator between suppliers and its customers. It bought the goods involved on its own account and sold them to the customer on its own terms. Suppliers looked only to appellee for payment and appellee bore the risk of nonpayment by its customers. This indicated that appellee was a party to two separate transactions, one being a purchase from the supplier and one being a sale to its own customer. Thus, appellee was not one who merely negotiated a contract of sale between buyer and seller.


OUTCOME: The order entering judgment in appellee taxpayer's favor was reversed because the trial court erred in finding that the proper classification for mercantile tax purposes of certain sales were brokerage transactions when they were clearly retail sales.

 

193 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Hession Condemnation Case


No. 437, January Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


430 Pa. 273; 242 A.2d 432; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 702


January 16, 1968, Argued  
May 21, 1968, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused June 26, 1968. *


* Reporter's Note: On January 20, 1969, the Supreme Court of the United States denied a petition for a writ of certiorari.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas No. 2 of Philadelphia County, Dec. T., 1965, No. 1092, in re relocations 6900 State Road, property of Joseph John Hession and Sophie Hession.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commonwealth sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) which found that appellee property owners had sustained compensable damages as the result of appellant's vacation of a road and construction of an elevated highway. The trial court's order also ordered a jury trial to determine the amount of damages.


OVERVIEW: As part of its construction of an expressway, appellant commonwealth vacated a road adjacent to appellee property owners' tavern. Pursuant to the Eminent Domain Code (Code), Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 26, § 1-101 et seq. (Supp. 1967), appellees petitioned for the appointment of viewers to assess damages. The board of viewers made an award of damages reflecting the property's reduced value. In accordance with § 516 of the Code, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 26, § 1-516 (Supp. 1967), appellant sought review of the viewers' report. The trial court entered an order finding that appellees had sustained compensable damages and awarding a jury trial to determine the amount. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded with instructions to enter judgment for appellant. Appellees had no vested right in the traffic going past their premises, the court held, and damages resulting from the diversion of traffic to the elevated highway constituted loss without injury. Noting that appellees still had access to their property from three directions, the court found that they had failed to demonstrate any compensable damages under § 613 of the Code, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 26, § 1-613 (Supp. 1967).


OUTCOME: Finding that appellee property owners had failed to prove compensable damages in eminent domain proceedings, the court reversed the trial court's order and remanded with instructions to enter judgment for appellant commonwealth.

 

194 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Bessemer & L. E. R. Co. v. Pennsylvania Public Utility Com.


No. 32, May Term, 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


430 Pa. 339; 243 A.2d 358; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 709


January 4, 1968, Argued  
May 21, 1968, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused July 15, 1968. *


* Reporter's Note: On November 25, 1968, the Supreme Court of the United States denied a petition for a writ of certiorari.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from orders of Superior Court, March T., 1966, Nos. 133 to 144, inclusive, and April T., 1967, Nos. 108, 109, and 110, and from orders of Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission at Petition Docket No. 7 and Complaint Docket C. 18018 for appeal No. 142, in case of Bessemer and Lake Erie Railroad Company, The Pennsylvania Railroad Company, Lehigh Valley Railroad Company et al. v. Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission.



DISPOSITION: Orders of Superior Court and Public Utility Commission reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant railway company sought review of a judgment of a Pennsylvania Superior Court that affirmed an order of appellee Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission that required flagging in all areas where a train stopped under circumstances where the train could be overtaken by another train.


OVERVIEW: Appellee Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission issued a rule requiring flagging in all areas where a train stopped under circumstances where it could be overtaken by another train, subject to certain exceptions. The lower court affirmed appellee's order. On appeal, appellant railway company argued that by enacting 49 U.S.C.S. § 26, Congress had preempted state intervention in the area of flag protection. The court agreed, reasoning that the mechanical devices required by § 26 performed the exact same function that manual flagging was intended to perform and that the language of § 26 was broad enough to indicate that Congress had chosen to regulate the entire field of rear end collision prevention. In addition, the court noted that where federal language was broad enough to indicate preemption, a state law could not escape interdiction on the ground that it did not conflict with the federal regulations. The court therefore held that Congress had preempted the entire field and that the states were not even permitted to pass legislation that was supplemental to the federal rules. The court reversed the orders of appellee and of the court below.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders of appellee Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission and of the court below because it found that Congress had preempted the entire field of railroad rear end collision prevention and that appellee therefore could not promulgate rules that supplemented the federal regulations.

Firemen's Relief Asso. v. Minehart


No. 15, May Term, 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


430 Pa. 66; 241 A.2d 745; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 672


September 28, 1967, Argued  
May 13, 1968, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, No. 592 Commonwealth Docket 1965, in case of The Firemen's Relief Association of Washington, Pa. v. Thomas Z. Minehart, Treasurer, and Grace Sloan, Auditor General, of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant auditor general sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas, Dauphin County (Pennsylvania) which granted the application of appellee volunteer firemen's association for a writ of mandamus to compel appellant to make payments to appellee's pension fund pursuant to the Act of 1895, Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 2262.


OVERVIEW: Appellee volunteer firemen's association had been receiving state funds for its pension plan since 1931 pursuant to the Act of 1895 (Act), Pa. Stat. Ann. tit. 72, § 2262. In 1965, appellant auditor general concluded that certain by-laws of appellee rendered its pension plan invalid for purposes of receiving the funds. Appellee brought an action in mandamus to compel appellant to make the payments. The trial court granted the writ. Appellant sought review, maintaining that the Act did not authorize the payment of moneys to any pension plan which failed to establish a minimum age for receiving benefits or would pay medical and/or death benefits to association members. Finding no such limitations in the Act's language, the court held that mandamus properly issued. Because the rules laid down by appellant had no sound basis in the Act and therefore were not in furtherance of his auditing duties, the court concluded that appellant had stepped squarely upon the toes of the legislature in direct contravention of establish principles of administrative law and in flagrant disregard of Pennsylvania's judicial precedents.


OUTCOME: Finding that appellant auditor general exceeded his statutory authority in withholding payments to appellee volunteer firemen's association on the basis of appellee's by-laws, the court affirmed the order issuing the writ of mandamus.

H. A. Steen Industries, Inc. v. Cavanaugh


No. 441, January Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


430 Pa. 10; 241 A.2d 771; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 667


December 1, 1967, Argued  
April 24, 1968, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Refused May 31, 1968.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas No. 10 of Philadelphia County, Dec. T., 1965, No. 2769, in case of H. A. Steen Industries, Inc., formerly Poster Advertising Company, Inc., assignee of Herman A. Steen, et al. v. Gordon Cavanaugh, Commissioner of Licenses and Inspections.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant commissioner challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas No. 10 of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), requiring defendant to issue two sign permits in a mandamus action brought by plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff obtained approval of the Philadelphia Board of Licenses and Inspections Review (Board) to erect two billboards. On the advice of the city solicitor, defendant commissioner of Board refused to issue the sign permits on basis that the Board's decision was contrary to law. Plaintiff initiated action of mandamus. The trial court concluded that the regardless of its correctness, the city was bound by the decision of the Board, and ordered the defendant to issue the permits. On appeal, the court reversed the order of the trial court. The court held that the trial court erred in treating the Board's decision as binding regardless of its correctness, because the Board did not have the judicial power to independently make decisions on matters of law. Instead, the Board was required to apply the law as it was determined by the city solicitor. The court further ruled that plaintiff had failed sustain its burden of proof of showing that the city code provision violated due process because it regulated private property for aesthetic purposes.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court, which had required defendant commissioner to issue sign permits pursuant to a decision of the Philadelphia Board of Licenses and Inspections (Board). The court ruled that Board did not have the power to made decisions on matters of law, rather the Board was empowered to apply the law as it determined by the city solicitor.

Pennsylvania Public Utility Com. v. Stiely


No. 22, May Term, 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


429 Pa. 614; 241 A.2d 74; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 844


November 28, 1967, Argued  
April 24, 1968, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, March T., 1967, No. 5, reversing order of Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission, No. 18137, in case of Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission v. Wellington Stiely.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission, sought review of the decision of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which reversed the decision of appellant and dismissed appellant's cease and desist order that alleged the unauthorized operation of a motor vehicle as a common carrier or contract carrier.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission, challenged the order of the superior court that dismissed appellant's cease and desist order against appellee company for the alleged unauthorized operation of a motor vehicle as a common carrier or contract carrier. Appellant claimed that the exceptions under the Public Utility Law, 66 P.S. § 1102, were limited to transportation of materials used in the construction of public roads. The court affirmed the decision of the superior court. The court held that the language of the statute was plain and unambiguous that appellant's interpretation was too narrow. The court also held that appellant did not meet its heavy burden of proof that the statute was unconstitutional unless their interpretation of the statute was adopted. The exemption was properly limited to a certain class of vehicles, even though it was not limited to public roads.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the superior court that appellee company was not engaged in the unauthorized operation of a motor vehicle as a common carrier or contract carrier, as the language of the statute was plain and unambiguous and appellee was exempted from the statute.

 

198 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Bykowski


No. 136, March Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


429 Pa. 406; 240 A.2d 809; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 819


May 2, 1967, Argued  
April 16, 1968, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargued March 12, 1968.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of Superior Court, April T., 1965, No. 295, affirming judgments of Court of Quarter Sessions of Allegheny County, March T., 1963, No. 343, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Henry Katz, Gilbert Katz, Martin Katz et al.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of court of quarter sessions reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought reargument of their challenge to an order by the Superior Court (Pennsylvania) which affirmed their convictions by a jury for the offenses of operating a lottery and conspiracy to operate a lottery. Previously, allocatur was granted by the court affirming the lower courts' decisions, however in light of two decisions rendered by the United States Supreme Court. Appellants' petition for reargument was granted.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were convicted of operating a lottery and conspiracy to operate a lottery. The decisions rendered in the United States Supreme Court required the court to re-examine its previous decision affirming appellants' convictions. The court reversed appellants' convictions and ordered a new trial because the two decisions of the Supreme Court changed the previous decision. Federal authorities seized the evidence against appellants under 26 U.S.C.S. §§ 4411-4412. In one case, it was determined that failing to comply with either section was a violation of a privilege against self-incrimination. The court held that if a federal prosecution based upon violations of § 4411 and § 4412 infringed accused's privilege against self-incrimination and this privilege also applied to the states, then a state prosecution based upon the same evidence seized by federal officers would equally violate that privilege. The court further held that if the evidence violated the U.S. Const. amend. V when used in federal court then it equally violated the constitution when admitted in state court.


OUTCOME: The court's previous decision affirming appellants' convictions for operating a lottery and conspiracy to operate a lottery were reversed and a new trial granted because the court determined that the evidence used to convict appellants could not be constitutionally admitted in federal court and the identical constitutional violation would occur if the evidence was admitted in state court.

 

199 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Newman


No. 145, January Term, 1968


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


429 Pa. 441; 240 A.2d 795; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 825


January 10, 1968, Argued  
April 16, 1968, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, Oct. T., 1966, No. 411, affirming judgment of Court of Quarter Sessions of the Peace of Delaware County, Dec. T., 1964, Nos. 101 and 102, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Henderson Newman.



DISPOSITION: Order of Superior Court reversed; order of lower court vacated; and new trial granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania) that affirmed the trial court's judgment of sentence after the jury found defendant guilty of trafficking in lottery tickets.


OVERVIEW: Early in the morning, four police officers went to defendant's home with a body warrant for defendant and a search warrant for the premises. The officers banged on the door and announced in a loud voice that they were the police. When there was no response within 20 seconds, they broke in the door. Defendant was convicted of trafficking in lottery tickets. The court reversed his conviction and granted him a new trial because the search warrant's execution violated U.S. Const. amend. IV. Prior to entering a private home to conduct a search or to make an arrest, the officers had to give notice of their identity and purpose. The court held that held that the officers' forcible entry of defendant's home without announcing their purpose violated U.S. Const. amend. IV. The court also held that there were no exigent circumstances that justified the officers' entry without proper announcement. There was no evidence that defendant was fleeing or about to destroy evidence. The court did not address defendant's argument that the search warrant, which was based on a confidential informant's information, did not supply the underlying circumstances upon which the informant based his conclusions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower appellate court's order that affirmed the trial court's judgment, vacated the trial court's judgment of sentence for trafficking in lottery tickets, and granted defendant a new trial. The execution of a search warrant for defendant's home violated the Fourth Amendment because the police made a forcible entry without announcing their purpose. Moreover, there were no exigent circumstances justifying the forcible entry.

 

200 of 1321 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Harris


No. 330, January Term, 1967


Supreme Court of Pennsylvania


429 Pa. 215; 239 A.2d 290; 1968 Pa. LEXIS 792


November 14, 1967, Argued  
March 15, 1968, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Superior Court, Oct. T., 1965, Nos. 233 and 234, affirming judgments of Court of Oyer and Terminer of Philadelphia County, July T., 1964, Nos. 279 and 282, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Melvin Harris.



DISPOSITION: Order of Superior Court and judgments of lower court reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Superior Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed judgments convicting defendant of burglary, larceny, receiving stolen goods, and possession of burglary tools and denied his motion for a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of burglary, larceny, receiving stolen goods, and possession of burglary tools. Defendant filed a motion for a new trial that was denied. The appellate court affirmed, and defendant sought review. The court reversed, finding that defendant's convictions rested in part upon the introduction in evidence, over objection, of a set of burglary tools and a brief case seized without a warrant from an automobile in defendant's possession at the time of his arrest. The court found that, as the search was unreasonable, under the U.S. Const. amend. IV, because it was not confined to "the immediate vicinity of the arrest" and could not be sustained as incidental, the evidence should have been excluded. Defendant was taken into custody in the house and was promptly handcuffed, the automobile was locked, and the police had the keys; therefore, the search was not necessary to prevent harm to the arresting officers, defendant's escape, or the destruction of evidence. The court found that defendant's apparent approval of the search was not free of intimidation or duress, thus it did not render the search lawful.


OUTCOME: The order affirming judgments convicting defendant of burglary, larceny, receiving stolen goods, and possession of burglary tools and denying his motion for a new trial was reversed, and a new trial was ordered, because burglary tools and a brief case seized from an automobile in defendant's possession at the time of his arrest should have been excluded, in that the search was unconstitutional.
